The Red Page
MENTOR AND PUPILS.
He warned of steps retrieved in pain.
“We have strength, we have blood, we are
young,"
Youth sows the links, man wears the chain.
“Shall a siceet lyric cease to be sung?"
The song is short, the travail long.
“Shall the morning brood over her grave?"
Forge weapons now to meet the throng.
“ There’s a bird flying white o'er the wave."
The torrent of the blood control.
“ ’Tis a steed bounding whither we will."
In more than name discern the soul.
“There is i^ r ve like a light on the hill.”
That light of Love is fleeting fire.
“In the deep sea of Love let us dive."
The test of Love is in the lyre.
“Give us Love, and the lyre is alive."
The chords are snapped by passion’s touch.
“She is there, by the tall laurel-rose .”
You sway the staff —you grasp the crutch.
“She is beckoning: who shall oppose ?”
Behold a giant in his prime.
“On her breasts are the beams of the day."
A cripple he. surprised by Time!
“She has loosened her girdle: give way!"
George Meredith, in “Letters of
George Meredith.”
Hardy
Born in 1840, Thomas Hardy, the last of
the giants of the Victorian era, still sur-
vives. But his work is done, and timely is
his appearance in the fine series of modern
biographies, published by Martin Seeker.
Laseelles Abercrombie, with already a repu-
tation as a poet, here, in Thomas Hardy: A
Critical Study (per Dymock’s Library,
Sydney), show's himself an admirable
literary critic. He is not here concerned
with biography; it is not the man but his
achievement that forms the subject of a
masterly analysis. His main division of
Hardy’s novels into those of epic form and
those of dramatic form is one that com-
mends itself to the critical mind. And his
simile of a Gothic church, formed by Hardy’s
works, with the pillared nave composed of
Hardy’s six principal novels, The Dynasts
forming the quire, and supporting, but not
intimately concerned in the chief composition
of the building, like side chapels, the poems,
the short stories and two of the minor novels,
aptly pictures the completeness and whole-
ness of general design in the work of this
giant. But Hardy’s Gothic church, though
informed with a spirit of grave dignity, is
not ecclesiastic; it is a temple to Pagan
sensuousness and Pagan humor, and his
God is modern scepticism.
Abercrombie makes the high claim that in
the Wessex novels fiction has achieved both
a style and a substance that enable it to ful-
fil the gravest function of art —that, for
example, enable this new art to take an
equal place with any great piece of poetry
or sculpture or drama, and to fulfil, at any
rate in English, this function for the first
time. And, granting for his critical pur-
pose the thesis of Abercrombie, that perfec-
tion of form is the chief criterion of judg-
ment, that claim may be accepted. But the.
author admits that if other criteria of judg-
ment are taken, Hardy’s claim must be con-
tested. Yet undoubtedly Hardy has made
the novel adequate for the high purpose to
which man has so lately elevated it among
the arts.
Here is an admirable statement of Hardy’s
sense of tragedy :—■
The general, measureless process of ex-
istence cares nothing, in working itself out,
for the ne-eds and desires of individual
existence; the only relations between the
two (but it is an utterly unavoidable rela-
tion) is that in the long run the individual
must obey the general. The main stream
of tendency has an ultimate power over all
the vortices within it. It is a state of
affairs which is, of course, especially un-
fortunate for consciously sensitive human
creatures who have the additional ill-luck
to be firmly persuaded that their desires
must have some creative value, some power
of modifying the worldly process. ... Human
desire must therefore be at best an irony;
when completely wrought into artistic form
it must anoear as tragedy. .. .The main,
ruthless stream of tendency, which the char-
acters must in the end obey, exhibits itself
not only around but in the characters them-
selves ; only thus could they symbolise the
basic conception of Hardy’s tragedy. They
have in them some weakness, disability, in-
herited instinct, or perhaps some error in the
assertion of their strength, which inevitably
becomes the chance for the power of the
world finally to assert itself against them.
As Abercrombie elsewhere says, the tra-
gedy is that of “personality for ever moved
to assert itself against the implacable, im-
personal drift of things.”
Of Hardy’s humor- —he had no wit: —
The epithet that is commonly applied to
Hardy’s humor is the best for it; it is
Shakespearian. In richness, in energy, in
absurdity, in disconcertingness, it comes
nearer to Shakespeare’s than any other ; and
especially is it near Shakespeare’s in keep-
ing. after all such lists of qualities have
been made out, its essential quality unana-
lysed.
And of Hardy’s use of Nature: —
Nature, in Hardy’s fiction, is neither an
abstraction nor a scenic setting, but a vast,
impassive organism, living her own immense
life, multitudinous but obscurely unanimous ;
and the strict formality of the art could
have no use for this enormous being unless
it was able to bear some part in the fable,
whose event becomes, therefore, an affair
only partially human.
As to the alleged pessimism of Tess and
Jude the Obscure, Abercrombie acutely says
that great art cannot ever be itself pessimis-
tic, however much pessimism may go to its
making. The pessimism is merely the mat-
erial out of which a work of art is made —
presuming that the result is a work of art.
For the art itself is not to be produced
without a vital exaltation in him who pro-
duces it. And the artist’s exulting sense of
life, the mastery of his formative imagina-
tion over the shows of things, and even over
the significance he draws from them —This
is the first and fundamental thing we feel
in any real appreciation of art.... So it is
with these two great novels of Thomas
Hardy’s. We may call their mood pessimis-
tic, but they themselves are nothing so; for
their art is altogether too shapely, too as-
sured, too masterful.
Yet, he admits, there are instances where
the mood escapes from the art, as in the
final paragraph of Tess. And apart from
the character of the mood, these passages
offend—but simply because the mastery of
the artist has temporarily broken down.
But Abercrombie goes further; he is the sort
of critic who, once mounted on his own meta-
physic, can go any length. He refuses to
admit that the mood of these two epic novels
is pessimistic.
A genuine pessimism must surely go fur-
ther than the conception of existence as an
evil; it must add to this a sad acquiescence
in the evil —-since what good can come of
anything else than acquiescence, if all things
end in evil? But tbe mood which governs
Tess of the D’UPbcrviUes and Jude the Ob-
scure is plainly not an acquiescence. It is
a fierce, burning revolt against the evil it
conceives.
J*
An interesting comparison of these two
masterpieces show's us Abercrombie at his
critical best.
If we may regard Tess as an epic state-
ment of the conflict between personal and
impersonal in its simplest and most general
mood, Jude the Obscure may be said to put,
in a very similar form of art, a special case
of the conflict.... The mere “will to enjoy”
(Tess) —the fundamental motive of per-
sonal existence —becomes the more complex
and more dangerous “will to power” (Jude)
.... While Tess only suffers her tragedy,
Jude deliberately courts his. She is pun-
ished simply for the sin of personal exist-
ence ; but Jude, with the 'more rebellious
consciousness of masculine nature, adds to
this the further sin of aspiration—he being
thus typical of his sex, as Tess is of hers...
It cannot be said that Tess’s opposition to
the power of the world is less brave or less
vigorous than Jude’s; but it is static....
And witho”’: absurdly forcing sexual differ-
ence, it set-ms justifiable to take these two
novels as giving a typically feminine and a
tynieally masculine embodiment to the same
tragic conflic- of personal against imper-
sonal.
(And how surprised must Thomas Hardy
be at finding out what unconsciously he has
done! One of the rare delights of fame in
old age must lie in finding the critic-explana-
tion for the inexplicable urge of creative
work. Be sure that Hardy did not deliber-
ately say to himself: “Now that I have
treated of the woman in the fell clutch of
circumstance, I shall round the matter off
by showing the man in the same toils. Then
I shall do for the race what I have done for
the woman and the man ; I shall get to work
on The Dynasts.")
&
Hardy’s poetry is written in prose. The
pattern is always ingenious, the metre al-
ways strict and accurate, but the words
have no “potential.” They are words used
as a scientist, and not as a poet, would use
them, words that cqrry with them merely
their “kinetic” energy.
With the scientist, the value of words de-
pends on their place in a sentence, their
individual obedience to a general purport;
with the poet, this logical value of words is
compounded with an additional value, just
as considerable, which is absolutely their
own, quite apart from any sentence wherein
they may be of use. And this additional
value does not embarrass the other, but on
the contrary gives it surpassing potency; for
the thought of a poet is not something that
can be stated precisely in words, and there
an end. . It shades off all round from the
precise into the indefinite and incommuni-
cable, into what can only be suggested. He
tries, therefore, to give his thought not only
a logical statment in words, but also to in-
fect his reader’s mind with the vague aura
surrounding his thought Such organisa-
tion of language will vehemently empower
the logic beyond anything that can be ef-
fected by the mere instrumental use of lan-
guage; but, most conspicuously, it gives
words that challenging and exciting quality
which they only have when they are used
as things existing for themselves as well as
for logic.
This division of the energy of words into
“kinetic” and “potential,” into their logical
force and their exciting force, suggested
first by Arthur Ransome, is a useful contri-
bution to criticism, and in his cohsideration
of Hardy’s prose and poetry Abercrombie
makes valuable use of it. Roughly speak-
ing. O’Dowd uses words in the “kinetic”
manner, and Hugh McCrae and Roderic
Quinn in the “potential” manner.
Of The Dynasts, the crowning product of
Hardy’s philosophy, Abercrombie lightly re-
marks that the easiest way to read it is to
skip everything printed in italics- —that is,
to omit the whole of the surrounding super-
natural drama and concentrate on the human
play within a play.
Abercrombie’s analysis of this tremendous
drama is greatly illuminative. One judg-
ment must here suffice :—
It is the biggest, the most consistent and
deliberate exhibition of fatalism in litera-
ture. The enormous tormented spectacle of
the Napoleonic wars has been represented,
not as a complex of human purpose gov-
erned by a presiding fate, but as a system
where there is neither being nor doing which
is not fate itself. No purpose, great or
small, moves through the mass of human
event, except the single purpose which drives
existence onward. Nothing whatever has
been, or ever could have been, attained, but
the continued action of the Primal Will.
The huge process of human agony and tri-
umph lias no function in itself; it is only
the eternal Will uselessly uttering exist-
ence.
On Hardy’s death there will doubtless ap-
pear the usual Life; but there is nothing
more to be said about his works. Abercrom-
bie in this book has done that duty.
Creature Life in Australian Wilds,
Cyril Grant Lane (Brane, per Dymock’s
Library, Sydney), ought to be a much better
book than it is. The author writes interest-
ingly of photography in the Gippsland bush
and somewhat ramblingly of the wild life he
met; but many of his numerous photographs
bear the marks of having been taken not in
the bush, but in some museum, not always
acknowledged, and not from the living animal
but from conveniently stuffed specimens. ' •
in many of his actual bush photographs Cyril
Grant Lane is the only “creature life” that
is in view. And Cvril Grant Lane’s adven-
tures in the wilds of Gippsland hardly seem
to an Australian as hazardous as he evidently
thought them. But there are many excellent
genuine photographs of Australian wild life,
snapped in its proper environment.
In the interests of Australian writers this page sent
for the prospectus of Andrew Melrose’s fourth prize
novel competition; and though the receipt ®f the
conditions governing this competition is too late for
the entry of Australasian writers, the chief condi-
tions are summarised for the guidance of future com-
petitors, as the event promises to become annual.
Anyone can compete. MS. must reach Andrew Mel-
rose, 5 Henrietta-street, Covent Garden, London, W.C.,
before April 30, 1913, and, presumably on that day,
for future competitions, in succeeding years. Novels
must not have previously appeared in any form,
must be typewritten on sheets measuring 10 by 8
inches, and should contain not less than 90,000, and
not more than 150,000 words. No indication must
appear of the author’s name or address on the MS.,
which must bear a pseudonym. The MS. must be
sealed in a wrapper, on which the pseudonym must
be the only writing. Over the wrapper a second
wrapper must be placed, bearing the pseudonym and
the author’s name and address, and stamne for re-
turn. The whole must then be placed in a third
wrapper addressed to the publisher, and marked
“Competition.” The MS., bearing no evidence
of the identity of the authors, will be sifted by a
special staff of readers, and the best submitted to
Mary Cholmondeley, Joseph Conrad and IV. .1.
Locke, who will award a prize of 250 guineas to the
winner. Each competitor must sign an agreement,
which will be forwarded by the publisher on the
competitor’s written request. It is this stipulation
which bars Australian authors from this year’s com-
petition, but it should be worth while writing Mel-
rose re next year’s' prize.
The Man Macdonald, by Kate Helen
We$ton (Holden and Hardingham), shows
no advance on her earlier Australian novel,
The Partners. That was a tale that, on its
rather commonplace plane, achieved an
effect. The Man Macdonald is merely the
f.a.q. novel for feminine consumption. The
pretentious preface sets the key of the story.
This deals with two marriages between, white
men and Eurasian girls, the scene being set
in Ceylon. In both cases the brown wife
conveniently dies in order that the white
man may ultimately marry the white woman
who wants him. The story of the meeting
between Helen Winter —the writer might,
with discretion, have avoided the likeness of
her own and her heroine’s name —and the
big, strong hero is told in slovenly style and
sicklied o’er with the pale cast of sentimen-
tality ; bpt in the development of the hero’s
entanglement with the brown girl the author
drops her heroics and writes simply and con-
vincingly. And in the relations between
this man and his Eurasian wife there is a
series of interesting situations treated natur-
ally and sincerely. The plot is worked out
with considerable ingenuity: and the novel
will doubtless have, and should have, a wide
reading from not too discriminating feminine
readers. But the book raises no hopes that
Kate Helen Weston is some day to write a
really fine Australian novel. The defects of
her undoubted qualities are here too plainly
protruding.
An Australian Literary Shrine
The first answer to this Page’s reauest-
"HaJf a Dozen Assorted y.Jf
rwy Shrines, is business-like:—
1, 2, 3, 4 and 5 Freetrade-street,
Sydney,
February 20 iqis
bear Sir,—Our town ambassador, “Sir Octonm
duly filed your order for Australasian Literary Shrine*
assorted.
There was a demand some time ago for Federal s. -
vice-regal shrines, but this is the first inquiry we h™
had for joss goods, other than gold and silver cm*
shields, tea services and ladies’ private wear w
have tried the trade everywhere, but cannot learn
anyone stocking exactly what you require, nor have
wc anything in our Birmingham department which we
could substitute.
We, ourselves, personally quite agree with you a*
to the necessity for genuine literary shrines, but we
might point out that they are an acquired taste We
must admit we feared the time was not ripe; other
wise we should certainly have included some literary
shrines in the new season’s goods.
We have, however, procured for you one-twelfth
of A.L.S., which we have had manufactured locally
to our own design, and this (carefully packed in sec-
tions) will be delivered to you to-day. .
At first sight it looks like a chunk of ordinary
Australian landscape. So natural is its appearance
one might think the Bush had been literally scalped
somewhere. A closer examination of the flora and
fauna reveals the fact that it is very representative
of the Dominion and Commonwealth, and remarkably
true to life. Human bones here and there speak for
themselves, as also do the burnt matches, the worn
boot and the rusty billy-can. The sand which forms
the tortuous track through the Bush is a mixed lot
from the various States, in the correct literary pit-
portions. The footprints are the leading features tf
the shrine, and special attention has been paid t»
them. They are those of Australasia’s great literary
blokes on the sands of Time. The cost of the whtle,
as per invoice herewith, is slight in comparison witk
most shrines, and the expense of maintenance, or ad-
ditions, would be trifling.
Our suggestion is that a suitable site be obtained
for the shrine, say, in the middle of Brickfield Hill,
Sydney, where it would be impervious to climatic con-
ditions, being, so to speak, at home. Here it should
be surrounded by an assorted fence, typical of the
bush. It should be called “The Wallaby Track, or
the Pad of* Literary Genius.” A brass plate near
where the track has been blazed might announce its
name. Small wooden semaphores could be employed
to indicate the authors of the footprints. As the office
blacktracker will doubtless inform you, provision has
been made for, as we say, a nice range of samples.
These include Kendall, “Bellerive,” Gordon, Marcus
Clarke, Daley, “Price Waning,” “P.J.,” “Scotty the
Wrinkler,” the McCraes, the “Cans,” Bracken, Sieb»B-
haar, Dorrington, Rudd, Dyson, Souter, Quinn, Bed-
ford, Boldrewood, George Reid, Willis, de Ron gem oat
and many ethers too numerous to mention. We ven-
ture to say the idea is original.
Whereas the garret and the squalid lodging seem
to have been the homes of most British and Conti-
nental literary genius, the same cannot be said of Aus-
tralasia. Even if it could, literary premises, mere
canvas, hessian or galvanised iron, with tired fvra-
ture and much-travelled wardrobes, we are afraid
would not have the same attraction for the tourist as
(say) Deeming’s cottage, the hulk Success, or any
waxworks carrying “the only and original Ned
Kelly.” It seems to us that to be popular a shrine
must appeal to all classes (and the driver of every
passing vehicle, at least, would worship at ours),
whether it be the temple of Thespis, Bacchus, Allah
or Ink. Convenience of situation is also a big factor
in the success of a shrine.
In taking the liberty of sending you this specimen,
we trust it will meet with your approval, and that we
shall be favored with a continuance of vour esteemed
orders, which are, at all times, a source of great
pleasure and satisfaction to us to execute to the best
of our ability.
We are, dear sir,
Respectfully yours,
McTag, O’Fancy and Emanbel.

ONE OF HUNDREDS OF TESTIMONIALS: Talbragar, Dubbo, Jan. 6, 1913.
Dear Sirs, —It is now about four months since your Air (las Machine was installed in my
residence. Its efficiency has exceeded all expectations; for light, economy, cleanliness, as well as
for cooking purposes, it is the best that I know of. My 20-Light Machine provides sufficient
light for all purposes, in addition to supplying gas for the stove. To be able to do all cook-
ing by gas in a country home at a moderate cost is a convenience that must appeal to anyone
making a gas installation, especially when domestic help is at such a premium. Wishing you
all the success that your machine merits. —I am, yours faithfully, (Sgd.) F. C. WAKE.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No Gas.
The large and dim policeman’s feet
In even cadence smote the night,
An 4r like a pale Khost down the street
Went Cissy clothed in simple white,
Ihe air was full of whispers fine,
Although the gas refused to shine.
All black and warm the shadows lay,
The hot air shuddered half-afraid,
And laws that rule the garish day
At hide-and-seek with gladness played;
VV e saw the long procession pass
Of lightsome joys that need no gas.
Pale candles at the casements shone,
And by a match’s fitful flare
The damsel put her beauty on,
And fixed with pins her evening hair—
Those tresses wonderful to view
As clouds with moonlight falling through.
Somewhere, with words profane and loud,
Beside his dark, unmoving wheels.
In wrath the fierce mechanic bowed,
And made his last forlorn appeals
To gods and devils in the mass
To stir a dead machine with gas.
Somewhere, in offices remote,
In stern official conclave met,
Grave persons all the darkness smote
With words of scorning or regret,
Discussing in the solemn night
The loss of profitable light.
Somewhere a politician nursed
A head whose brain was gone on strike.
Each plan of action seemed accurs’d,
.And weighted with the crowd’s dislike;
For in the stern affair of gas
He felt an overburdened ass.
Somewhere, amid dim pubs astray,
The gas-man swore above his beer
That, lacking one more bob per day,
The strike might last for half a year;
For why should he with toil illume
A world where cash refused to bloom?
But warm and black the shadows lay,
And like a pale ghost down the street,
Young Cissy took her pleasant way
On heedless, dainty, tripping feet—
No need of flarine gas was mine
Who saw her dark eyes closely shine..
Half-seen, the large policeman strode
Across the landscape of the dark,
The tall, black houses faintly showed
At certain points a tiny spark
But how should I a gas-jet miss ,
When shadows held a warm, sweet kiss."
N.S.W.
CUBSE O' MOSES.
Spring in the Black Forest.
Because you love me tenderly
You sent a flower across the sea.
And lo! it brings me near to thee I
How deep the forest heart is here,
With trunks that tower and pines that rear
Their tufted heads against the sky,
To catch the spirit winds that fly,
And underfoot the tender spring
In gold mist sweetly blossoming.
And down the vale the white roads wind,
And we who follow, far behind,
Hear the call, insistent, low,
“Follow, follow, where I go, .
Through dreams, of day and truths of night
Into the purple after-light.”
And like a ribbon, silverly,
The Rhine flows down to meet the sea.
Through banks bestarred with April s light,
Where mayhap many a gallant knight
Made such a vow as thee and me
Of love and faith and loyalty.
And oh, your face is close to mine,
And in my eyes your sweet eyes shine,
And all the lonely, saddened hours
Are scattered, e’en as petalled flowers
Are softly blown aslant the grass.
The grey mists creep, the gold mists pass. ..
And this is all that’s left to me,
A flower you sent across the sea,
Because you love me tenderly.
VICTOBIA
G. J.
Restaurant Music.
Amid the clash of knives and forks
And thud of waiters’ feet.
The earnest feeder loudly talks
While sawing at his meat.
The crash and rattle of good plates,
Flung down in rage or haste,
The watchful feed-boss agitates;
He cries as if disgraced.
And shoots away as if on wheels,
To fume behind the screen,
Whence come the dismal yelps and squeals
Of kitchen-folk unseen.
Meanwhile the band plays tenderly
(To make up for the meat)
The melting airs of Arcady,
At once so nure and sweet.
But though good Cornet with his hat
His bugle’s blanngs strains,
And Fiddle soothes the inmost cat,
Their music still contains
A hint of sou)), for all its grace,
While on Piano’s notes,
From cool, clear trebie to thick bass,
The tell-tale grease-flake floats.
Boiled opera they scrape and puff,
To drown the kitchen dm .

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/’ is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these'
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for■ return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 13, 1913.
THE STRIKE AND THE NATION.
N. J. Sievebs, an Australian, who until
lately was drinking in Oxford culture at
New College, wandered down to Sydney
Trades Hall a few days ago and told the
P.L.L. and some sympathetic persons about
syndicalism. He described the new Euro-
pean movement, or disease, as a “policy of
strike, strike, strike until the wages system
was done away with.” As a summing up of
the ancient and evil thing which wears a
new and fancy name, the description could
hardly be bettered. Only a few years ago
the strike, as an industrial weapon, stood
condemned by its own failures and by all
the suffering it had brought upon strikers
and their families for an infinitesimal gain
or none. Labor leaders were determined to
find newer and more peaceful means of
securing to the workers the fruits of their
toil; and on all sides it was freely recognised
that the hope of the people lay in Parlia-
mentary legislation and the fair arbitration
of claims. Now a great wave of impatience
appears to have swept over the nations,
and the strike as an offensive and defensive
weapon has gained a stronger grip than ever
upon the imaginations of thousands of those
who lift the universal burden and do the
world’s perspiring. Doomed as most strikes
are to failure, faith in the things cannot be
crushed out, and the almost world-wide idea
seems to be that out of the multitude of
failures must come one grand success. Aus-
tralia has felt the outer wave of the change
of thought, and even here, where Labor
holds political power, the feeling grows
that the crude, early weapon wnich the
movement professed to throw away many
years ago may be stronger than all Parlia-
ments and more effective than any arbitra-
tion. In a word, Labor here as elsewhere ap-
pears to be split into two camps, and the
faction favoring threat, violence and a
general smashing-up of the more or less
settled order of industrialism is growing
and gaining in numerical strength.
Paradoxical as it is, Syndicalism owes its
existence largely to the movement for Inter-
national Socialism. International Social-
ists, in addition to faith in red flags and
similar emblematic ornaments, take the
ground that all workers the world over are
one in aim and spirit, and that mere national
and geographical boundaries must be broken
down in order that organised Labor may
come to its own. This view presupposes a
tremendous advance in universal education,
wipes out all notions of racial or tribal
animosity, and accepts the broad proposi-
tion that all men are wise enough and suffi-
ciently under self-control to live in perfect
peace and amity if only their fullest indus-
trial rights are conceded. Yet in absolute
contradiction of such a spirit as it alleges
to exist —such a spirit as alone might make
International Socialism possible—these
people adopt syndicalism and assert that all
this kindly brotherhood and love must stand
down for a while in order that their objec-
tive may be reached by constant strikes and
long-continued industrial bitterness. On the
face of things, it seems pretty evident that
the man who will put up a tierce fight for
the extra shilling a day, which he regards
as his due, will put up just as strenuous a
controversy over an extra two shillings a
day to which he may not be entitled, but
which he thinks by using the strongest
means in his power he may be able to get.
The fighting spirit, which is one of aggres-
sion all the time, is not yet bred out of the
race, and men will battle for an advantage
as readily as for a right. In fact, they will
battle for nothing at all sometimes and find
joy in the exchange of wallops.
Syndicalism takes us back to the time of
the first French Revolution. Then the
shout was for liberty, equality and brother-
hood, and through oceans of blood the first
Republic waded out, not into the promised
land of new order and beauty, but into a
large, open place where the people as a
whole, having liberty of thought, immedi-
ately made choice to live under a newer,
stronger and more glorious despotism.
Throughout all history the fact stands plain
that change and ’-'fform gained by violence
and by the wreckage of a social and eco-
nomic system can never endure beyond the
moment; the things which are really going
to count the day after to-morrow are those
which have been evolving since the century
before last. Unless the future is to belie
every lesson that men have been taught in
the past, the way ofit of the present evils
is not by means of constant industrial strife,
which will wreck nationalism, destroy even
the present attempt at order, and create an
anarchy whose prevailing features must be
hunger and suffering. The road proposed
by the syndicalists is an old, rough track,
often tried, but it leads only to the dogs or
the Devil or. something worse, and history
has written a large “No Thoroughfare”
across its wider end. Under modern condi-
tions the nation which is attacked at the
heart by. syndicalism must go down in the
struggle for existence with other nations.. A
community cannot earn its living by idle-
ness, and perpetual interruptions of national
work must in the end mean national
starvation. It is perfectly true, as N. Jc
Sievers declares, that it is unreasonable to 5
expect a man to be “patriotic about an
empire on which the sun never sets, when
he lives in an alley on which the sun never
rises,” but it is equally unreasonable that
the nation or the world should put back the
clock of time, and attempt to reproduce the
conditions which existed before the organ-
isation of the primal tribe, at the bidding
of those who only see human hopes and
human aims through the rank mists of the
sunless slum. Labor, in the present state
of the world, cannot afford to spread its
organisation beyond the bounds of nation-
alism. Even the dweller in a Whitechapel
court may be the envy of one whose home
is in a three-foot lane of Canton. Men in
Australia, earning eight or nine shillings a
day, have plenty of local sympathy in their
attempts to secure a better wage, but nine-
tenths of Asia and a big section of Europe
would be glad enough to rush their jobs at
half the present rates. In the nature of
things neither syndicalism nor any other
machine, process or devilment can produce a
universal result at exactlv the same point of
time; and it must follow as surely as
whisky follows thirst, or thirst whisky, that
the section of the world which achieves the
millennium first will have to fDht for its
reign of peace against pretty well every other
place that is a shade worse off. Leaving
out the age-long racial hatred altogether, the
law of the survival of the fittest in the
struggle for life is not sroing to cease because
a few thousands or millions of people some-
where have arrived at their industrial goal.
International Socialism may be a very
noble idea —though International Socialism
by way of syndicalism is about the most
blithersomely insane notion that has ever
entered a human mind —but International
Socialism, or any other sort of Socialism, for
that matter, can have no hope of ever com-
ing to birth except by way of nationalism.
Men are not built on a uniform pattern, nor
will the same sort of medicine cure all
diseases. In various parts of the globe men
are to be found in all stages of development.
Some races are still in the Stone Age; others
in the Bronze Age; many in a transition
enoch corresponding to the French or Eng-
lish Middle Ages. The best that can be hoped
for is a group advance; and the lines of
each advancing eroup are conveniently
marked by nationality. The person with the
large enthusiasm and the red flag fails to see
this: but the fact is patent enough to any
observer who hanpens to have a baby or two
about the premises and has a little resard
for the immediate future. Nationalism must
stand before industrialism. As the State is
more than the individual, so the welfare and
general progress of the State are of more
importance than an extra shilling a day to
a group of workers. Looked at fairly, the
nation is itself a group of workers with a
few idlers here and there who have discovered
a means of exploiting the;rest. The depre-
dations of the few can never be quite so dan-
gerous to the community as a whole, as the
idleness of the many. It rests with., the
nation to use its united intelligence in set-
ting crooked matters straight and getting
every man his just due ; but the nation which
knocks off work and lets the baby starve is
plainly just an insane nation and ought to
be put in a large lunatic asylum. Syndical-
ism proposes Ao starve all the babies in
order that when they grow up they may
have plenty to eat: but along the lines of
national development and intelligent reform
the babies can get something to eat all the
time, and a lot more for their own babies
in the time to cortie. Australia has her own
w T ay out of each and all of her troubles, and
that way is not by ceasing to do her work,
but by doing more work and attending more
intelligently to her own business. The
making of our laws is absolutely in the
hands of the people, if they will only use
their power. Industrial strife along the old
barbaric lines which has found a recrudes-
cence under the fancy name of syndicalism
only retards the nation in its work. While
the wheels stop and the furnaces are allowed
to cool for the sake of a shilling a day, the
half-crown a day which was on its road is
being delayed. . What, we need more than
anything else is an intelligent impatience
which will raise Cain over the things that
really matter, and whjle its agitation is
working towards success, will keep the
nation’s pot boiling by seeing to it that the
sum of national labor, which is the sum of
national wealth, does not decrease. The
disease of strike, which is really the disease
of loaf in a bado’orm, can kill the healthiest
nation deader” than all the guns of a pos-
sible invader.
PATENT MEDICINES.
Three letters from a corpulent bundle.
First, let a doctor have his say:—
“Backblocker’s” letter (B. 27/2/T3) contained
statements so wide that they should not go without
a reply.
The book of “Secret Remedies” which the Com-
monwealth Government has thought of sufficient
importance to issue as an official paper has been
subjected to the fire of criticism of a Royal Com-
mission, and has stood the test. The analyst has
had to put up with about the most severe cross-
examination of modern times, and the book stands.
In all cases where there is any element of doubt,
the chemist plainly says so. And the chairman of
the Commission finally says, “We may take it that
the analyses as printed in the book are as correct as
is humanly possible.”
“Backblocker’s” suggestion that the B.M.A. in
its opposition to “secret remedies” has some wicked
motive is quite on a par with the opposition which
the natives of a certain part of India recently-
showed to a resident doctor who found out the cause
of one of their filth diseases, and so prevented any
more cases coming his way. He eventually had to
shift or starve. Why? Simply because (and I speak
as a country medical man) a good deal of the
average doctor’s work comes directly from the habit
of self-drugging. It is a matter of common know-
ledge to the medical man that the ordinary citizen
has no more ideg of the anatomy of his own body or
any of its simplest needs than the domestic cat has
of syntax. At the present time I am attending (1)
a baby suffering from intestinal trouble, caused
by an affectionate mother administering a well-
advertised diarrhoea remedy; (2) a man, a highly
intellectual Government official, who is suffering from
poisoning, due io taking a “nervine tonic” ; (3) a
woman' who has uterine disease, due directly to the
action of a famous “female remedy.” Personally, I
think, with “Backblocker,” that the action of the
B.M.A. is most reprehensible. It is interfering with
both the liberty of the subject, and, what is much
more important, my living. I hope to get a good
many guineas from the father of the aforesaid baby,
the above-mentioned Government official, and the
husband of the woman. Right at the back of my
head I know that the maker of a well-advertised
“patent” is about the best friend I have, because he
eventually brings me work, and generally well-paid
work. But, all the same, as a more or less human
being, I think it is a shame he is allowed to do it.
The actual specimen taken by “Backblocker” is
unfortunate, because neither the B.M.A. chemist nor
the English Government ditto could unravel the mys-
tery of what else was in the well-known “line,”
neither could the man who made it, under the cross-
examination of the English Commission.
“Backblocker” says that it is a “useful remedy.”
So is lard, and lard is not so expensive. The parti-
cular “remedy” mentioned by the writer of‘the letter
is about the worst that could have been chosen by a
man calling himself by such a pen-name, presumably
living among the gum-trees. It is typical of a cer-
tain Australian attitude, which prefers eucalyptus oil
with an American label.
The drivel about chemists’ prices, and chemists’
apparent connection with the medical profession, is
pure nonsense. Almost any chemist will treat any
case “on his own,” as most doctors know very well;
and the B.M.A. has made it penal for any medical
man to have any pecuniary interest in any chemist
shop. As to chemists’ drugs differing as widely as
publican’s Her, possibly the writer has not yet heard
of the Pure Foods Act. This Act renders every
chemist liable to trouble if any drug does not come
up to a veryj’definite standard, as some chemists know
to their cost, even though dependent upon a whole-
sale firm.
No! “Patents” came before the B.M.A. There
were “patents” before there were legally-qualified
chemists or doctors, and the cause of their being is
not the rapacity of either, but the woeful ignorance
of the poor old public. Let “Backblocker”
try to find out in his spare time how many patents
are taken by either the doctors or the chemists when
they are ill. And these people are supposed to know.
If I get a pain in my back I go to the best man I
know for pains in this region, and pay him for his
advice, and do as he tells me. When I come to the
mental state of taking Somebody’s Backache Pills, I
shall get my wife to call in two other practitioners,
and discreetly put me away.
* * * *
Now for the chemist: —
“Backblocker’s” view of the Patent Medicine
case (B. 27/2/’l3) casts an undeserved slur on the
chemist. Instead' of pharmacists charging extortion-
ate prices, the complaint is that internal competition
has caused a lowering of rates, and that, whereas
almost every other trader has advanced his charges
during the past few years, dispensing prices are, if
anything, lower than they were 10 years ago. The
average pharmacist certainly does not earn more than
a bare competency It is rarely one hears of a chemist
being able to retire. Income tax statistics in v
toria, at any rate show that a large proportion of
chemists make under £2OO per annum. What win
internal competition and the inroads on the bus-
made by general stores, to say nothing of the ino'r" 683
ing scope of hospitals, free dispensaries and FrinnA
Societies, there is little chance of a pharmacist over*
charging. The trouble is that the doctors inch!
in a prescription a high-priced proprietary n renf , ra
tion. To dispense the prescription, the chemist
has to buy a whole bottle of this (costing from
3s. 6d. to 7s. 6d.). He uses (say) Jth of it i n the
prescription. The rest remains on his shelf as useles-
stock, since possibly this particular remedy i s only
a “fancy” of the doctor’s. Go to any good dispensing
chemist, and he will tell you that quite a number 0 f
the prescriptions handed in to him are for proprie
tary articles.
I agree with “Backblocker” that much of the
evidence put forward against patent medicines is
tainted. At the British Royal Commission inquiry
(now sitting in London) several witnesses have proved
that the analyses included in the B.M.A. publica-
tion, “More Secret Remedies,” are incorrect and
misleading. Besides, it is not a fair thing to name
the prime cost of an article, and nothing else. In
many industries the actual cost of the raw article
is the least charge on the finished product. Skill
labor, advertising, machinery and a host of other
charges soon send the cost up. In country districts
where often there are neither doctor nor chemist'
“patent” medicines are a veritable boon to the com-
munity. I am speaking now of the ordinary house-
hold remedy.
By all means close down on the “cancer fakir”
and the “consumption fiend,” and others of the
same class. Sift out the tares from the wheat, but
in doing so don’t spoil the whole crop.
* * * *
And, lastly, from the public, as repre-
sented by O. C. Beale :
Children’s teeth are often erupted in a half-decayed
state, and the deterioration goes on yearly from bad
to worse. Every chemist and most grocers sell
whilst every daily newspaper advertises, a most sale-
able drug which is a mercurial poison for soothing
infants. It does soothe them —for the time being.
Mothers administer to their babies, unknowingly,
subchloride of mercury and starch (the sole consti-
tuents) as a sort of regular evening meal. But the
subchloride, because of careless supervision—in spite
of “Backblocker’s” assurance—is often mixed with
(or becomes) bi-chloride (corrosive sublimate) —an
irritant poison. Yet every advertisement blares out
the words, and each packet is inscribed, “Contains
no Poison” ! The stuff is sold by tens of thousands
of grosses throughout the British Empire, Australia
especially, and Chambers of Commerce are always
there expressly to assist the traffic. I’m not myself
a backblocker, but a commercial man, and claim that
thousands of my class loathe and abhor, beyond all
means of expression, these respectable infamies of
quack druggery.
I hold a heap of evidence upon this wholesale
poisoning of children, and when amassing” it I ques-
tioned (amongst others) the editor of the British
Medical Journal upon the probable effects on the
unerupted teeth of infants of the continuous inges-
tion of calomel (subchloride of mercury). He
barked out indignantly: “I tell you it rots the teeth
in their heads! What more do you want?” The
editor of another, the oldest, medical paper—he
being also chief physician to a large London hospi-
tal for children—wrote to me fully on the same sub-
ject. But to poison for profit the nation’s chil-
dren by mercury, opium and even worse , is a part of
the glorious privileges of the British subject. Minis-
ter Tudor is up against the backbloekers of Com-
merce when he dares hand to Parliament authenti-
cated and authoritative exposures of gilded swindles.
Pity the suffragettes don’t heave a casual axe at
this form of criminality, for it involves more rob-
bery, damage and murder than is perpetrated by all
other criminals added together and multiplied manv
times! But the drug-scoundrels are respectable, and
so neither new glory nor kudos is to be won by ex-
posing them.
Your mention of cancer cures roused “Back-
blocker” to sympathy with the sellers of bogus
remedies, and to denunciation of the authorised prac-
titioners of medicine. Well, Charles Edward Ful-
ford, inventor of two of the most widely advertised
of the patent remedies, spent £300,000 (as he
claimed in court) to introduce his drugs into Eng-
land, made a large fortune, and died at Pennant
Hills, Sydney, on August 26, 1906, aged 36 years,
of cancer. He did not trust to his own or any other
quack drugs, but placed himself in the hands of a
qualified Sydney doctor, who, so to speak, lowered
him into his grave with the least possible suffering.
It was an early age, but cancer passed consumption
10 years ago as a successful reaper for Death, and is
bounding ahead, never again to be overtaken. Cancel
in our race occurs at ever lower ages, and in an
ever-augmenting ratio. Especially with women. Ant
our beautiful laws allow any rogue —with Chambers
of Commerce to protect him —to pack any trash,
harmless or poisonous, and to sell it as a sure ant
certain cure for cancer (or anything else) bv the
help of the great Australian and British press. At
the same time, such rascals outrage by circulars the
whole medical profession, which never ceases o
denounce the infamy of secret drugs. Yet these same
slandered doctors devote their best efforts, gratuitous y
in hospitals, to alleviate the hopeless agonies of the
cancer-quack’s victims, whose flesh drops from t eir
living bodies, as The Bulletin has described. - 0
wonder Minister Tudor kicks — God bless him!
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Philippine Trouble.
The avowed intention of the new President
of the United States and his party to aban-
don the Philippines may bring a new set o
foreign complications very close to
lia’s door. A certain element of muclaie
philanthropy has moved the U.S. Demo-
cratic party to dream dreams
the cultivation of a brown nation
spirit, and about an uplifted hilipino
race which will run an uplifted imipin
republic, and serve as a burning &n
a shining light to its not-yet-uphitea
brown neighbors. True the Filipino p
never shown any notable lifting capacity-
Before the Spaniards came, some few ce -
turies ago, he consisted of some thousan
of village despotisms or anarchies, and n
had apparently never been any better, an
had seldom been much worse. Spain intro'
duced a certain measure of order. It sup-
plied religion and education, it made wra <
built cities, and shoved the brown man som
distance along the path of progress. -t
Americans happened later on, broke tn
Spanish power, and started the new uphtting
process already mentioned. They found®
a Filipino Parliament, hut having no tait
whatever in its capacity the American com-
mission—mostly American but partly f ll '
pino—had a veto on its acts and procee d-
ings. And then, in case two American mem-
bers of the Commission might be suffering


simultaneously frona stomach-ache, and in
their absence the Filipino majority might
do tricks calculated to make the angels
weep, a final veto was lodged with the U.S.
Governor-General, or failing him his de-
puty, or even his deputy’s deputy. So the
U.S. Government, when the Republican
party ran it, had plainly little confidence
in its uplifted brown Legislature. And even
to this day that Legislature only represents
a scrap of the native population. The hard-
fighting Morros in the South are not repre-
sented because they are a fierce Mohamme-
dan race and might kill the Speaker at
sight. The Igarotos of the North are not
represented because they are practically
naked, and might heave rocks at the Clerk
of Parliament. So the situation of the
white inhabitants is one of some anxiety.
The new Yankee Government —that of the
benevolent Democratic party —threatens to
hand over the islands to the comic opera
Filipino Assembly. Unless the white popu-
lation stands by that body and fights like
the very devil to hold it up, the Morro will
arrive cheerfully from the South and cut the
Assembly’s throat. If the white population
keeps off the Morro and holds up the Fili-
pino Assembly, that inflated institution will
probably play tricks with the white man’s
goods and chattels which will make con-
fusion worse confounded, for the Filipino is
the kind of person who would simply burst
with vanity and insolence at the idea of
having a White man for his subject. And
meanwhile the shadow of Japanese annexa-
tion and Japanese rule will come nearer and
nearer. The programme of the white resi-
dents —American, Spanish, British, French,
Portuguese and sundries —is to declare a
composite white man’s republic as soon as
the U.S. authority withdraws, to create a
militia with all possible haste, to sack the
absurd Filipino Assembly, and to put the
archipelago up to auction. It isn’t to be
auctioned on a cash basis, but is to go to
the Power which promises the biggest garri-
son and the best defence, accompanied by
some reasonable semblance of liberty. And
if the Japanese get on the spot before the
sale is effected then another outpost of the
white man will be obliterated, and the
Asiatic trouble will be so much closer to
Australia.
The Man with the Hoe.
Tile strike as an instrument of public tor-
ture is tolerably effective; as a means of
securing industrial rights or advantages it
is about as useful as a huge club with iron
spikes against the modern rifle. But the
public voice which howls down the striker
for his folly and his callousness is not pro-
ducing exactly the sane noise which pro-
ceeds from knowledge and understanding.
If Australia possessed 1500 Judges of the
High Court, and they elected to hold up the
community as, for .instance, the Sydney gas
strikers did the other day, every word of
scorn, loathing and abuse that could be
hurled against them would be far short of
expressing the real measure of their crime
against decency, sanity and law. But
gasmen are not Judges of the High Court.
They are men engaged in very rough toil.
Week in week out, in a temperature which
scorches and blisters the body, and may well
scorch and blister the temper and mind, the
men who supply light and fuel ply the shovel
and the coke-rake, earning their pittance
with bitter toil and black sweat. Outside
the retort-house, where the air is clear and
the temperature normal, are the Parlia-
ments, Arbitration Courts, Wages Boards
and Political Leagues, while up and down
the sun-bright streets the motor-cars carry
the people who draw dividends from gas
shares. The man with the grimy perspira-
tion pouring into his eyes may be pardoned
if he fails to see clearly the trend of the
greater industrial movements and the enorm-
ous advantages of Arbitration. His mental
vision is quite clear enough, however, to see
the urgent need of the missus at home for
a trifle more cash at the end of the week;
and if he adopts the most direct as well as
the clumsiest and most irritating method ot
getting what he w r ants, the action is in an
conscience natural enough. He has little
time to think of strategy, and a direct
frontal attack on the public certainly does
bring his case into notice as nothing else
could. In a civilisation which demands
countless conveniences and comforts, some-
body must do a lot of rough and dirty work,
and doing rough and dirty work does not
tend to produce fine feelings and hig 1
sideration for others. That when such men
have a grievance their kinder and v ..
course would be to hang on to their joos.
and insist upon means to secure arbitration
of their claims, no one who knows anything
of the history of strikes can doubt; but it
they choose a rough method, let us at least
remember in palliation of their off.ence I that
it is in keeping with the rough life they are
forced to lead ; and nobody has a light to
throw any particularly jagged rocks at
them.

The strike of the Sydney gasmen for an
extra shilling a day produced, m tlie usual
places, that fatuousness without which no
war strike or other public unpleasantness
would be complete. The frog-braint 1

against’the pifbUc dice)
flames that sort of claptrap against the day,
and urges all right-thinking people to be
impressed, if not overcome, with tne loyalty
of its spirit. That it has nothing to do
with the important argument going on
round the corner is a mere matter of detail.
The loyalty of the economic cheap-Jacks and
jerrybuilders of the social edifice is mani-
fest, the Almighty Being has been specially
requested by the influential capitalists to
keep an eye on George V. at W indsor, and
the threatened fracture in the Empire is
secure in the strong, heroic grasp of place-
hunters. A few samples of the worst kind
of fatuity may be served up cold from the
meeting which was conducted the other
day at the Town Hall by Gaud Mayor
Cocks to enrol volunteers to man the for-
saken gas factories. H. Y. Braddon, presi-
dent of the Chamber of Commerce, can have
pride of place :
I say this, that if in a city such as Sydney is to-
day we allow a condition of affairs like this to
exist, then we are recreant to the traditions of the
race, and we don’t deserve to be called Britishers.
(Loud and prolonged cheering.)
Incidentally, in face of that flap-jack, it
may be presumed that the worst kind of
sweating of women and children which goes
on in Sydney under the degrading dividend-
gluttony of some of Braddon’s fellow-mer-
chants meets with his and his friends’ ap-
proval as Britishers, since they allow it to
continue. Next frog-boom came from
Hitchman, chairman of the Chamber of
Manufactures: —
“There must be light, and, by heavens, there will
be.” Pointing his finger to the disorderly section
he exclaimed: “Now, Mr. Interrupter, back to your
work.”
And Gaud Mayor Cocks himself. He re-
gretted that some scallywags at the meet-
ing had the audacity not to desire both sides
of the case to be heard. But when the
secretary of the Musicians’ Union desired
to move an amendment to the big-frog
motion, Cocks forgot all about that senti-
ment, and thundered forth that he and the
other patriots were going to work “for the
love of their country.” It was a crushing
retort, and the fatuous frogs boomed, the
Anvil Chorus or something. Nothing is to
be gained by that sort of puerility, and
much is to be lost. This dull-witted strike
of the gasmen in Sydney might have been
settled in 48 hours but for it. The strikers
had neither the sympathy of the Labor
Government nor the helpful support of the
Labor Council. Nevertheless, from the out-
set they had factors for much trouble be-
hind them; and to call tenders for that
trouble was just what the large -fatuous in-
competents of the community were doing.
The Yass Election.
The narrow margin by which the. Labor
candidate scraped through in Yass (N.S.W.)
seems to indicate that, little as the electors
think of McGowen and co., their opinion of
Wade is so awful that they dare not trust
him to take things over even after the ex-
periences of the last year or two. The
position is pretty hopeless. Beeby and his
third party are not taken seriously, yet all
indications point to such an even vote be-
tween the bigger contestants for office that it
is faintly possible that Beeby, with a follow-
ing of half a dozen, may hold the balance of
power after the coming general election.
On its wild borrowing policy alone. the
McGowen administration should be kicked
into the outer darkness; but the choice of
the electors is so curiously restricted that
the false Labor crowd, with all its State
Frights huddled upon its back, looks as re-
spectable as the poisonous parties which
oppose it. Beeby went out because the nar-
row provincialists with whom he was for-
merly associated were too broad for him;
Wade has a record that will take a lot* of
living down, and is such a helpless, floun-
dering person, and so prone to fall over his
own boots, that it is almost inconceivable
that the people would willingly return him
to power; and McGowen has a heavy
incapacity and Holman a thin capacity for
doing what doesn’t matter much, and leav-
ing the other things undone. The conse-
quences affect us more than a gas-strike.
In the most populous of Australian States,
where the majority of the people are found
to vote for the thing which looks most like
big Australianism at a Federal election,
there is not one local party or fragment of
a party that stands for the national ideals.
The one gleam in the murky situation is the
prospect that the dreadful choice will hasten
the time when stinging and venomous State
Parliaments will no longer be tolerated by
the Australian people. Meantime, the duty of
the Australian nationalist is plain. By every
means at his command he must extend the
cramped powers of the Big Australian Par-
liament; and he can strike the first prac-
tical blow to that end by whooping up “Yes”
votes at the forthcoming Referendum.
The Sinful Effects of Sabbatarianism.
Common-sense, in conjunction with certain
caterers who supply it with a long-felt want,
is appealing for the abolition of a Victorian
municipal by-law, whereby Sunday picture
shows may be prohibited at St. lvilda and
elsewhere. The excuse for enforcing the
by-law is “the annoyance caused by certain
shows to people attending churches.” It is
not pretended by the municipal autocrats
that a show situated far distant from the
nearest tabernacle can possibly disturb the
holy calm of any worshippers; but on the
ground that a rowdy audience might possibly
assemble next door to a church, the local
council puts its precious by-law into action.
The moral influence of the Sunday shows is
scarcely denied. The police everywhere give
them a testimonial for at the least negative
virtue. They serve to attract street idlers
and keep them out of mischief. They deliver
hot-blooded youth from temptation by offer-
ing an alternative to the ancient customs of
the beaches and parks. They thus make for
law’n’order and social decency, and undoubt-
edly they have saved many souls from that
very devil whom churchgoers are always
mentioning with horror in their prayers.
Within three months they -have done more
towards diminishing the Sabbath drink
traffic than all the temperance sermons have
done in 30 years. But because a few church-
goers in sundry places have suffered “annoy-
ance” from these salutary entertainments,
the local municipal councils would restore
the bad old order of things, even as the par-
sons in those States where the Totalisator
is legalised would re_store, if they could, the
pernicious system of bookmaker-betting
which the Tote has scotched. . Society at
large suffers all sorts of pernicious suppres-
sions and oppressions from the persons who
are always remembering the Sabbath Day
to keep it holy. They are really a small
minority; but their persistency in asking for
something to be put down, or shut up. gives
them a great advantage over the large un-
assertive body of citizens who usually take
everything as it comes, and grin and bear
it. Common-sense is a long-suffering animal
in most cases, but in this case, goaded by
men who have something to lose, it seems
likely that the gentle animal will stand up
and kick hard. Certainly, if it knows the
value of its own freedom, it will.
A Naval Policy for Maoriland.
“Kohutuknokawau” has a dream or a
scheme, or something :
Now that Maoriland has turned its attention
seriously to the consideration of naval matters, and
has had the pretty straight tip from J. Bulls lead-
ing naval authorities that it ought to be doing some-
thing better for its own sea-defence than finding the
interest on Dreadnoughts for use in other waters, it
is up to the people in charge of the Dominion s
affairs to throw a large eye round the local sea-
scape, and evolve a plan suited to the special con-
ditions of the Shaky Isles. A citizen army is
already in the making; but Maoriland has a better
chance than any other country on this planet ot
creating something of a novelty in the way of a
citizen navy. Owing to the peculiar configuration
of the Maoriland coastline and the numerous isolated
ports, sea connections are as vital to the national
life as the great continental railways are to Aus-
tralia. The main bulk of the coastal trade is in
the hands of a single large company, which, to its
credit, serves the country a great deal better than
a consideration of the big monopoly it holds might
lead us to expect. Not many bricks can be cast by
the Maorilander at his Union Steamship Co.; but it
is nevertheless quite evident that sooner or later
the whole matter of coastal shipping must be taken
directly into the hands of the State. Maoriland
cannot always afford to allow even the best sort of
Private Enterprise to control its mam highways ot
local trade; and even a person with the °Padue
mental vision of J. Ward saw this, and fo -
shadowed the taking over of the U.S.S Co. iock
stock and barrel, by the Government And it is just
here that Maoriland’s splendid opportunity to create
a citizen navy comes in. Let the Dominion build
ships of war by all means, or combine with the
Commonwealth to do so, but let it also t “ rn f
ships of peace into vessels sufficiently armed to form
a second line of defence, and train every sailor in
its coastal trade for naval service. By freeing the
men on the ships from their cargo-shifting duties
in port, and substituting for that work sea-drill and
gun practice, Maoriland might break a fresh record
by calling into being a large and well-trained flqet
actually capable of earning its own Irving.
That the ships of the coastal trade would not
be first- or even second-class fighting machines, may
be taken for granted, but there are few of the
vessels now owned bv the U.S.S.. Co which are not
in nrettv well all respects superior to many of the
gunboat J sent of recent years to the Australasian
station. That” such vessels could not engage with
success in a great sea-battle is plain enough, but it
is also tolerably plain that no nation could afford
to send much of its first-class ironmongery to the
Maoriland shores; and after the first smiting, when
the wounded Dreadnoughts crawl away f °r. repairs,
large things may still be done by a multitude of
weaker craft in the hands of well-trained and deter-
mined seamen. There are enormous possibilities m
the situation. Maoriland is able to produce as
smart a race of disciplined sea defenders as .any
nation has yet turned out, and there could be little
tr More 4 bTVn/at
the mihimum of cost and without in any way pre-
udictog Uie interests of trade or the regular pas-
.luuicnig creation of such a fleet
senger traffic. A » “ e Maoril and absolutely safe
against ifnv posLle tovasion, and do a heap towards
insuring the permanency of the white race at th
pud of the planet. Massey and Allen have their
g chance if they have only strength enough to rise
to a great occasion.

a tvottter Victorian manufacturer whom
Australia’s mongrel tariff has caught in the
midriff, so to speak, and who is
turning importer,, published the
stance last week. He has, or had, a boot
factory at North Melbourne. A fire visited
him in January, and when he had got the in-
surance money he sat down and considered
things. In the end he decided that if
effective Protection is presently introduced
he will recommence manufacturing; mean-
while he turns importer. There is far less
anxiety and worry in that line; there is no
need to keep yourself a a fait with the
latest improvements in manufacturing
methods; there is no need to lay out a
large capital on machinery, raw material,
etc. When you import somebody else does
all that. All you want is an office, a small
store, some credit and energy. Possessing
these requisites he will doubtless do as well
as he used to, if not better. But his 120 ex-
employees will do nothing of the sort. Pos-
sibly a dozen may find work as packers,
storemen, carters and drivers, though none
of them will be well paid; the others will
rest on the public offices’ doormats, and re-
quest the Government to find work for them.

The gas companies can make good any loss
due to higher cost of production resulting
from a Wages Board award ; but they can't
make good the shrinkage in revenue caused
by loss of customers. And they will lose
customers all right through the recent dis-
location. There was a time when they
could sit back and smile because the only
opposition was the kerosene lamp and the
guttering candle; but nowadays the City
Council’s electric light and power cables are
spreading, and at least one energetic private
corporation is pushing out. The secretary
of this latter concern summed up the situa-
tion nicely the other day. Said he: “We
sympathise very deeply with the gas co.,
but, of course, we have to do our duty to
the public by connecting up as many new
customers as possible to minimise the in-
convenience.” Many shops and hotels which
had stuck to gas for lighting because of the
expense of installing the electric power and
the general upset howled loudly for electric
connection the Monday after the strike took
place; and every wiring staff in Sydney has
been working overtime for the last week.
The Australian (Sydney) Gas Co. is a huge
concern, but even it will not feel any better
when it overhauls its books and finds that
hundreds of good customers have gone never
to return; and the North Shore Co. won’t
feel any the happier for the demand which
is bound to increase in volume for the in-
stallation of electric-light works on the far
side of the Harbor. The greater share of
the blame in this trouble wasn’t theirs; but
they didn’t do all they might have done to
calm the waters when they became troubled,
and for that they will pay a stiff penalty
yet.

“Te Ngakara” from Maoriland
The announcement is out that “Dahn” Fisher
(the alphabetical son of George Fisher), the young
Wellingtonian who has followed his father right
into the Cabinet room, is going over to Australia
presently to confer with Federal Minister Tudor on
Reciprocity. It is a big question for a young man
who hasn’t had half as much experience in commer-
cial life as he has had in tennis ; but, then, who else
is there to send over from the Massey Ministry now
James Allen is in London? Whatever else may be
done or left undone, there is one anomaly that Aus-
tralia and M.L. should fix up pretty soon, if not
sooner—that of the exchange of postal notes. It is
true the M.L. Government wi-ll not recognise Aus-
tralian coinage. For that there may. be the business-
like argument that M.L. may thereby get its own
mint. But what argument can hold in support of the
present most unneighborly and unsatisfactory lack of
facilities for and Maorilauders to remit
money through the post office? Take the position over
here in M.L. If I want to send a postal order for any-
thing under 20s. to England, it costs me only 3d.
For a similar order to Australia, I’ve got to hand Is.
over the counter. And to-day there comes to me a
postal note for 3s. taken out in Sydney, and payable
in any part of the Commonwealth of Australia, and
yet it cannot be cashed over here. Senator Pearce
and James Allen have already discussed Reciprocity
in Defence ; Tudor and our Fisher are presently to
Reciproconfer on Customs; and although Fraser
and R. 11. Rhodes have recently recipped on postal
matters I haven’t heard that the unneighborly dis-
ability referred to above is to be remedied.

A Melbourne citizen with the seraphic-
name of Jasper Long is a victim of bad;
luck, as a result of which he will presently
receive 30 lashes of the “cat,” in two doses
of 15 each ; also, he will spend the next four
years in gaol—all on account of the ever-
lasting old 30 pieces of silver. Jasper was
charged at last Melbourne Criminal Sessions
with assaulting David Hearn, who dwells
in a lane off Little Lonsdale-street, and rob-
bing him of the sum mentioned. Hearn
identified Jasper, who denied the charge on
oath; but Hearn’s mother swore to having
seen the accused hanging around the local-
ity ; so, there being no other witnesses, it
was about one and a half to one against
him The jury seems to have considered
these odds hardly strong enough for convic-
tion. yet too strong for acquittal; so, after
pondering the matter for six hours, it in-
formed his Honor that there was no hope
of an agreement. Just here Jasper’s bad
luck began to hit him. Madden, C.J.. who
was on the Bench, curtly told the 12 he
wasn’t taking any disagreement, that to his
mind the evidence was plain and conclusive,
and that if they didn’t speedily discover
unanimity, he would lock them up all night.
Thereupon they speedily found a verdict of
“guilty.” Most Judges would have accented
the jury’s first conclusion —or want of con-
clusion—and sent the accused back for an-
other trial; also, most Judges, there being
no previous convictions against Jasper.
would have sentenced him at once and had
done with it. But Mr. Madden had other
ideas. He desired the police to attend later
on and say what they knew of Jasper (he
didn’t ask that person to bring his friends
along and say what they could in his favor,
but that was a mere detail) ; and of course
the police gave Jasper a shady character..
Then C.J. Madden informed the hitherto>
unconvicted Jasper that he was a scoundreF
and a bad egg generally, and had undoubt-
edly committed perjury, as far too
persons were in the habit of doing, ana 1
banded him the sentence mentioned. Doubt-
less, Jasper is of little use and less orna-
ment, and a spell of genuine toil would do
hiin good; but he had very bad luck. In
particular, he had the misfortune to strike
(1) a jurv whose missus strongly objected
to being left alone all night, and (2) a
Judge who seemingly was quite prepared to
punish him, not merely for the offence with
which he was charged, but for others with
which he was not charged, and which he
had no opportunity to disprove. And not
merely for his own offences either; but ap-
parently for those also of other people.
The Awakening.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fact that the Warrenhtip Liberals have been
caught napping is not evidence of laxity in other
parts of Victoria, but it would be well for all Lib-
eral bodies to make a specially strenuous effort
between now and May 31 in order to avert possible
disaster. — Argus.
the task is always much the same,
A Sisyphean sort of toil —
To wake an interest in the game ;
The tough elector to embroil
In duties grim and struggles stout;
To cozen him by hook or crook
To put a sitnple Fisher out,
And reinstate a subtle Cook.
The papers shout with voices shrill,
The agitators beat the drum,
And over verdant dale and hill
The strident old campaigners come.
Thev veil in Voter’s blasted ears,
Thev ring the bells, and brag and bawl,
To find at length, when night appears,
He hasn’t been awake at all.
That’s how it looks to Tory scribes.
But sad their error is in truth.
Tt is not wise to waken tribes
That show the Fat Man little ruth.
’Twere best no kind of din to make,
But leave them Sleep’s untroubled
For Liberals who’re wide awake
Won’t vote for Lib’ralism now.
VICTORIA.

WHERE
EVERYBODY

“Old Soldier” : H. Charles Wood, writing
in the Fortnightly about “The Armies of
the Balkan League,” says of what has proved
the most efficient of them —that of Bul-
garia :
Not a single foreign military officer has been em-
ployed in Bulgaria since the year 1885. In that
year, and at a moment when the country was in a
most critical position, the Tzar withdrew all the
Russian officers, who, until then, held most of the
higher commands in the Bulgarian army. As a re-
sult of this, Prince Alexander was compelled to
undertake the successful war against Servia without
the assistance of any foreign military advisers.
Since then, while a few staff-officers have annually
been sent abroad for higher military instruction, the
very large majority of Bulgarian officers has been
•entirely home trained.
There can be no question that the Bulgarian
army during the war with Turkey has shown
itself vastly superior both to its allies and
opponents, and according to this writer it is
singular in never, for nearly 30 years, having
had the advantage (or handicap, which is
it?) of imported foreign advisers. If one
may judge by the facts of Bulgarian his-
tory, the foreign advisers may be a very seri-
ous handicap, since the Tzar had no sooner
withdrawn the Russian officers from the Bul-
garian army, thus depriving it of all its gen-
erals and staff officers (those, that is, who
could least easily be replaced) when Ser-
via fell with a wallop on the presumably
crippled kingdom. Yet the Bulgarian army
was victorious in every engagement, as if to
prove that the imported foreign commander
or staff officer, whatever merits he may have,
is not specially likely to help the army he is
attached to to win victories that it could not
win without him. The foreign officer, even
if he can talk the language of the country,
is necessarily ignorant of the customs and
mode of life of most of its people, especially
those of the peasants who fill the ranks.
Even when nominally in command, he must
depend on the native regimental officers and
sergeants to see his orders carried out. The
foreign adviser of an army is only a figure-
head. When he leaves he is not missed, and
his place is quite easily filled, or quite easily
left empty.
The N.S.W. State Labor party has got out
the mop and broom at last. Carmichael,
Griffith, Trefle, George Black, McGarry and
Lynch stand under the condemnation of the
party’s committee of inquiry on the ground
that they assisted (or, at any rate, didn’t
help to stoush) Beeby at the Blayney elec-
tion, and that they are unsound on the ques-
tion of leasehold tenure. The counts don’t
seem like skyscraping crimes, but there are
alleged to be a heap of other things that
could be laid to the charge of some at least
of the six. But as the still immensely influ-
ential J. C. Watson favors letting the
accused off with a caution or the rising
of the court or something, in order not
to weaken Labor’s chance at the next elec-
tion, nothing sudden js likely to happen ; and
anyway, to punish this six on these charges
would be straining at the gnats and swal-
lowing a whole carnivorous zoo.
An incident that happened at Fremantle
(W.A.), the other day, adds to the sus-
picion with which this paper views the Com-
monwealth figures relating to the allegedly-
dwindling Chow population of Australia.
When the Singapore boat Charon arrived, it
was boarded, as usual, by Customs officials,
who searched from keelson to binnacle for
opium and illicit Chow, and could find not a
trace of either. But it happened in this
particular case that a Customs man had got
a hint of something; so, as a long shot, on
the third night (after watching the ship
meantime) a disguised official took a boat
and made fast to the vessel on the riverside.
The net was well cast. After a couple of
hours one Ah Sin was carefully lowered
through a porthole to him, and Ah Sin was
followed immediately afterwards by six
others. When the boatman was satisfied
that all his likely passengers were aboard,
he rowed them ashore, and they are being
very well taken care of now.
Police Magistrate Tanner, who was ap-
pointed some time back as a Commissioner
to inquire into some astounding allegations
made in Vic.’s Assembly against the Queen’s
Memorial Infectious Diseases Hospital, Fair-
field, has satisfied himself concerning some
of the charges—these amongst them :
(X) The body of a patient had been allowed to
remain in the mortuary eight days after death, what
time relatives were being informed that he was
“still improving.”
(2) The relatives of another patient were told
that he was “just the same.” In a manner of
speaking he was, because he had been dead for some
time.
(3) Children with clean heads on entering the
hospital or kennel were discharged vermin-infested.
(4) Children had been allowed to run about the
grounds inperfectly clad and barefooted.
(5) One girl .child, aged 5$ years, was placed in
the same bed as a half-caste Chinese, aged four.
(6) Employees’ clothing had been sent out for
washing without first being fumigated ; and male
employees had repeatedly left the hospital or sty
without disinfecting the clothing they had worn
while handling infected matter.
(7) The incinerator attendant had meals, milked
cows, drove the ambulance and carried patients
without changing his clothes.
(8) Cows whose milk was used in the hospital or
stable were permitted to eat hospital refuse.
(9) Patients were frequently left from four tb
five hours in the ambulance, to the danger of their
lives.
All of which goes to prove how well.hospitals
may be managed when horrid Socialism is
not allowed to meddle.

The playgoer who desires to meet “The
bunshine Girl” will find her at Sydney
Royal, the lady having moved up from Her
Majesty’s in order to make room for “Puss
in Boots,” the panto, that has been lingering
prosperously in Melbourne. “Puss” will
jump off the lighted mark on Saturday even-
ing, and commence her long run. Nothing
can go so fast or so far as a well-trained
theatrical cat.
Walter Bentley will still continue to elo-
cute on occasion, but the curtain is down on
his last big production. Beginning in a gas
strike which kept the people at home looking
for. candles. and ending in a howling tempest
which shook the solid freestone wherein Syd-
ney’s roots are fixed, “Hamlet” at the The-
atre Royal had as much reason to complain
of his bad luck as when Denmark played it
low down upon him in the flesh. Still, there
was plenty of enthusiasm when the veteran
actor died his last spectacular death and
had a glorious resurrection to receive an
illuminated address. The audience out-
howled the wind in its applause, and then
waded home through the torrents with a glow
of sympathy and admiration warming its not
too numerous heart.
