The public demonstration by Mrs. Nigel
Armine of her sugarrof-lead cure for super-
fluous husbands, made at Sydney Criterion,
may be witnessed for only a few more
nights. As far as can be seen from the
appearance of Mr. Armine, the treatment is
much more gentle in its action than lysol.
Julius Knight and his excellently-cast com-
pany white and brown, make the most of
this melodrama of poison.
“UNDER TWO FLAGS,” AT SYDNEY
ADELPHI.
(1) The horse refuses to go to the hero's rescue.
(2) The horse declines to be solemn while the
heroine passes away.
(3) The noble animal allows hunger to over-
come dramatic effect and devours a floral
offering.
“Under Two Flags” suffered a revival at
the Adelphi on Saturday night, after being
out of action for two years. Miss Ethel
Buckley is in her old place as Cigarette, the
vivandiere and pet of the French regiment
at Algiers ; and Miss Nellie Fergusson is, as
of yore, the gentle Princess Venetia. Other
than these the cast is new. George Cross is
Bertie Cecil Royallieu, afterwards Louis
Victor, the hopelessly impossible hero, who,
in the first act, smites two policemen and a
money lender, and escapes to Algiers,
where he serves as a soldier under
the French flag. In that capacity he
wears a pair of stripes and an army
coat that is too small for him, wanders round
the stage-fort in a casual way, looks home-
sick when the rest of the men have gone,
and carries on generally in a manner that
would earn him a court-martial anywhere
else. Cigarette’s ride through 20ft. of stage-
scenes and her subsequent self-sacrifice to
save the hero are, of course, the features of
the whole business, which, with the pos-
sible exception of “The Monk and the
Woman,” is the best thing the Adelphi has
turned out to date. T. W. Lloyd makes the
most l of a thankless job as Col. Chateauroy,
the Black Hawk ; and Gerald Kay Souper,
as Lord Rockingham, gets a bouquet for the
way he pleaded for the life of the hero.
“Under Two Flags” will show during this
and next week only.
** * *
“The Prince and the Beggar Maid” will
evacuate the Palace to-morrow night, and
General William Anderson’s flag will be
hauled down for the season. The following
night the theatre will be in possession of
the recently-recruited Hamilton-Plimmer
unit, which is officered by such tried stage
veterans as Mrs. Brough, Beatrice Day,
George Titheradge, and Harry Roberts.
“Dr. Wake’s Patient” is is on the general
orders for the occasion.
* * * *
The Tivoli’s newest arrival, Henri
French, billed as the “Intoxicated Gen-
ius,” combines nonsense, juggling and
trick cycling; and though he uncorks
some beer that is old and flat, there
is a sparkle in the rest of it. His best item
is a skating exhibition with bike wheels,
whereon he waltzes, describes figure eights
and does some daring speed business.
The Kremka Bros., who provide expert
tumbling and more nonsense, and wind up
with several spring-board items, are a pair
of clean and clever acrobats: Vaude and
Verne make another of their periodical visits,
and unload a bundle of venerable gags.
Others on the programme include the Lanca-
shire Opera Co. (last nights), Mr. and Mrs.
de Mario, Kitchen and Foy, and Welch and
Carbasse. Will Whitburn is promised for
next week.

As The Bulletin goes to press, the Reli-
gious Editor is speeding towards the National
Amphitheatre (Sydney) to feast his eyes on
Van Camp’s educated pig, advertised as “the
only one in the world.” During the Reli-
gious Editor’s misspent life, he has collided
with many ignorant hogs disguised as mer-
chants, debt collectors, policemen and so
forth. Every one of them pretended to be
more or less educated ; but the pretence was
always too obvious. The timely arrival of
Van Camp’s cultivated animal affords him
a unique opportunity of comparing those
posturing razorbacks of the past with the
real Oxford article. The attractive Amphi.
bill also includes the Seven Heraldos and the
Lessos.

_ The Little Theatre, Castlereagh-street,
Sydney, has recently been reconstructed and
refurbished, and has made a fresh start in
the life of pleasure. On Easter Saturday
Hugh Buckler and Miss Violet Paget, with a
company that reads well, will present “The
Man on the Box,” a dramatisation of Harold
McGrath’s book. People who wish to attend
the first night in comfort will find the plan
at Paling’s.

Branscombe has a company of Dandies
comfortably housed by the sad sea that waves
at Manly. The situation is ideal; and both
co. and show are up to the Branscombe stan-
dard —which is to say, there is nothing else
so good in the way of open-air entertain-
ment in Sydney, except when Thomas
’Enley does a turn in Government ’Ouse
grounds. Branscombe’s Dandies are mostly
nine, whether they frivol at St. Ivilda or
take Adelaide firmly by the wool; and these
of Manly don’t worry the rule by departing
from it. Miss Linda Bradford, the con-
tralto, finds most favor in - the eye of the
gods; Walter Whyte, the tenor, appeals to
the goddesses; and Roland Henry, who sup-
plies nonsense at the piano and any other
old place, catches the sprat of the'infants
who will be gods and goddesses by and bye.
Leo Chateau stands at the receipt of custom
and says that the world goes very well
nowadays.

“THE MONK AND THE WOMAN ”
MELBOURNE PRINCESS’S.
Enter —the Woman.
“The Resurrection,’’ a flickerised version
of Tolstoi’s work, has been Seeping the
Crystal Palace ticket-office busy during the
week. It takes four reels to tell the yarn of
the Siberian and other sufferings of the lady
on account of the man ; but, as Nella Webb
used to sing, “it all ends ri-hight.” The
best of the other J.D.W. offerings was “The
Smouldering Spark” at the Empress, a sea
story wherein an apparently harmless sailor,
after putting up with a lot from the crew,
asserts himself, and in the end marries the
skipper’s daughter, having meantime quelled
several mutinies, fought five of the crew at
one time, and quenched a fire on board.
(Loud applause.)
* * # *
For the 23rd consecutive year Wirth
Bros, will bring the “greatest show on earth”
to Sydney for the Eastertide. There will be
a new crop of actors just landed from
America, and a few cargoes of performing
wild animals from the uttermost ends of the
planet. Among the marrow-freezing novel-
ties is Madam Garcia, who drives an auto-
mobile down a steep chute and then up into
mid air, where the car with the lady in it
gracefully turns no less than three somer-
saults, and comes out undamaged—with luck.
There are cowboys of America and rough-
riders of Australia, beautiful divinities who
walk slack-wires, and other features too num-
erous to mention —all to be seen at Belmore
Markets on Saturday next.
Everything save death happens to every-
body during the visit of “A Thief in the
Night,” revived at Melbourne King’s for the
little while before Easter. Death is reserved
for Michael Pagani, his lady accomplice,
and the foolish husband of the last-named
person, whom she and Michael murder, more
or less, owing to complications arising from
the burgling of somebody’s diamonds. The
remaining characters live to curtain-fall, to
get whatever happiness is going at the time.
Michael and his doomed companions are
members of a Dago Red Hand Society in the
first place, but, later on, the gory organisa-
tion seems to peter out, so the plotters drift
apart in a mysterious way, whilst Joan, the
heroine, is doing seven years for stealing
those diamonds. Ere she was removed to
gaol she and her wrongly-suspecting husband
oersuaded a parson to christen their rag
baby in the name of the Trinity, and when
she returns from prison, in picturesque even-
ing dress, she goes to her long-lost home and
steals her offspring—who has emerged from
the rag stage into a precocious female
cheeild. The persistent Pagani thereupon
captures the fond mother, and imprisons her
in a garret until the populace bombard the
building, and everything is forgiven. Mean-
time, the misguided husband of Joan has
become a Cabinet Minister ; also, the comic-
relief characters have grown out of compre-
hension, and the poor wandering plot fre-
quently hides its diminished head whilst the
cheeild is folding her little hands in prayer.
Rege Robins painted some highly commend-
able scenery for this respectable perform-
ance of a curiously foolish melodrama. The
acting interests habitual patrons of the
King’s because the oast is entirely changed
from what it was three years ago. Roy Red-
grave, the Pagani of the original outrage, is
now the fatuous husband of the virtuous
child-stealer ; and Herbert Leigh is the Dago
who works his way through the whole menu
of crime. For the other parts there are the
ladies Remee, Florence Richter,_ Helen
Fergus and Lilian Wiseman, assisted by
Charles Taylor, Walter Dalgleish. Cannam,
Lester Carey and others. Better bellodjamas
have been worse played.

The Asche season is passing out in a blaze
of revivals at the Royal. To-night (Thurs-
day) sees the last of three reappearances of
Asche as the red-headed Jew of Venice, and
Miss Brayton as the Portia who broke’ him
up on a legal point. To-morrow and Satur-
day they will repeat, in “Othello,” the Bard’s
immortal warning against the mixing of the
colors in unholy matrimony; and two per-
formances of “The Shrew,” on Monday and
Tuesday, close the chapter.
“ THE MONK AND
THE WOMAN,”
MELBOURNE
PRINCESS’S.
VIVIAN
EDWARDS AS
PAUL, THE
HEROIC ONE.
The girl in the monastery continues to be
good for business at the Princess’s. The last
time this chronicler looked in to see “The
Monk and the Woman” he found the King
threatening to let Inman’s silent head fall
from the headsman’s block if the missing
female was not promptly restored to the
royal presence, and it set him thinking of
how Henry of Navarre had deteriorated since
the days of “Ma Mie Rosette.” The Henry
whom Wallace Brownlow introduced to Aus-
tralia was a fine, attitudinising profligate
with a silvery baritone and a blonde beard,
whereas his Majesty in the drama is a cleau-
shaved and comparatively unimportant
johnnie who couldn’t sing for nuts.
** $ *
A bit of quick-change swank endeavors to
conceal the moderate musicianship of Staley
and Birbecks, who turned up at the Opera
House on Saturday; while the polished
agility of the Maxini Bros., who also
arrived, served to throw limelight on their
fox terrier, Bobby. Robert is verv clever
indeed. For the rest, Bryant and Co. per-
sist in their poor sketch, showing what
"Katy Did” ; while Claire Romaine is in the
last nights of a triumph won by bawling am-
biguities to indifferent music.

Melbourne Bijou is to get another chance,
and a good one at that, of recovering its
long-lost hold on the attention of local the-
atre-goers. _ The Oriental Amusement Co.,
of U.S.A., is producing next Saturday (15th)
an American Musical Burlesque crowd in an
irresponsibility entitled “The Grafters.” For
a ragtime songandance show of this sort, the
Bijou offers a congenial home, and there
never has been any solid reason why a live
management should not boost the place back
into notoriety. The management of “The
Grafters” appears to possess the necessary
liveness.
* *
Spencer’s list of offerings at Melbourne
Olympia last week included “The Long
Strike,” about half a mile of murder and
upheaval, interrupted by wireless telegrams
and blissful moments. The wireless memos
were the feature of the film, for had they
failed to apprehend an important witness,
the hero would have received an electrical
shock that he didn’t deserve, and never
would have recovered from. “The Model of
St. John” was a strictly pious contribution,
the picture filming the contrast between the
beauty of temperance and the ugliness of
blear-eyes. “Love Among the Geysers”
showed intermittent cuddling among the
waterspouts of the Yellowstone ; while “Ire-
land and The Oppressed” exhibited some
more of the woes of the most distressful
country that ever yet the showman turned
into good money.
** ❖ *
Melbourne "Majestic has been entertaining
its public on things ranging from “A Forest
Fire” to the dread “Mystery of the Seven
o’Clock Express.” The former had a fair
amount of pictorial interest, but the latter
got complication of the plot in its middle
region and finally fell to pieces. “Captain
Barnacle’s Reformer” retailed an average
yarn of a “Monty Peet” me'rchant and his
giddy son ; and “The Unusual Honeymoon”
told of a bridegroom who boarded a balloon
and inopportunely got his head in the clouds.
As regards the comics, like “Dupin in Search
of Quiet,” they were obviously designed by
curates for retailing to good young men.
“The Native Born.” the newest thing by
the “Albert Edmund” collaborators, has had
its first mainland trial at Adelaide Royal. It
is, in this critic’s judgment. All Right._ and
very nearly Bosht a r. Melodrama, certainly ;
but less on English than American lines.
That is, there is not a wedding in it. nor a
bedroom. The villain is not haughty, the
she-villain is his sister, and there is no sug-
gestion of anyone having been, or wanting
*? e ’ unmoral," from start to finish. On

in the support, notably by Alfred Harford
as an Irish policeman, funny without being
idiotic. The dialogue varies from fair to
brilliant.

The inevitable Sydney ferryboat catas-
trophe just missed ip the storm on Saturday
night. Some time is always wasted in Mos-
man Bay owing to the incoming and outgoing
Mosman boats being due at Musgrave wharf
at approximately the satne minute. The in-
coming boat has usually to pull up in the
middle of the bay till the other boat has
cleared Musgrave wharf, though sometimes
it manages to get there first. On Saturday
night the Iviandra was, as usual, loitering in
the bay for the Ivirrule to get clear of Mus-
grave, when a squall forced it towards the
other boat. Each signalled that it was going
to port; then each signalled that it had
changed its mind ; then one signalled it had
qhanged its mind again, and the other was
too busy attempting to prevent a colli-
sion to whistle any more remarks. Residents
of the harbor-front had then a one
ferryboat ramming another. The Iviandra
smashed bow-first into the Kirrule’s side. The
Ivirrule, which was attempting to slip be-
tween the Iviandra and Cremorne, had
enough way on to come within a few feet of
the rocks. Had it been driven those few feet
farther there would have been some corpses
to collect. But it backed in time, and after
manoeuvring, broadside on to the waves, man-
aged to slip past the Kiandra and proceed
with smashed side to the Quay. The Ivirrule
succeeded in getting into Mosman Bay whari
with a damaged rudder, and decided to stay
there for the night. So Svdney must wait a
little longer for its ferry catastrophe. Didn t
someone once say something about a tube or
a bridge or something?
AT POVERTY POINT
Melba’s son is causing mother some unhappy
moments. Six or so ago he was operated on
for appendicitis, and, when convalescent, was taken
for a trip to Paris. There he contracted pneumonia,
and at latest was seriously ill. George has had a
strenuous life. Though not yet 30, he has been
divorced twice—once from his wife and the other
time from his appendix. He got another wife; but
he’ll never marry another appendix.
When J. C. Williamson started his successful
career as manager he did so in partnership with
Garner and George Musgrove. Garner was the first
to drop out, and then Williamson and Musgrove
split over “The Belle of New York.” Musgrove
produced it in London with enormous success as a
speculation of his own; Williamson thought it
should have been secured for the firm. Garner has
long been unheard of; but a letter signed with his
name, and dated from Streatham, appears in a
late issue of the London Referee —a letter about
pantomimes and the changes they have undergone.

“Lud”: Mrs. Meyer, whom the Melbourne Divorce
Court has been talking about, is one of the daughters
of Mr. and Mrs. Kendal, the eminent theatricals,
whose name in private life was Grimston. Mrs.
Kendal often declared that none of her children
should go on the stage. However, three of them did
—a son and two daughters. The son was known in
Australia as Dorrington; the daughters acted o ,
as Grimston, the other as Kendal. Their father s
brother, Grimston, acted in England under the name
of Cawthorne. Mrs. Kendal was Madge Robertson,
youngest of 20 children of Mr. and Mrs. Robertson,
both thespians. Their well-known sons included Tom
Robertson, the dramatist, Craven Robertson, and
Shafto Robertson, who was accidentally killed bv
falling into the machinery aboard steamer on the
voyage from Melbourne to Bombay, the same day
that Walter Montgomery suicided.
Passed out at 67, J. W. Turner, head of one of
England’s most popular and prosperous touring
operatic cos. He made his operatic debut at the
Royal, in Melbourne, as Don Caesar de Bazan in
Wallace’s “Maritana.” On returning from Australia
to England he joined the Carl Rosa Opera Co., and
remained in it for a couple of years. Then he
formed a company of his own, and for a quarter of
a century he and they have been popular favorites
in the provinces and the London suburban theatres.
“Giovanni”: James Norrie (real name, James
Nutter), whose death is just reported, was, a few
years ago, one of the biggest draws in vaudeville.
He landed in Australia as a stowaway, and, later,
toured S’Africa and America with immense success.
In London, with Dan Leno as partner, he worked
three halls a night, receiving £lOO a week for his
end. But his greatest hit was made in Western
America. There, by singing simple nursery rhymes
in his beautiful treble voice, which never broke, he
could bring unfailing tears to the eyes of the Bret
Hartean miners. He retired, through ill-health,
somewhere about 10 years ago.
After four years of solid grinding at the problem,
the Edison crowd has perfected a machine which
takes pictures of men or animals, and at the same
time also records the noises they make. Of
course, that was done before on two separate instru-
ments ; but the trouble was that it was almost im-
possible to time the action record and the speaking
record so that the reproduced sounds suited the actions.
Thus the cow didn’t open its mouth and moo—
mooed first, and then opened its mouth. The strong
point of Edison’s new machine is that words and
actions coincide exactly. When Spencer, of oietu'
fame, was in Amurka recently he saw the first of
these perfected machines to hit the public eye. A
man strode downstairs, stopped at the bottom of the
screen, and, stretching out his right arm, com-
menced: “Ladies and gentlemen”- .Just then
dog dashed in, and with a noise between a bark and
a growl, seized him bv the calf. The subsequent re-
marks of the man and yells of the dog were all
beautifully timed. The next thing Spencer saw was
the interior of a zoo—and he also heard it. The
only thing missing was the smell. The name of the
contraption is the kinetophone, and when Spencer
had seen and heard the last record he hied him
round to Edison for all he was worth. When he
came away, he was about £lO,OOO poorer, but buoyed
up by the fact that he held the sole right for three
years in Australia and Maoriland. Thirty machines
are now on their way to the Commonwealth,
“Hermes”: When the amalgamation of the Austra-
lian film merchants was accomplished, the men who
engineered it looked forward confidently to the
abolition of what cut-throat competition there was,
and an equal distribution of the suburban and country
business. From the standpoint of the Combine
everything in the garden was lovely. The small
suburban men, however, became alarmed. Many of
them had not long started, and were still in debt to
the people who had supplied the machines, and a rise
in the price of films, however slight, would hit some
of them hard. In a few suburban shows the best
seats cost a shilling, but in the bulk of the picture
entertainments the choice seats bring only six-
pence for the whole evening, ahd to raise
the prices above sixpence and threepence
would mean a slump in the attendance. There
were denials that the Combine intended to increase
prices; but the suburban picture-merchant probably
felt that if he held a monopoly he would put the
screw on somebody, and he guessed human nature
was just about the same in a combine and a sub-
urban show. Both were out for all they could get.
The Victorian and Adelaide suburban and country
picture-showers took prompt action, and before the
Combine got itself properly shaken together they
had in hand a co-operative co. to supply themselves
with films from the Old World. The authorised capi-
tal is £30,000 in 60,000 10s. shares. Twenty thou-
sand shares are being issued to go on with, and a
buyer is said to have started for Europe. It is said
that a similar co. is about to be started in Sydney, to
do the same office for the Sydney suburban and
N.S.W. and Queensland towns. If these two concerns
get properly going, the Combine will lose about half
its outside customers in two swoops. To make mat-
ters worse, one of the greatest film manufacturing
firms on earth has announced that it will employ no
more agents, but deal direct with the buyers. The
strong point of the Combine was that it holds, or
held, the sole Australian rights to the films of a
group of the world’s best picture-makers. Whether
this group of manufacturers will stand to the Com-
bine in its hour of trouble no one knows; but there
are likely to be some interesting developments in the
picture business very soon.
“Harrison O.”: The dramatic possibilities of
Brother Binghi have not been properly exploited, but
Frank Brown (half-brother to Louis Esson), who
spent some time with Binghi in the Northern Terri-
tory, has written a one-act play, “The Spirit of Our
Ancestors,” in which the principal characters are
aborigines. The playlet is really an attempt to
justify the murder, by an aborigine, of a white
trooper. Brown, who knows his subject, evinces
much sympathy for Binghi. The play may be pro-
duced at the Australian Drama Night, which the
faithful in Melbourne are organising, in the absence
of the prophet Billmoore. Louis Esson, by the way,
has a new four-act Australian drama on the stocks.
“Uncle Tom” : Mummer Wilson Forbes, of the sil-
very mane, with the willowy and tragical Ella
Dodds, struck heavily on the rocks with their co., at
Walhalla (Vic.), in September last. There was no cash
in the crowd, so the co. took possession, and managed
by a couple of scratch entertainments to rake together
enough to get most of them back to the city. The
“old man,” with a wife and kiddy on his hands,
applied for work at the Long Tunnel, and there being
a shortage of hands there, was armed with a pick
and sent below on a timber-repairing job. Being
full of pluck he made good, and lasted until illness
seized him and drove him to a Melbourne hospital.
But he was by that time able to pay off all his debts
in the town, which naturally sent a “Better luck!”
after him.
“Fogged”: Mary Bronton, an Australian actress
who came into sudden prominence with Brough and
Boucicault, and did some very clever work under
Dot Boucicault’s tutelage, is still at it. She figures
as Mrs. Callander, the braw Scotch body in “The
Christian,” with Marcus Draper’s Dramatic Co. at
London Lyceum and in the provinces. Her son, Jack
Bronton, one time an Australian journalist, is now
an English author-actor-manager. He writes short
plays for the halls, acts them with his own co.,
and is kept going up and down the British Isles.
“Rosna” (from London) : Australian pianist Una
Bourne has gone with Melba a-touring over these
isles, about as good a piece of luck as could befall
a young piano-hammerer in Britain, where engage-
ments for pianists don’t grow wild on the trees
and hedges, but are mostly grabbed by the agents
and sold to the highest bidder.
IN PRIVATE LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I read the papers day by day,
And more and more I feel
Astounded at the vast array
Of knowledge that the actress may
To common folk reveal.
There’s Miss Le Starr, at Spoofkin’s show,
When interviewed last week,
Informed The Lance, with phraseful glow,
Her mission on this earth below
Was studying the Greek.
I knew, of course, she studied him —
I’ve seen her supping there
On crabs served by his seraphim,
On crabs and stout, as is her whim,
But I was not aware
That she had swatted ancient lore,
And knew that Argos grew
When Abraham was getting hoar,
The first Olympiad long o’er
(’Twas in the interview).
Another shock was Dollie Lee
(She dances at the King's).
She used to go to school with me,
Then grafted in a factoree
At making coats and things.
I read her story in The Chimes
A day or two ago:
She’d starred in leading pantomimes
In all the most exclusive climes
(Here, music sweet and low).
Her hobby in her hours of ease
Was study of the Church ;
Upon the ancient liberties
Of clergy and the laities *
She headed all research.
Next day I saw, with more amaze,
That Tottie Tartalotte,
Who sings the giddy song re stays
In that great comedy, “The Jays,”
A column space had got.
And there did recklessly admit
She didn’t like the stage.
Her heart was with the fowls that flit
About her farm, while socks she’d knit,
As in her convent age.
This thing goes on from year to year.
Maybe it’s stratagem ;
But if it’s not, it would appear
These actresses have missed their sphere;
Their hobbies seem beyond compeer—
I wish they’d stick to them.
N.S.W.

WINES
An early reference to the Government
dockyard in Sydney, from the Gazette of
April 17, 1810
We had the pleasure on Wednesday, the 7th of the
present month, to witness the launch of the new
schooner built by H.M. dockyard, Sydney, by com-
mand of his Royal Highness the Prince Regent, as a
present for the King of the Sandwich Islands, and
named by his Excellency the Governor The Prince
Regent. She is a very fine vessel, her burden 40
tons, and will be dispatched to the King of the Sand-
wich Islands as soon as completely rigged and pro-
perly equipped for so long a voyage.
That’s all! Not one single curse-word about
the infamy of having ships built at a State
dockyard. Evidently grandfather didn’t
know what a desperate tiger Socialism is.

The S. Australian Methodies, sitting in
their annual conference, have just listened
to an unusual outburst. “Ah,” wailed one
of the saints, “what poor things we Metho-
dies are nowadays! And what good gnd
great giants there used to be among us!”
Thereupon one Catt’s tail bristled. “Non-
sense !” he spat out, and proceeded to tell
some plain truths to this effect: the better
Wesleyan a business man used to be, the
tighter other people buttoned up their
pockets when they had dealings with him.
He simply wasn’t trusted; he was, on ex-
perience, put down as a humbug who got the
best of you while he prayed loudly for your
immortal soul. “We don’t cant so much,”
said Brother Catt, “but the Methodist in
business isn’t any longer made to feel that he
is regarded as a sneak-thief.” And, the
family of Catts being long honored in S.
Aus. Methodism, the conference gasped at
the dreadful reflection cast on the sect’s fore-
fathers. Yet this brother merely repeated
the old and true gibe, that once upon a time
the better a Methody was the wickeder he
thought he could afford to be, because, being
so good, he naturally would get forgiveness
for more than an average dose of sins.

An art note :
Bernard Hall hangs 54 pictures at Melbourne
Athenasum, and, as there are under half a dozen
landscapes, the walls look less like advertising
hoardings for real estate than they have done for
some time. Immediately opposite the door is “The
Quest,” founded on Omar:—
“I sent my soul through the Invisible,
Some letter of that After-life to spell.”
The artist spent some years over this work, which
is catalogued at 300 guineas. He pictures a draped
female, sitting on a floating sphere, sailing through
space. It is a delicately-executed piece of work;
but the lady evidently borrowed her right leg and
left arm from some other person, and they don’t
quite fit. “Sleep” (a reproduction of which, in
The Lone Hand, some years ago, caused a Maori-
land policeman to ring up the municipal destructor
and order out the Are brigade) occupies a prominent
place and exhorts admiration for its masterly com-
position, its beauty of line and the artistic color
contrasts provided by the stone wall, the flesh tints
and the bronze shield which hangs above the central
figure. “A Studio Shrine” is another artistic piece
of work with a bronze shield in it. Besides “Sleep,”
there are other nudes, nearly all posed with the ele-
vated hip which has become an obsession with Hall.
About the best of them is “Beauty and the Beast.”
A number of still-life subjects attract attention by
their artistic arrangement. In four interior views
of his own house the artist shows his skill in the
handling of textures and surfaces, but “The Old
Mirror” is spoilt by the introduction of a stiff
female figure. Two decorative panels, “Twilight”
and “Dawn.” are shown. (There is a touch of theatri-
calism in the careful pose of the “Twilight” lady;
but “Dawn”—a girl with roses in her streaming
hair, proclaiming the day with clashing cymbals—
is vigorous. Of the portraits, the most successful
are “Portrait of My Wife” (35), “The Spotted
Veil” (in which “my wife” again figures for the
manyth time), “Anglo-Japanese” and a beautiful
piece of pastel work, “Arab Head.” A drawing
(49) of a head, bearded, dank-haired and crowned
with thorns, merely invites disrespect. The artist
labels it “Christus.”

The reward of firm but tactful
unionism came the way of the
marine engineers in the service of
that big and wealthy Maoriland
concern, the Union Steamship
Co., last week. For a couple of
years the men in charge of the
engines of the fleet have been
raking much discontent out of
their position, and when Justice
Higgins, of the Federal Arbitra-
tion Court, handed out his im-
proved award for operation on
the Australian coast, the Maori-
land engineers made up their
minds that their conditions should
be on a par with those of their
Australian brothers, or as near
par as possible. With little fric-
tion the co. was finally lined up on
deck and spoken to with a firm
Scotch accent (or what remains
of it in M.L.) ; and after being
“surprised that loyal servants”
should do such a thing, the direc-
tors said they would be
happy to alter the pay
sheet, time sheet and privilege
sheet according to the Australian
award, but the overtime claim
was too tall. On this detail
Premier Massey agreed to act as
referee, and he declared the
Institute the winner. So all is
peace, and travellers on the co.’s
ships will now turn in on a dirty
night knowing that the engine-
room staff is tackling its tremen-
dously important job in a happier
and more satisfied spirit. And
it makes a heap of difference on
board ship to know that.
The railway porter wandered wearily
down the Sydney suburban railway plat-
form, carrying in his oily hand a lamp,
which he proceeded to insert into the glass
box wherein usually flared the gas jet.
“What,” said the sympathetic passenger,
shifting his pipe to the other side of his
face, “no gas to-night?” “Naw!” said the
porter solemnly, “the Lud Mayor broke his
shovel-handle this afternoon, and we’re up
ther spout agen.”

comparatively small craft, but a picture to
look at —painted green, with a brass-sheathed
rail, and all her gear taut and trim as a

(docks) to Hobson’s Bay has never been

South Aus. is still wondering what a
Judge and a gentleman should do. The case
came to the public through the Criminal
Court. A man of 25 admitted unlawful in-
timacy with a girl under 16. She looked
quite that age, and he swore that he
thought she was more; also he swore that he
meant to marry her, and would have done
so but for her foster-mother’s opposition,
and he would like to marry her, anyway,
“in gaol or not,” before the child was born.
The girl admitted her willingness; but the
young and austere Judge Murray remarked
that “no happiness could come out of such
a union,” and gave him two years. It is
a nice point. To let the man off on condi-
tion of a speedy marriage may possibly en-
courage the seduction of little girls. But
to punish him thus is to punish the girl as
well as an innocent baby that certainly
does not deserve punishment. As for the judi-
cial yarn that “no happiness could come
out of such a union,” the chances are that
it would turn out as well as most other
unions; and, anyhow, the question is
whether there was not the probability of
more unhappiness sticking out in the other
direction.
“Gordon G.” : Apropos of A. L. Gordon’s
line, “Life is mostly froth and bubble,” and
its inscription on public memorials in
Canada and Papua (B. 23/1/T3 and later),
I know of an instance nearer home. A
confirmed old boozer in a northern Vic-
torian country town had shuffled off. leav-
ing a fortune, in which a vast number of
long beers had bored no appreciable hole.
He had evidently made his last will and
testament in a state of semi-besottedness,
for when it came to light not one word was
there of his eldest son and best friend.
The surprised beneficiaries pocketed the
spoils and left the son to pay for the old
man’s funeral and. incidentally, supply a
tombstone. This duty the son performed.
But he let off a little of his feelings by
having carved on the stone, under the old
man’s name: “Life is mostly froth and
bubble.” And that beery innuendo is there
still.
More about the .mystery we hnv
Private Enterprise and give to baby L ° m
Sewerage milk? I should say so. The Bulletiv
gets my support for every remark it made (27/9/>io
Likewise it gets my sympathy, for I once serve, tJ'
members of its staff. I have never worked for VT
coastal dairy cocky, and know nothing ahnnt l-
methods, dirty or otherwise; but I do know tk
metropolitan cow-ranch. In the outlying <L-, e
suburbs there are any number of dairies where
animals are dryfed, and I worked at one of the
Passing by the place the other day I dropped in
and found that the same old septic methods were
in force. When a dairy owner and his employees are
naturally filthy, it needs very stringent regulations
and an inspector always on the premises to save the
public from sewerage milk. The inspector wasn’t
constantly on our premises.
We ran 140 cows in a small, bare paddock, which
was covered with the manure of ages. When cows are
thus herded together, it stands to reason that infec-
tion can travel through a herd much more quickly
than is likely to be the case in country districts
where the animals have space and freedom. At this
place when the somnolent, beasts were roused up off
the manure heaps and bailed, their udders were sel-
dom washed. Sore udders were common. Nor did
the milkers generally wash their hands. There were
no facilities to speak of for doing it —just a tap, and
the milking buckets. Once a driver we knew as “The
Dane” was rinsing out his shirt in a bucket, when
the casual inspector looked in. We had not ex-
pected him, but the Dane was equal to the occasion.
Casually emptying his washing into the big feed
vat, he emptied half a bag of chaff on top, and pre-
tended to stir the mixture vigorously. The inspector
peeped into the vat, but as the surface looked all
right he passed on.
We all slept (there were 11 of us) in a 36x10
loft above the stalls. This place was one vast bunk,
and we went straight from the insanitary hovel to the
milking. Once a week most of the crowd went in
for a spree. The spree wasn’t always hygienic. One
may understand how milk can get like sewage
when a man who has been drinking, etc. (etc.,
particularly) cleans his unwashed hands in it. The
only thing our crowd cleaned properly was the cans.
If we hadn’t done that, the milk would have soured
before delivery; and that in the boss’s eyes would
have been the one unpardonable sin.

SOLEMN DIRGE.

(To be chanted , on the occasion of a mayor’s coro-
nation, by the nuisance inspector, to an accom-
paniment of muffled dustbins.)
During the last meeting of the Drummoyne Coun-
cil, an adjournment was made while the new Mayor
was invested in the mayoral robes which have been
purchased by the ratepayers of Drummoyne
Alderman Cohen, handing over the robes, said he
could not bear to see the Mayor of Drummoyne
without robes of office, when the Mayors of the
municipalities on either side were always able to
attend State functions attired in garments fitting to
the occasions. —Sydney D.T.

It was a pity the Melbourne divorce case
Bird v. Bird, wasn’t heard by Madden, C.J ’
instead of by Cussen, J.—it offered such an
opportunity to expatiate on the innate ras-
cality and general irresponsibility of the
average respondent. Certainly, the respon-
dent in this case was the wife, but it would
have been a pleasant change to hear Mr.
Madden impartially castigate lovely woman.
Bird’s narrative was that when he married
in ’95 he was a_London stockbroker making
from £3OOO to £5OOO a year; but the slump
in Kaffirs during the war ruined him and
he became a “broker” simply, and ulti-
mately came to Australia for the good of
his pocket. Here he ran a Gippsland farm
for a while, and presently sent his wife £6O
and a request for her presence, affection and
so forth. She retained the £6O but
ignored the other matter; so he went to
London to speak with her. She received
him coldly, and passed a few remarks about
the weather; and, after assuring him that
life on an Australian farm was no use to a
Society lady, mentioned that she had an
appointment, and departed. He had subse-
quently seen her on a ’bus or motor-lorry, or
something, when she gazed at him vaguely as
though she thought she had seen him before
somewhere, and that practically ended their
acquaintance. It was a tale with more than
half the chapters missing; but the desertion
by the wife was proved beyond doubt. The
only question was whether Bird was per-
manently domiciled here, and Justice Cussen
said he iwasn’t sure of that, and would think
the matter over. Thus was lost a great
opportunity to animadvert on the unwifely
conceptions, mercenary ways and selfish life
of a Society lady, and to call attention to
the pluck, energy and abiding affection of
the husband. There are times when Mr.
Madden has his uses, and it is a pity he
wasn’t privileged to hear this case.
Siberian plague, due to infected furs, has
broken out in tailors’ workshops in St.
Petersburg. Are the ladies listening?
* * * #
When all is said and done, the gas cos.
came out of the recent Sydney trouble with
the least credit. When the strike started the
men had all the curses flung at them; but
as mayors and other right-thinking persons
walked up proudly to volunteer for a stoker’s
job, and four hours later crawled back with
the story that the job was worth a heap
more than eight shillings a day, people began
to hunt up excuses for their curses. “The
men deserve more money,” said the right-
thinking citizen, “but they shouldn’t have
gone this way to get it.” And the right-
thinking citizen, for once, hit the bull’s-eye.
But why did he wait so long to find out for
himself' the sinful under-payment of
these men —an under-payment that the
men have been complaining about for months
and months? And why didn’t the right-
thinking citizen hold a few meetings to right
that wrong? When the srasmen did an in-
justice, the well-dressed crowd was quite
willing to kick. Why didn’t it kick before
at the other dangerous injustice? The fact
is that the well-dressed crowd has a habit of
thinking that Fat can do no wrong, and that
the grimy workman’s complaint is not worth
heeding. Perhaps for the future it won’t
be quite so cocksure about that.

Judge Russell has been disappointed in
an attempt at a judgment-of-Solomon in an
Adelaide court. An Italian bird-dealer had
lost his magpie, whose strong point was
that it could say “Wakool”—the name of a
ship. By-and-bye he heard the familiar
word, and discovered the bird that said it
in a Chinese fruit shop. But the Chow
vociferated that his bird, bought and paid
tor, says “Nypor” (Chinese for mother),
t n< 7 the .other thing at all. Then the
Judge had his luminous idea. He sent for
the magpie, and let both sides “sool” it on
to talk. And the thing that was bound to
happen did happen—-the wretched fowl re-
fused to say anything at ail. Dago non-
suited accordingly.

The N.S.W. Labor Conference threw eco-
nomics and general politics aside for five
crowded minutes to discuss the case of the
babe who on his journey to a cold world
misses the wedding ring through which all
children must pass or be damned. Mrs.
Lane, speaking on behalf of the Paddington
Political Labor League, suggested that the
Conference should whisper in the ear of the
Government that a natural corollary of the
maternity bonus would be an Act to make
the infant born out of wedlock the legiti-
mate child of his father. The Conference
carried the idea almost unanimously; and
the wonder is that the Wowser has not
already gone on the war-path. At the pre-
sent time the child has • to suffer for the
illegality of its parents; and the Wowser
declares it is the Law of Divinity. It is
also the Law of Divinity that Wowsers
should be born without trousers; but no one
has ever yet met a Wowser who refused to
wear trousers because Providence —or the
Devil —sent him into the world without
them. There is no earthly reason why the
child born out of wedlock should suffer on
that account as regards his share of his
father’s estate. Rather, considering how
much of home life he is deprived of, he
should be enabled by law to recover damages
from his old man , for the wrong he has done
him The attitude of the Labor Conference
is eminently sensible. The day will come
when the Illegitimate will be the special
ward of the State, and will sue his dad for
libel and for unprovoked aspersion on his
character, prospects, good name and re-
spectability. Come to think of it, it might
be worth while for a natural son to try a
case against a rich unnatural father in the
Equity Court even as the law now stands,
and thereby create a new precedent. Surely
Wowserdom would pass around a lavish,
not to say reckless, hat to raise the wind in
such a good cause.

Where is the writer who would dare to
stretch the bow of fiction as far as fact
pulled it lately at Eaglehawk (Vic.)? Three
men had charged a run of holes and lighted
the fuses; but, before they could clear out,
one of the charges exploded, knocking them
down and putting out their candles, two
had groped their way nearly to safety when
the third called to them that he was pinned
down by a splinter of stone. The two went
back —a piece of pluck that was all in the
day’s work—and were striving to release
their mate when they were knocked down
bv the second explosion. They scrambled
back to the plat; but by the tune they
reached it the remaining shots had fired, so
thev groped their way along the
order to pick up the fragments of their mate.
But a very-much-alive mate met them on the
wav The last explosion, instead of shat-
tering him, had shattered the rock that
pinned him, and set him free!

“The Old Bold Mate of Henry Morgan”:
There’s a man after my own heart swinging
the axe for the Sydney Telegraph at the
Federal capital military camp. From his
last despatches:—
A ration of rum lias been served out to-night to
all hands. Everything is, therefore, in good
spirits,...
A ration of rum is half a gill per man, and this
has been ordered by the brigadier....
A rum ration was served out to the older hands...
It is hard to understand why the ration of rum
....should have been denied to the trainees....
There is no rum for anyone to-night....
The camp has relapsed into its former state of
cheerless quietness....
It is once more “dry camp.”....
The army service cart arrived with a supply
of rum from Queanbevan, and all retired to rest in
a merrier frame of mind.
Hooray!

When February was dying, the grand old
problem or nightmare of the missing Mary
Ann was wheeled into the foreground by a
deputation of N.S.W. public-school teachers,
who waited on McGowen’s Minister for
Education, with a request for a Domestic
Science College as a means of gilding the
unrefined frying-pan and casting a glamor
o’er the deserted gas stove. One lady
champion of the scheme, by way of prelude,
asked indignantly why the domestic “help”
should be looked down upon? Of course,
nobody tried to supply an answer, the ques-
tion being obviously one of the oratorical
variety. But the plain fact is that Mary
Ann is no longer looked down upon, because
the place where she used to stand is unin-
habited. But she isn’t absent on account
of the down-looking, or evil-eyeing, or what-
ever it was. In her present factory or
workroom job she still has to endure the
scorn of the ruffling suburbanites. But her
troubles about that! Her hours of
work are limited, she gets her wages regu-
larly, she lives amongst her own people,
and she eats good food and sufficient of it.
Sad to say, the so-called “servant difficulty”
is to some extent a difficulty of poor food
and unpaid wages. Quite a number of
Suburbia’s well-dressed womenfolk have the
reputation of feeding their household ./Hur-
riedly and cheaply on sardine lines, and
putting the domestic surplus, on their backs
and heads. Mary’s wages are frequently
included, and that and the sardine diet
drive her to sudden resignation while the
missus is away in town afternoon teaing
magnificently amongst the local peerage at
a shilling per hat. The glory of social
climbers’ suburbs has to be paid for, but no
intelligent daughter of a Laborite is going
to willingly contribute her health and wages
to the cause. The weary old question is
almost as much a “mistress difficulty” as a
“servant difficulty,” and a Domestic Science
College won’t do much good unless its cur-
riculum includes the following as compul-
sory subjects: Cl) Manners, (2) Considera-
tion for Mere Hirelings, (3) Plain Honesty,
(4) Advanced Honesty, (5) Tact, (6) Care
of the Stomach. The honors course might
consist of three months’ scullery service in
a large household, under a person with some
such name as Jane Smith.
* * * *
The Victorian Government, pushing on
with the scheme for the electrification of
the Melbourne suburban railways, sends
Blazey, superintendent of the goods train
service, and Calcutt, signal engineer, to
America, Germany, France and England, to
study their lines; and Chief Commissioner
Fitzpatrick goes for a general brush-up. The
power station, Yarraville, will be quickly
started, but cannot be finished till the end
of next year. The first suburban lines to be
converted will be to Sandringham, in the
south, and to Broadmeadows, in the north,
passing through Brighton, Prahran, Mel-
bourne and Essendon. These should be open
under the new conditions in June, 1915.
The contracts now in hand include: Bab-
cock and Wilcox, power station, £258,230;
Parsons and Co., station equipment,
£122,235; Siemens dynamo works, plant,
up to £168,000; the New York Electric Car
Co., plant, up to £678,180. Other options
await Merz’s decision. The first job to be
locally put in hand is a siding from the
Williamstown railroad to the power house
site at Yarraville; and this should be started
in May.

A LAY OF A WORSHIPFUL SHOVEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“But I am prepared to work as a volunteer in the
interests of the community for four hours a day if
sufficient help to make this offer effective can be
guaranteed.”— Sydney’s Gaud Mayor.
The gasman put his coke-rake by,
And swore by all his grimy might
That none with gas should boil or fry,
Or see a gleaming jet at night,
Until they granted his demand
And paid an extra bob a day
To every wight whose toiling hand
Had taught the big retorts to pay.
The statesman came with visage grate
And promised all that reason might ;
He pleaded with the blokes to save
The public cove from rayless night.
’Twas vain! The gasman set his boot,
And vowed he would not bend or bow,
Or raise one single flake of soot
By perspiration of his brow.
He would not shift, he would not move ;
He let the furnace sink and cool
With keen and high desire to prove
His title to the name of fool.
The city lay in black despair,
Maintaining life on things in tins,
While through the darkened summer air
The candles twinkled like small sins.
The statesman in his zeal proclaimed
That there and then the strike must end,
That such wild doings must be tamed
And such stiff backs be made to bend ;
And some policemen wandered down
To help kind Peace to do her job,
Lest maddened persons sacked the town
In search of that elusive bob.
But still the heavy darkness reigned,
And still the half-starved public swore,
And every lightless thing remained
Exactly as it was before,
Until the splendid voice was heard
Thrilling the pulsings of the air,
And with a toil-compelling word
Stepped in the breach, my lord the Mayo*.
With rounded waistcoat in the van
He seized the shovel of his might,
And sweating like a common man,
Set to the task of making light.
Upsprang the jets in all the ways,
The household grills were wrapped in heat,
And flaring gas-lamps sang the praise
Of his high Gaudhead in the street.
The strike was dead (or should have been
If courage had its true return),
And splendid valor thus was seen
To teach that even coals will burn;
Yes, even coals and bits of wood
And scraps of paper leap in flame,
Inspired by such a hardihood
As heroes in their might proclaim!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Silas Snell”: His name was Samuel
Ogden. His wife called him “Sam’l.” They had
a son named Thomas. They called him “Tummy.”
Sam’l wore very large, obtrusive booots, and “t*
ould lass” had teeth to match. They were looking
at Lockover’s land, with a view to purchase. “Ay,
lad,” said Sam’l, “yon isna gradilv good land, but I
reckon I can do summat wi’ it.” The land was at
Three Hills, and its chief defect was rabbits, a
defect Lockover could not hide from the cute cus-
tomer “fra Lancasheer,” as bunny was bobbing up
all over the place. Lockover was selling before the
rabbits ate his family. Sitting on any log on the
estate you might have hit 50 rabbits with a stick
in half as many minutes. A bargain was struck.
Sam’l Ogden would give seven-ten an acre for SO
acres. He had been gone about 20 minute" when he
drove back, and approached Lockover with marked
concern. “Mind, lad, you must let the rabbits go
wi’ t’ land,” he said.
SUMMER PASTIMES—TAIL-HUNTING AT BATHURST.
A local resident, writing to the press, says he has official proof that 295 dogs were shot by the police in
Bathurst during last year. He asserts that the animals were captured, each tail being worth 2s. 6d. to the
“apprehending” constable. — News item.
“Piccaninny” : Re the land explorations of
crocodiles (B. 20/2/’l3). I have seen the brutes in
Northern Queensland rivers quite 200 miles as the
fly crows from the sea, and the Lord knows how
many miles as the river serpentines. At Einasleigh,
on the Etheridge, they may be seen all the year
round —not big chaps, yet running up to sft. and
6ft. long. Brother Binghi of those parts is very
fond of baked croc. I was in Georgetown in 190/,
and, noticing a commotion amongst the blacks in
their camp in the river-bed, inquired and found they
had picked up the tracks of a croc, in the dry sand.
After two days’ hunting they got him in a big
waterhole, and for the next two days you couldn t
get a bit of wood chopped or a horse run in for love
or money. Every nig. had his equator full to the
limit of its capacity. I had intended to taste, but
the smell and the fact of Brother Binghi having got
his hand in first stopped me. All I can say is that
the flesh cooked as white as whiting or bream, and
looked tender and juicy. But the smell it was
worse than gidyea on a wet morning 1
“Jersey”: “Fax” (B. 27/2/’l3) kicked
over the milk bucket in his haste to deny my abso-
lutely truthful statement that the average cocky’s
methods of milk-production are hideously dirty.
I’ ve seen thousands of cows milked, without the
cow’s dirty udder being washed, and the filth accu-
mulations have been added to the strength or hum
of the milk. It is a common practice for the farm
hand to go to his work in the bails at “cow-time”
direct from the paddock, begrimed with sweat and
mire, don a dirty old sack, and fasten to the bull’s
wife. The sack is to keep his pants clean. Most
cockies imagine that the limit of cleanliness is
achieved when they comply with Government regula-
tions to cement milking bails. The stench and filth
of the yard (even when paved with cobble stones),
in which perhaps 30 to 100 cows are herded, is a
minor item. I intended to say several other things
about Private Enterprise, but it is easier and more
convincing to let Dr. Clubbe, Sydney’s well-known
child specialist, have a go. Here’s a fragment or
hunk from his evidence before a Royal Commission
recently:—
He had known a case where milk had been tested
after one hour’s sterilising, and found to contain
from two to eight million bacteria. When the sup-
plier learned of the results of this test he was
righteously indignant; he sent samples of his milk
for analysis, direct, feeling certain of its purity.
The analysis was simply appalling. His samples
were found to contain thirty-four million bacteria 1
And if that is the case of the man who is indig-
nant because he reckons he has a clean bill, what
about the man, bloke or fair cow who doesn’t give
a thingummy bob, or even thingummy sprat, whether
he has a clean bill or not?

“Reka” tells the story of a de’il of a
fright:—
For years Maoriland fruit insoectors have kept
a vigilant eye open at Auckland for the Mediter-
ranean fruit-fly. Cargo after cargo of oranges
have been dumped into the destructor on suspicion,
until importers furiously asserted that what the
inspectors suffered from most was a diseased imag-
ination. Furthermore, all owners of orchards were
ordered under fear of awful penalties to spray regu-
larly with arsenate of lead, whereupon most sub-
urbanites. dispensed with orchards altogether.
Numerous inquiring babes had narrow escapes by
poison, and nervous old ladies chopped bits out of
themselves in the effort to annihilate the peach-tree
in the garden. Then the Department announced one
day that, owing to the thoroughness of its cam-
paign, not a trace of fruit-fly existed in the whole
province. It specially commended the noble co-
operation of the orchardists, who had spent hun-
dreds in guarding against the pest. Next day a
pleasant, chatty little man from the North Shore,
who didn’t want to make any trouble, but liked
to know things, called in at the office with a
couple of peaches off a tree in the back yard, and
said: “I’d like to know what kind of an insect this
is that’s eating my peaches. It’s bigger than a
codlin moth, and riddles the fruit more.” The
expert in charge investigated the insect, and gave a
blood-curdling shriek. All the staff rushed in.
They looked, tore their hair, and danced upon the
carpet. The insect was a particularly fine speci-
men of the fruit-fly, and the owner of the back-
yard peach-tree reported plenty more at home. In
less than an hour that tree was a pile of ashes, and
so were a few more trees belonging to inoffensive
neighbors. The fly was, nevertheless, expected to
have a firm hold next season ; but there was never a
sign of him. Now an extra-special expert an-
nounces that Auckland need have no fear of the
pest, as it would never survive the wet, cold winter ;
and the men who rooted up the back-yard peach-
tree, and the ladies who cut pieces out of them-
selves with the meat-chopper, don’t know whether
to be glad or mad about it.

round Ganmain and Coolamon (N.S.W.) is worthy
0 £ bbe mos (; enthusiastic disbelief. I’ve been at

the solidest graft within four seas. Working on a
wheat-thresher is far more solid, as I can vouch after
five years at it. If “Ginger” knows anybody who has
done better than thresh and sew 312 wheat bags (the
old big bags) in a day (one man sewed the lot and
helped to stack them), I’d like a lock of his hair,
Never heard of 27 stitches in a chaff bag. Twenty-
four would hold wheat in an old malt bag well, and
18 in a wheat bag of to-day is considered close sewing.

“Te Babarantiko”: The “yam” of the
Northern Territory mentioned by “Mallarapoonie”
(B. 6/2/’l3) is probably a very close relation to
arum cordifolium (the pulvia of the Samoan dia-
lects and the babai of the Line Islands) of the atolls
of the Pacific. On these hungry coral islets, rising
but slightly above highest springtides, where the
bill of fare consists almost entirely of cocoanuts,
pandanus fruit in season, and fish —and often not too
much of any of them —the babai is widely culti-
vated. Lacking a swamp, large, more or less regu-
lar shaped trenches about 6ft. deep, are dug in the
sand. (Brackish water is found at that depth on
most atolls.) The edible is white, cone shaped, with
masses of fine roots growing from “pores” in the
skin. There are several varieties, and some are an
excellent substitute for potatoes. A tasty (for the
native potato) dish is made of grated babai, grated
cocoanut and molasses made of fresh palm sap,
mixed, kneaded and baked in a leaf. The arum
cordifolium (related to the taro) is a slow grower,
but, given time, attains to enormous size. The
lily leaves and stalks I have seen upwards of 15ft.
long, and the leaf large enough to completely en-
velope an average-sized man. At a feast given on
Funafuti in honor of the visit of the King of Nuku-
leilei, I saw one arum c. root which took two stal-
wart natives to carry slung on poles, and a root re-
quiring all of one man’s strength to carry on his
shoulder is not in any way a rarity.

“Incas”: Persisting in an old habit of
speech, one of the “British Isles” tennis team talked
the other day of coming 16,000 miles to win the Davis
Cup. It is a hoary myth brought from the “British
Isles” to the “Australian Isles” (mainly Hobart
Town) in the days of sailing ships and passages tak-
ing 120 days. Here are the P. and O. figures from
Tilbury: Fremantle, 10,125; Adelaide, 11,478 ; Mel-
bourne, 11,978 ; and Sydney, 12,555. The place by
steamer route furthest from Tilbury is Yokohama
(by the Cape of Good Hope), 14,403 miles, Seattle
and Vancouver are 14,345 miles via Capetown. May
one hope this hie jacet will cover two dead stupidi-
ties —the other one being the expression “British
Isles.”

“L. Soup”: I agree with “Ginger” (B.
13/2/’l3), that the bloke who sews behind a chaff-
cutter deserves the Hard Graft Medal. All the same,
“Ginger” staggered me when he talked about sewers
putting in 27 stitches per bag. Never knew more
than 10 stitches (excluding lugs) to be put in, and
I have sewed behind Rowston’s, Evans’s, Logan’s and
Conway’s cutters, the chief ones round Coola-
mon and Ganmaiii (N.S.W.). The average output
was 400 to 550 per day; and 678 were the most I
ever sewed in a day. For that particular cutter it
was a record. No cutter in those districts can put
out a daily average of 600 bags, bar Mangelsdorf’s,
and there is a standing offer of £4 a week for any
single sewer who can sew behind it. But he wouldn’t
be asked to average 900 a day, as “Ginger’s” sewers
were. I’d like to have a look at that 900 a day
cove. So would Logan, at Ganmain, who has a
standing challenge of £5O for his sewer (record
tally was 712 sewed and stacked) to fight anything
in the country.
“Pick” : Here’s another of your decadent
Australians from the wheat country. He’s 14 years
old, and lives near Gundagai (N.S.W.). With a
three-horse harvester he took off 81 bags of wheat
between jackass and kookaburra. He then rode 18
miles to a dance, and commenced operations there
by fighting 10 rounds with a bigger boy. He danced
in his black eyes and bluchers all night, and next
day—Sunday—hunted foxes until sundown. Then
he rode home (18 miles) and was on the harvester
again in the morning. Incidentally, during the
week he heard from the “old man” that dancing and
fox-hunting were a (blank) lazy game, and that he
ought to have been home digging out rabbits.
SHOOTIN' AN’ FISHIN’.
“ Talk about s hootin’ and fishin! Shootin' all
day; cornin' home, spotted six big wood,
ducks siftin' on a limb over swamp. Used
all shot, so opened pocket knife and dropped
in barrel an' fired. Knife cut limb just
above their heads. Ducks flew up an' got
necks caught in closin' limb. Limb broke.
Fell in water. Waded in. flung limb with
ducks stickin on over me shoulder. Dash
me, when I got out outer th' bank, if the
seat of me pants wasn't full o' fish!”
BRINGING BACK THE BEER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bottles wreathed around each neck,
Bottles slung from every wrist,
Thus we save the beer from wreck.
(Beer is very dear.)
Midnight moonlight shining bright
Black the bush and silver-white,
Full the night of noise and fight,
When good-feeling we assist,
Coming back with beer.
Out at camp our cobbers wait
For their well-beloved whack.
Though the hour is somewhat late,
Beer is drawing near.
Shifting yards and yards of soil,
Long has been the time of toil
Until evening billy-boil.
Now we tramp the moonlit track
Through the bush, wjth beer.
Though at noon the dry miles are
Nothing but a dusty nark,
When we leave the cool bush bar
Toilward then to steer,
By the moon the miles are less
When with joyance we progress.
Knowing that the boys will bless
Us who, through the scented dark,
Campwards march, with beer.
Filing down the creek we come,
Near the finish of our tramp ;
Soon we’ll all be having some.
Hear the blighters cheer,
Echoing the row we make!
One cheer for ourselves we take;
Two are raised for beer’s own sake
As we swing along to camp,
Bringing back the beer.
VICTORIA.

Dear Bulletin.—Permit me to cross nibs
with “Nym” (B. 6/2/’l3) on the subject of certain
ceremonies practised by the natives of the Northern
Territory. As he says, the practise of circumcision
is almost universal with the tribes; but the custom
doesn’t carry the importance that “Nym” would
seem to attach to it. Any young native buck, be-
tween seven and 12 years, or just before the age of
is liable to be run down and summarily
circumcised upon the slightest provocation; and the
happening, beyond the enforced adoption of a loin-
cloth, doesn’t materially effect his standing in the
tribe. The initiatory ceremony of “man-making” is
quite another thing; and takes place some years
later iii life. I write of the tribes I know best—of
the Mootburras of the Victoria Biver basin and of
the Wogites of the tablelands behind Camooweal.
Among these, so far from the ceremony of circumci-
sion being a secret one, or performed at any given
time of day or phase of moon, it may be perpetrated
any old hour or season, though the Old People prefer
strong sunlight for any business connected with the
letting of blood. Any white man, or other person
interested, may attend this ceremony in the most
open and most cordial way. But the matter of sub-
incision, or what may be called “the race-suicide
operation,” is quite another thing; though it is
almost as universal as the other among tribes of the
far interior. So far, too, from “Nym” being correct
in his statement that the natives “keep a few of
the best type of blackfellow to breed from, but geld
all inferior males,” it may be said that no such rule
exists. To confound sub-incision in any way with
castration (or “gelding”) is wrong. A sub-incised
blackfellow may not only become a father at will,
but hundreds of him do so, and in a manner that is
absolutely beyond any necessity for proofs. Therein
lies the crux of the whole custom —the subject may
only become a father at will; and in regions where
food commodities are scarce, and where the necessity
of curtailing the population is more or less great, and
where, consequently, the custom is most often prac-
tised, the native husband doesn’t will with any great
frequency—perhaps once or twice in his lifetime he
permits himself a child. ■ No process of enforced
selection is in vogue among the tribes. Any native
may select his wife providing she is outside the pro-
hibited degree of consanguinity. That he has under-
gone some hideous “stone 1 surgery” operation seems
to make no difference to the woman. She is taught
to expect nothing else; and lucky is she, indeed, if
she has not undergone some dreadful mutilation of
her own; such as that unspeakable horror which is
so often perpetrated upon their young women by
the ghouls of more than one Central Australian tribe.
—Yours, etc., Mali.arapoonie.
HOLIDAY MEMORIES.
What the gentle hillside pathways become at last.
“Gouger”: The tree-fern nut is Binghi’s
flour, though the hairy yam and half a dozen
other such vegetables are almost as good. A large
quantity of the nut is picked and roasted on a flat
rock on the river banks. The nut is then cracked,
the kernel taken out, and crushed as fine as flour
between two rocks. The natives are very dexterous
and can crush an enormous quantity of nuts in a
very short time. The ground-up flour is then heaped
into a dilly bag, which is a beautifully interwoven
basket of a very fine fibre, and acts in the same way
as a finely made culinary sieve. The basket with its
contents is then placed in running water for about
twelve hours. This dissolves all the badness in the
nut, otherwise it would be uneatable. Only the
wholesome flour is left behind, and the natives mix it
up as we do a damper.
MANNERS BEFORE EVERYTHING.
DOCTOR (to new “case” holding end of his finger on): “Well! Here , take your hat off !”
“Copo”: In B. 6/2/’l3, Aldridge Evelyn
bolsters his case for a vegetarian diet by this argu-
ment :
If man was meant to be carnivorous he would
have teeth resembling those of other carnivorous
animals, which he has not.
Supposing that to be so:—
(1) If man was meant to be herbivorous, like the
cow, would he not have four hoofs and other facili-
ties for getting about in the paddock and eating
the nice fresh grass and vegetables ?
(2) If the cow was intended to be herbivorous,
like man, would it not have a gas stove, a cooking
pot and a box of matches to boil its vegetables
with ?
(3) If man is to eat like a horse, why do you
never meet the horse at the dentist’s, sitting up in
the chair and getting his molar filled? Also, why
can’t you tell a man’s age by his teeth, the same
as a horse?
(4) If man is intended to be a vegetarian, why is
it that the most notable vegetarian in the Scripture
only stuck to his diet of green-stuff while he was
mad ?
(5) Why is it that the meat-eating races boss the
earth, and the nations that eat green-stuff don’t?
There are other questions, but these are a few to
go on with.

“X.P.D.”: “McNorth” (B. 16/1/’l3)
doubts whether the Papuan has a good ear for music.
First of all, I protest against the slumping together
of several entirely distinct races for the purpose of
discussing some characteristic or propensity that may
be strong in one lot and entirely absent in another.
Taking. British Papua alone: Round about the Fly
River the people look like bookmakers dipped in
soot, tall, with hooked noses, and speaking a lan-
guage abounding in s’s and k’s, using bows and
arrows and making sago in between times. About Port
Moresby they are skinnier, smaller in features, speak-
ing a lingo called Motu, and being kept by wives,
who are potters. From about the end of the Penin-
sula, adjacent to Samarai, to Goodenough Bay, or
thereabouts, they are like Samoans in appearance,
language and disposition. About Collingwood Bay
are the Maicin, who may, perhaps, have been of the
same stock as the Fly River and Kiwai people, but
speak a language showing little or no resemblance.
Further north again is a race of which the Binadeli
is a type, speaking a language so different in its
very fundamentals, construction and accent that
the southern boys seem to be unable to learn it. Com-
parisons of skull formation, customs, language,
carvings and some other things go to indicate that’
while a large section of the tribes towards the north
had Asiatic ancestors, the Samoan-like southerners
hailed from the other direction—the Pacific coast of
America, or Hawaii perhaps, and further back still,
from wherever the Aryan race had its beginnings. In
other words, the southern ’ Papuans are more nearly
related to the Anglo-Saxons than to the northern
Papuans. Which reminds me. I have heard a scratch
team of natives from about Taupota and Bartle Bay
—north-east coast —singing in church. Tune was
always kept, and the tone and individual voice-
quality were better than those of an average white
church choir. The women are inclined to sing
through their noses, but the voices of most of the
men are naturally musical, and from the chest all
the time. One of the sweetest bov-voices I ever heard
was owned by a Taupota lad. I don’t know much
about the musical abilities of other Papuan nations.
“Gouger”: In the N. Queensland forest
and scrub country you can gee more snakes when
you’re sober than you can in most places after you’ve
started a bad attack of Queensland rum-fever. In
many parts of the scrub the tiger snake abounds,
and when met on the narrow tracks he refuses abso-
lutely to budge. As his kiss means almost certain
death, a man gets a nasty shock now and then. But
there is one creature that doesn’t care, and he is
the wild pig. I have seen an old sow in the scrub
make a rush for an eight-foot carpet snake, plant
her front toes firmly on the reptile to prevent him
getting away, and then literally pull him to pieces,
meanwhile calling up a litter of fat young suckers
to partake of the feast. I spoilt the feast. I had a
gun. Also, I wanted pig for dinner. Snake bites
never seem to affect the pigs, owing probably to
their armor plate hide. I measured one lot that
was 2in. thick above the shoulders. And that wasn’t
anything special either. Shoot an old boar with a
cannon,-and when the ball rebounds you’ll get a new
light on the subject.
* * » *
“Stohr Ruddell” : The writer who doubts
the utility of barb-wire in fences (B. 16/l/’3) can
have had no practical experience of stock-keeping.
No number of ordinary wires will keep a fence taut.
A few months’ scratching and rubbing, and not only
the wires but the posts are loosened. I have seen
seven wires strained in August in a four-mile fence,
and when the grass has commenced to dry off, in
November, you could without difficulty make any
two of the wires meet. In an adjoining paddock,
holding as many head of stock, a small swag couldn’t
be pushed through a five-wire fence, with two barbs,
which had been erected 10 years-before. Where there
are cattle, and the feed is growing scarce or rank,
the barb is the only part of the fence that counts.
A bullock smells an object before attempting to
clear it, and the barb gives a restraining shock to
his sense of touch. Admittedly, numbers of good horses
have been ruined, but mostly through ignorance or
laziness on the part of the landowner. New settlers
in the Vic. and South Aus. mallee run up “light-
ning” fences (posts a chain apart and one barb) ;
then when stock has been turned into the unfamiliar-
surroundings nocturnal galloping has ended with
the majority of the horses tom from hock to neck.
Cases of serious injury where the fences are of five
or six wires with barbs are exceptional.

“Karee”: Out on the Wide Bay Islands
_(Q.) the favorite method of breaking the Sabbath
is by going stingaree-spearing. In the words of some
bloke or other, it’s like beer—once a customer
always a customer. The operators carrv spears
made of cypress pine with fencing-wire points, and
wade on the sand banks towards the “beater” from a
small boat. It takes skill to hit a “sting” dashing
by at full speed, and some manage to get away in
deep water, in spite of perhaps five spears hooked
m their hide. When one’s cornered, with the killing
stake in his gizzard, the fun starts, as he lashes with
his tail, and the “hunters” must dodge and watch
their chance to collar him and wrench the sting off
the quarry is good eating, too, when properly
skinned and cooked. I’ve tried all sorts of hunting-
boar, stag wolf, dingo and ’roo—and I’ve shot lions,
tigers, buffaloes, Spanish-American and South Afri-
can farm-burners, but I back “sting”-spearing for
exhilarating sport and excitement. And there’s a
hn.tL 0 * d ; l T r ’, to0 ’ aS Sharks prey on these oth er
brutes, and the hunt sometimes ends in a very earn
est rush for the boats. y
“Leo Lear”: Has any Abotanist run i nto
strawberry clover, and can he tell me any bad points
about it that I have missed? I don’t know where
it was imported from; but, as far as I’ve got with
my investigations, it’s one of the few things that w
are the better for having. The vegetable is nJ
eminently suited to wet, swampy ground near th
coast, and flourishes along the Victorian seaboard 6
Where any other but swamp growth would be killed
out by water, the strawberry clover gets its roots in
and hangs on to life until summer happens along and
the liquid subsides. Then it starts to do things, and
its growth is swifter than the growth of sin. Farm-
ers drive their sheep off it at night, as the tendrils
grow through the wool and anchor the animals to
earth; and then only a meat axe can liberate the
jumbueks. I think it first appeared on the Gipps-
land River flats about 12 years ago ; now its pointed
leaf is found all along tae Victorian coast. I\ e
never heard of it growing far inland, but it gets as
near to the sea as any pasture, frequently occupying
with marram grass the last sandflats. In places I’ve
found it down at the water's edge; then Neptune
resenting the infringement on his sea-growths, hits
it below the belt with an extra-high tide, and there
isn’t any umpire on hand to call it a foul.
* * * *
“D.R.Y.”: What makes a herd of bees
leave its home without apparent reason? I
know that they will vacate their boarding-house if
they are attacked by ants, if strange knocks resound
on the outside walls, or if small boys or stock
unduly interfere with them, but in the case I am
puzzling over none of those things could have hap-
pened. Last spring, or there around, I unearthed
a colony of the honey merchants near the summit of
a huge box-tree near Albury (N.S.W.), and on every
occasion that I spied out the land they were still at
toil. Yet, when last Sunday three of us hewed that
vegetable down, nary a bee, barring two dead
drones, was there, despite the fact that each cranny
and crevice was full of honey. And the tree was
not infested by ants, boys or other noxious animals.
Did these honey-fowl hear us saying that we would
come to rob their store on Sunday, and quit on
Saturday night in consequence—shoot the moon, in
fact?
“D.McC.”: I don’t say Jim got a fair
deal, still he started the game himself. Mrs.
McCorkscrew kept a pub on the tinfields, and amongst
the admirers of her handsome daughter Nancy was
Hop Sing, who was stacking away a tidy fortune
by storekeeping. Now, Sing had a sporting chance
when Jim and Bill happened along, and, being white
(and occasionally tight) Australians, they were
naturally riled. So, recognising Nancy’s weakness
for wealth, they let it be known that they were big
station-owners from Queensland, and that they were
going to stay for a bit to spell the horses and do
some business. Things progressed (as they sometimes
do) and presently Nancy became Mrs. Jim. Then
she discovered that that sportive joint was a mere
drover, with no maddened impulse to leave ma-in-
law’s pub. The next performance was the arrival
of that interesting member of our native fauna, the
stork. We were all in the kitchen to hear the news
when old Dr. Moriarty, who had ridden 15 miles to
his patient, walked in. “What is it, doctor; a
boy or a girl?” was the excited inquiry. “It’s a
Chinaman,” he replied, as a wild shriek tore the
atmosphere. It came from the direction of the store,
and I took it that Jim was breaking the news to
Hop Sing.
A DREAM OF FAIR MEN.
“I've just been speaking to Wadgeree. He's
very white for a black fellow
“ Sure, an' shmall wonder, sorr. Wasn't kis
mother married to wan av th' fair sex?"
The root of the tongue is a pretty ghastly
place in which to get bitten by a snake, yet that is
where Henzenroeder, a locally-famous charmer of
Tanunda (S.A.) got it. Still, he had fairly asked
for it, for his favorite trick at performances is to
put a “tiger’s” head in his mouth. The trouble
came while he was rehearsing, away from home; one
sudden streak struck before he could close his teeth
behind its head to keep it quiet. He collapsed and
went nearly the color of Jack Johnson, but skilled
attention has brought him round.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
THE RELATIVE IMPORTANCE OF
THINGS.
In these days of our growing strength and leap-
ing revenues, the mere question of cost is too paltry
to be considered. Expense there must be in any
case ; and the sweating of local Governors to save a
few thousands does not appeal to Australians. If
we do the thing at all, we can afford to do -it well.
—Melbourne paper on the mooted abolition of the
superfluous State Governors.
Granny : “You horrid little beast! You don't
mind spending millions and millions on
ugly guns and arsenals and factories and
things, but when it comes to paying for
these imported and far more beautiful
and artistic and noble and inspiriting
ornaments, you start your growling and
sneering an' — an' —” (dissolves into tears).
Dr. “Jimmy” Graham, who specialised in
mankind s innards, was hit on his own strong
point last week, and died suddenly. Com-
mercially, he didn’t make much of a success
of his profession; he had too much public
spirit and too little “bedside manner.” But
he was very useful as a promoter of the
Women’s Hospital, and as Mayor of Sydney
he did more than anybody else to break the
city of its insanitary habit of spitting on the
pavements. In his latter days, not having
much of a practice, and being squeezed out
of both Parliamentary and municipal life, he
did some guinea-pigging, and, as a company-
director, was mostly regarded by the men
who worked with him as a conspicuous
success.

The original Watson, of Wat-
son’s Bay, Sydney, is recalled by
the death of his son, Charles, at
Bordertown (S.A.). This son was
born at the bay in 1836, or there-
abouts ; spent a long time on Vic-
torian goldfields, and finished up as
a farmer and stock-breeder. Those
fine jumpers, Coral, Isis and
I X L, belonged to him, and he was
generally as ready to lay against
anyone else l s horse as to back his
own.
One of Maoriland’s best-known
railway men, A. L. Beattie, is on
the eve of handing in his spanner
as chief mechanical engineer, after
about 36 years’ service. His early
training was given him at a big
railway foundry at Leeds, and,
after completing his apprentice-
ship, he became draughtsman and
assistant works manager there. Yet
he was only 22 when he arrived in
M.L. and joined the Otago service
as assistant engineer. From that
day he moved steadily up the lad-
der, until 1890, when he was
handed the iob he is now handing
back .in return for a pension.
Beattie was a conscientious public
servant and a good railway man,
although he never got the boom
that lesser _ abilities received in
M.L.’s Public Service.
