Consul in Sydney soon after the close of the
(Timean War, has just retired from the
office, after serving in it for the record period
of 56 years.
Archdeacon Gunther, for many years at
the historic Church of St. John, Parramatta
has completed his 50th year of parsonhood’
He is the son of the Rev. Gunther, who
years ago, was attached to the aboriginal
mis |i° n at Wellington, and was afterwards
at Mudgee. It was he who gave the name
to Havilah, the Paget Bailys’ old home near
Mudgee. A picnic was held in the nlace • Mr
Gunther, fossicking for gold, found a color,
and instantly exclaimed “Havilah!” the
Biblical word for gold. Mr. Baily adopted
the name, and Havilah sheep soon became
well known on the market.
The wheat which won the champion prize
-j at the Adelaide Show this month was grown
s on the farm near Mt. Barker that pro-
duced the wheat which won the gold medal
for grain against the world at the Crystal
Palace International Exhibition (London)
in 1851. John_Frame was the exhibitor in
each case, but the present-day John is a
grandson of the farmer of 60 years ago.
A man in Paris tells of the new French
President and his election: —
It is at Versailles, in the large hall of Louis XlV.’s
stately palace, that the members of the Government
meet to elect the man who is to oe at the head of
the nation for seven years. The ancient streets and
grand old avenues were gaily decorated; every hotel
and restaurant had smartened itself; a special ser-
vice of trains had been organised between Paris and
Versailles. Paris was soon strangely quiet; every-
one of note and fashion, every visitor to the capital
eager for historic sights and scenes of local color,
went out to Versailles. The voting took place during
the afternoon. Three successive ballots were made.
The excitement was tremendous when, towards seven
in the evening, the final result was known. Cries of
“Vive Poincare!” rang through the palace, the
palace courts, the park, the city. The new Presi-
dent was escorted to the railway station by an
enthusiastic crowd. Another crowd welcomed him
on reaching Paris, and the retraites militaires —that
time-honored custom of soldiers returning to bar-
racks to the sound of the drum and the tambour, a
custom lately restored here in France —made their
retreat that night to cries in cadence of “Poincare a
I’Elysee, ton, tain; Poincare a l’Elysee, ton, taine!”
Very soon the cafes of Montmartre, the gay quarter of
Paris, were resounding with songs hastily impro-
vised.
Poincare is but 51 years of age, young compared
with the last two Presidents, Loubet and Fallieres.
And he means to be a more vigorous President than
his two predecessors. A man of character and
energy still in the prime of life, he will lead rather
than be led. “It will be a dictatorship,” say his
enemies.
A native of Lorraine, his birthplace is the little
town of Bar-le-duc. His parents belonged to what
is usually described as the upper middle class of
society; his father was an engineer charged with the
oversight of the roads and bridges of his country.
The deeply regretted Henri Poincare, perhaps the
greatest scientist of his day, was the new Presi-
dent’s first cousin. The President himself is a mem-
ber of the French Academy, and he has decided to
keep his name on the roll of barristers. His mother,
a proud and haopv woman to-day, is with him at his
Paris house, and will be with him at the Elvsee. He
is now no longer a practising Catholic, but he can-
not be said to be an irreligious man ; nor anti-
religious. And he is acknowledged by all parties to
be straight, upright, high-principled.

J. F. McEachran, a Glasgow-trained vet.,
formerly in N.S.W. Public Health Dept,
and latterly Government Vet. and Chief In-
spector of Cattle in S.A., goes to the North-
ern Territory as Chief Inspector of Stock.
At his instance most of the States have
agreed to hold a conference, at which he
will propose uniform regulations for the
registration of stallions, with the object of
gradually starving out the weeds.
Madigan, with whom Mawson begins the
list of the five he expects will have to
camp with him for another winter in the
Antarctic, is a youngster that the leader
picked up at Adelaide University. A while
before Mawson asked him if he would like
to join the expedition, this fathom-and-
more of young Australia had won a Rhodes
scholarship ; but the wires were pulled, and
the trustees agreed to hang it up until he
got back from the ice.
Serving aboard Maoriland’s “gift” Dread-
nought, now en route to the Loan Islands,
is a sub-lieutenant named Battenberg. Ilis
Christian name is merely George, but on
State occasions he plants Prince in front of
it. . Incidentally, he is the son and heir of
Prince Louis Alexander of Battenberg, a
vice-admiral and sea lord of Britain, and
personal A.D.C. to Queen Mary’s husband.
Young George was born in 1892, so that he
is barely of age. Like the ship he is serv-
ing on, he is understood to be pledged, but
in his case it is to a lady.
Judge Gordon got back to work on the
S.A. Supreme Court Bench last week, after
being away more than a year. First he
was on the Sugar Commission : since then he
has been on Kangaroo Island, recovering
from a bad breakdown in health. His son-
in-law, “Jim” Anderson, K.C., is now in
hospital, battling with a desperately serious
illness, and,, on behalf of the local Bar,
Paris Nesbit, Iv. C.—who was formerly
partner to both of them—mingled congratu-
tions to the one with sympathy for the
other.
“O.K.’s” recollections of Dealtin the
barrister:—
Re Alfred Deakin and the Curse of Deeming.
Deakin did more than “help to prepare the defence
of the wife-slayer,” as stated in a recent paragraph.
He appeared in court as counsel for the murderer,
and made an ornate speech in which Ancient Greece
and other matters that had nothing to do with the
case were introduced. Deakin’s notion of what to
say on behalf of an epileptic sex-maniac struck me
at the time as being profoundly stupid. Ever after-
wards I thought of him as an unforceful person —a
feeble advocate of an 3' cause he took in hand. It
was not so long after his poor little attempt to gain
notoriety at the Bar by defending a sensational mur-
derer that Deakin sold his law-books and gave
himself wholly to politics.
Major Hinton, Adelaide’s 100-year-old-
doctor, celebrated his centenary on
March 7, told his friends on that occasion
that he would be satisfied if he lived another
five years. He held a reception of veterans
of different wars, and chatted about fights
and generals of 50 years ago, as though they
had been incidents of yesterday. The old
warrior fractured his thigh some months ago.
amd cannot take his five-mile walk daily, and
that is a great trouble to him. Hinton is
the oldest, member of the Royal College of
Surgeons in the world, and retired from the
Bengal army with a pension as long ago as
1868.
From the Oldest Inhabitant !- —
The S. M. Herald's special correspondent, in an
article on the new capital, the site of which belonged
to Robert Campbell, who came to Sydney from Cal-
cutta in 1796 (not 1799 as stated), mentions that
this pioneer had four sons, “two of whom, John
a ?^„Charles, were members of the Legislative Coun-
cil.” The “special” makes no mention of the most
notable son, Robert, who represented the City of
Sydney in the first Parliament, and was Treasurer
in the first and second Cowper Ministries (the
second and fourth under Responsible Government).
This Campbell died in harness from overwork on
March 31, 1859. John renresented Sydney Hamlets
and the Glebe in the first three Parliaments. Charles
was not in the Assemblv, but sat in the Leg. Coun-
cil from 1870 to 1888." He died in Scotland. All
the sons were born at “the wharf.”
THE DICE OF DEATH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bathed in brilliant sunshine, St. Petersburg yes-
terday celebrated the tercentenary of the coming of
the Romanoff dynasty.
The streets were beautifully decorated
A lon g Procession, partly barbaric, and symbolical
ot the Russian Empire from its earliest days ac-
companied the Royal Family from the Winter Palace
to the Kazan Cathedral, the entire route being lined
by millions of cheering subjects.
The little Czarevitch was carried in the arms of
a giant Cossack.
At the end of the service the royalties returned to
the palace, once more accompanied by the rapturous
plaudits of their subjects.
—Cabled item of the week.
High on the air the banners swept their colors up-
ward to the sun;
The crash of music thrilled the hour; the shouting
crowds ; the booming gun ;
Over the arch the eagle proud ; the gleam of steel;
the prancing steed;
The walls of ancient streets and ways commemorate
of tingling deed.
The long procession threading through the rapturous
subjects of a Tsar—
This was a day of joy for him—the Ruler loved, the
Nation’s star ;
A March of Triumph for a Prince; a pageant for
a People’s King ;
The sharing of the Loving Cup; forgiving evil every-
thing.
Yet there came one with pallid lip, ringed round
with glow’ring men and armed,
Peering anon through Cossack ranks, furtive and
sad as one alarmed
Beyond repose. Nor once deceived by anthem swell
or rolling cheer.
Thus passed the fateful Tsar —the man who knew
that while there’s life there’s fear.
None knew so well, for close beside they carried
them his stricken son.
Before his eyes there rode the ghosts of Romanoffs
all foully done
By such as these that gaped, and cheered, and cursed
beneath a sharp-drawn breath —
This was no March of Joy to him : this was a game
of dice with Death!
Backward from Holy Church again, with Death
first loss upon the throw;
Once more the game of chance to play along that
open steel-framed row
That led to home, and fortress gates, and sullen
guns that hedge a Tsar
To hear the old dread words of Death: “You won,
but I shall not be far l"

THE SNAKE.
HUGGER-MUGGER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Loraine, principal boy of the “Puss in Boots”
pantomime, sold two guineas’ worth of kisses on
’Change, the proceeds of which went to the Scott
Memorial Fund.
The heroes die among the snow;
The fat man his respect to show
Invests a golden quid in kisses
From dimpled pantomimic misses.
On glory were the sailors set —
This the lip-service that they get!

For the first time for 33 years this bank has just
decided to increase its capital: not since 1880, when
it was brought up to £1,500,000, has any assault
been made on the shareholders’ pockets. In the
days of the boom, bank dividends ran up to 20 p.c. ;
but when people began to look more closely into the
figures of the cos. with which they traded, and State
banks and the like were talked of, prudent Aus:
tralian bank directors and managers set their faces
against running dividends up so high as to attract
hostile attention. If profits, grew, these wise men
decided that it was safer to let the shareholders
benefit by issuing to them shares either at par or
at. a very small premium. The Union and ’Asia,
being - directed from London, didn’t quite get the
drift of things, and pushed their dividends on past
10 p.c. Now, however, they have evidently seen the
wisdom of the other people’s policy ; and both Union
and ’Asia have announced fresh issues. The Union’s
little lot will consist of 20,000 shares, on which
£25 will be paid ; but, like all the other shares in
this bank, they will carry a liability of £5O, pay-
able, however, only on the winding-up of thebank.
And the winding-up of a bank whose reserves run
to nearly 11 mill'ons, protecting less than 17 mil-
lions of risks, seems a long way off. And it seems
still further off when it is remembered that for
years past the bank hgs been using large sums to
write down assets, and has, no doubt, accumulated a
very extensive internal reserve. Its premises in Aus-
tralia and Maoriland are valued now at only £392,293.
Fifteen years ago thev were valued at lust over
£600,000, and since then it is quite safe to say
that another £ 300,000 has been spent in new pro-
perties and additions to old ones. From this item
alone one would fudge that the position is stronger
even than it looks on paper.
And yet the issue of new shares is the right thin);
to do. Some figures in the following comparison will
help to drive in that point
Liabilities.
Aug.. 1911. Aug., 1912.
Notes .. .. £294,168 £188,949 Dec. £105,219
Hills .. .. 1,697,376 1,756,450 Inc. 59,074
Deposits .. 22,576,644 21,927,522 Dec. 649,122
biquid assets .. 10,605,996 bL *10,158,414 Dec. 447,582
Advances .. 16,454,910 16,302,674 Dec. 152,236
Premises .. 524,794 510,181 Dec. 14,613
The bank owes the public £23,872,921, and its own
visible funds amount to £2,993,349. Thus it has
nearly £8 of the public’s money at risk for every
£1 of its own; and that proportion is on the large
side—which, of course, points to the wisdom of
getting in more capital. Moreover, the bank
seems to be well able to distribute more
profit; last half-year it had £50,582 avail-
able to lay by after paying the 14 p.c. div.
The issue of new capital will kill two birds at one
shot; it will strengthen the bank’s position, and open
up a way for giving the shareholders a larger helping,
if the staff can get a larger helping, too, everything
in the garden will be lovely. The discontent of the
rank and file of the staff is the one smudge on a
fine bank. If the directors want proof of discon-
tent they should call for a return showing the num-
ber of men who have thrown up the service during
the last (say) three years.
There are no unexpected changes in the figures.
Deposits have shrunk a trifle, the natural result of a
tighter money market. Advances have kept fairly
steady; and the net movement is a shrinkage of
£447,582 in the liquid assets. Nevertheless the
liquid assets still represent 43 p.c. of all liabilities
to the public, and that is strong enough for any-
thing. In fact, it suggests that the bank, even
without the new money which will presently come
in from shareholders, is in a position to let out a
few more pounds when the right men come along
with the right securities.

This is the other sort of office. Owing mainly to
the success of the A.M.P., which had the good
fortune to be born just at the right time, Australia
for many years believed that there couldn’t be any
but a mutual office —anyway, not one worth looking
at. That the mutual system could have weaknesses
wasn’t dreamed of. Wasn’t the success of the
A.M.P. evidence enough? Didn’t its bonuses carry
conviction? Then other mutual offices came along,
and that was unfortunate, because it proved that
success wasn’t inherent in the mutual system. There
were some mutual offices which paid miserable
bonuses; worse still, there were one or two whose
figures aroused a suspicion as to whether they could
even pay the face value of their policies. And that
suspicion set people wondering. A mutual office has
no capital. Policyholders cut up among themselves
whatever there is to cut up, be it much or little.
If there isn’t enough to go round, it is with the
mutual office just as it has been with a good many
friendly societies—somebody must go short. No-
P u ts up any money by way of guaranteeing
anything. The people who founded this co. be-
lieved there was room for an office which would
guarantee something; and its remarkable success has
proved that they were right. Of course there were
considerations Having their own capital at
. take® they claimed that they would be more vigilant
in management than would otherwise reasonably be
the case. They were never foolish enough to attack
the mutual system root and branch ; all they claimed
certainly all they had a right to claim—was that
they were strengthening those points in a mutual
office where weakness might show itself.
For a good many years the Citizens’ office was
j ® s , fran ' { as it might have been. It seemed
afraid to stand by its principles, probably because
it knew that those principles would not be gener-
ally understood. In return for its guarantees, etc.,
it asks one-fifth of the surplus' or profits divided as
bonuses. The mutual office divides all the profits
among policyholders. This office said that mutu-
ality gives you no guarantee that you will get anv
profits at all, and that five-fifths of nothing is not
much. The M.L.C. sort of office certainly doesn’t
guarantee bonuses either; but it alleges that if the
people who run the (Concern are directly and finan-
cially interested in earning bonuses, bonuses are
more likely to be earned than would otherwise be
the So these people are entitled to take one-
fitth. But formerly that one-fiftli, if taken, wasn’t
distinguished from the bonuses paid to policy-
hohlers; and in the table on top of this it isn’t in-
cluded among the expenses. Lately separate figures
have been , available ; so if you add the shareholders’
bonus to the expenses things look like this:-

bonus and the retiring allowances, the exes, amount
"? 4s. 2d. p.c. of the total income. But if
the shareholders’ bonus is included in that way
it has to be remembered that the policyholder gets

not offer and doesn’t pretend to —a guarantee of

of the new-business premiums.
But, satisfactory as these figures are, there is a
serious doubt whether it is fair to charge the bonus
JJfi tO , working exes, in this unconditional wav.
Ihe whole theory of the thing is that more profits
are earned because profits are shared by the men
who run the business; and a revenue account can’t
show those profits. The actuary’s report does
that. In all, £199,135 will be divided amongf
policyholders this year, and that is 25.4 p.c. of last
year s total premium income—a most excellent re-
sult, especially in view of the fact that large sums
have been set aside to strengthen reserves. The
bonuses include those paid on account of old M L A
and Widows’ Fund policies. The interests of the
holders of those policies are looked after by two
separate lots of trustees. The Widows’ Fund trus-
tees (who include the former chairman and man-
ager) report: “The rate of bonus represents, on the
average, an increase of 100 p.c. over the rate of
bonus distributed bv the Widows’ Fund prior to the
union.” The old M.L.A. trustees report to similar
effect, and add a most enthusiastic appreciation of
the co.—of “the sound investment of its funds the
economical management of its business, and’ the
careful and capable administration of its affairs.”

Expenses (including interest on the guaranteeing
capital) run to about £39 15s. p.c. of the total
income; and considering the large proportion of
very small premiums which this society collects the
rate is satisfactory and much under the average
But it is poor business. Only two things can be
said m favor of it: but for it, thousands of people
wouldn’t have any insurance at all; and thousands
of others, having been got into the insuring habit
pass on to the much more desirable Ordinary branch
Last year in the Ordinary branch 12,573 policies
were issued, assuring £2,517,200. Thus the policies
averaged almost exactly £200; and when the £5OO
and £lOOO policies are remembered, it is pretty
plain that the society must have last year issued
a good many Ordinary policies for under £lOO
This system of giving the little man the benefit of
the much cheaper premiums in the Ordinary branch
is to be highly commended. And it makes the low
expense rate in that branch all the more creditable.
Finally, the newest branch—
Accident Branch.

Practically, however, this has nothing to do with
either Ordinary or Industrial policyholders; and it
may yet be found advisable to dissociate the two
businesses. Canada, for one, does not allow a life
insurance office to carry on the more speculative
Accident business, and there is a good deal to be
said in favor of that stand. The life policyholders
in this ■■ case have a right to ask that the capital
shall be kept wholly available for guaranteeing
their policies, and shall not be jeopardised in any
other business. Up to the present, certainly, it is
in no jeopardy at all; but nobody can say what the
future of the business may be.
As hitherto, the investments of the whole busi-
ness are satisfactory. Public securities represent
45.40 p.c., mortgages 43.12 p.c., and loans on poli-
cies 8.02 p.c. of the assets. Those three items
account for 96.54 p.c. of the assets, leaving only 3.46
p.c. to represent freeholds, leaseholds, furniture and
fittings (written down to nil) , outstanding pre-
miums and uninvested cash. The average earnings
for the past year were £4 12s. 3d. p.c.—consider-
ably more than the rate for recent years, owing no
doubt to the general shrinkage in the supply of
money, and all so much to the good of the policy-
holders when bonuses are being served out

This is one of the cos. in the Melbourne brewery
combine. That means that the Carlton and United
Breweries, Ltd., took over its business and shut up
its premises. In return, the Shamrock got shares
of a face value of £72,500 in the C. and U. 8., Ltd.
The difference between that amount and the value
of the plant, rolling stock and other tangible assets
transferred was put by the Shamrock Co. into an
Assets Realisation Account. In other words, a part
of that £72,500 was represented by nothing tangi-
ble, and to make the accounts balance, a reserve
was created on the other side. In the table, that
fund, being of course also intangible, hasn’t been in-
cluded with the reserves. As at December 31, 1911,
it amounted to £48,923. The amount has now been
cut into as follows: £BOOO has been transferred to
Profit and Loss account, £23,208 has been wisely
used to write down brewery and malt-house pro-
perty, and the balance ( £17,715) remains to the
credit of the account. The brewery and malt-house
now stand- in the books at» £16,000. The writing-
down is the proper thing, because, if the C. and
U. B. shares have any goodwill value, it springs from
the concentration of business and the closing-up of
superfluous beer-factories, with the consequent de-
tructiop of their value as beer-factories. The transfer
of any part of the paper item to the P. and L.
account is more questionable. But there was this
reason: the directors wanted to restore the Bs. 6d.
per share written off in 1897. and the other reserves
were a bit short of the necessary amount. With
the transfer, the capital becomes £78,525 instead
of £45,152 ; so it looks as though the shareholders won’t
get any more 10 p.c. divs. for a while. Still, that won’t
affect the hard cash distributed. For the past year
the revenue was £6514 from the combine, and
£lO6l from rents, interest, etc. ; and exes, absorbed
£568. In addition to the combine shares and the
brewery, the co., on December 31, had £4394 in
freehold pubs and £7687 in advances, deposits and
other assets. But the C. and U. B. lately issued
some 7 p.c. pref. shares, and as this co. took up its
proportion ( £7250 worth), it has, presumably, had
a word with its banker before this. And no doubt
he was affable, for on December 31 the co.’s total
liabilities were only £855.

The Labor party ought to get this co.’s annual
accounts republished and send a copy to every poli-
tician and alderman in the country, along with a
little dissertation on the advantages of doing things
for yourself. The Railway Commissioner who can’t
see the advantage of having his own coal mine
should also be presented with a copy. Wade, V.C.,
should get a special note reminding him that the
rule applies to bricks as well as beer. The position
was just this. Nine years ago various hotel-keepers
who poured a sea of beer into the mouth of Mel-
bourne decided that it would be good business to hr
their own brewer. Beer-making under average con-
ditions is good business ; but they figured it out that
it should be extra-good in this case, seeing that they
wouldn’t have to chase themselves at great expense
to get their own custom, and pursue themselves at
further great expense to collect the money from
themselves, and write off large sums for bad debts
through failing to pay themselves what they owed
themselves. Things turned out just as they figured.
As soon as a start had been made, other people
wanted to come in, and they were let in on payment
of a stiff nremium. As the business grew, and more
money was wanted, more shares were issued at pre-
miums that grew no smaller. The first lot of shares
issued consisted of 22,525 of 10s., subsequently cut
up into 45,050 of ss. There are now 108,082. and
the premiums have totalled £71,086, so the 63,032
issued since the first lot have cost on an average
275. 6d. apiece. The 21,476 sent to market last
year averaged over 30s. On that lot, of course, the
100 p.c. div. is really 16 p.c. Existing shareholders
had the sole right to apply for these shares. The
report says that nearly all those offered were taken.
This paper doesn’t doubt it.

Bonus on pref. shares, £4377 .. .. 22,926
£20,000 transferred to capital .. .. 2,926
1912.. 21,152 230 10 p.c. = 10,000 10,922 131848
This is another concern whose historv ought to be
r.wd and digested by the public men of the country.
The business was run for many years by the Aus-
tralian Coffee Palace Co., of Melbourne; and it
wasn’t a brilliant success. The premises cost the
co. something over £138,000. To relieve itself
somewhat this co. helped a new co. into life, and
this new co. took the premises over at £lOO,OOO.
But as it didn’t have £lOO,OOO, and needed some
till-monev, it had to take a mortgage. The very
capable men who were at the back of things de-
cided that they would get the money cheaper if
they undertook to forego a solid amount of profits
mid apply the money to the reduction of the debt.
They, therefore, bound the co. not to pay more than
6 p.c. until the place was free. Business improved
under good financial control. Then, having money
in hand, the directors decided to invest a bit of it
in the Australia Hotel, whose ownership and con-
trol were just being transferred to this side. Out of
that deal the co. made over £45,000 —-something
like 110 p.c.—in about 12 months. These three
things explain the recent bonuses: (1) The taking
over of the valuable freehold at a low price; (2)
the foregoing of dividends in the early days and the
paying-off of debt out of profits; and (3) the fine
deal in Australia shares. One other transaction
calls for a word. To raise a necessary £17,000
from outside at the beginning, the promoters had to
offer a 6 p.c. preference. About a year ago an offer
was made to the pref. shareholders to buy them
out at a small premium. The table shows £4377
paid as a bonus on pref. shares. That reallv is the
premium paid by the co. to get rid of the pref.
capital; and the £230 of pref. dividend paid dur-
ing the year was the small amount of interest due
to March, 1912, when the capital was repaid. There
are now, therefore, only ordinary (“B”) shares.
On December 31 these represented £lOO,OOO of
capital; and another £20,000 is now added from
reserves. The business has been very profitable
during the year; but the figures are no doubt ab-
normal. Australia has been enjoying probably un-
precedented prosperity—a lot of it artificial, the
result of furious borrowing—and a travellers’ hotel,
such as the Metropole, gains as much from a burst
of national extravagance as almost anything. The
figures are. therefore, practically certain to be far
less favorable presently. The cheerful thing in the
shareholders’ eyes is, no doubt, that the profits can
wilt a lot before the 10 p.c. div. js- in jeopardy.

been £8000; probably it was the latter. In other
words, without a reduction of capital, the original
shareholders got 15s. or 16s. of their 20s. back. They
might have capitalised their reserves, and made their
capital £20,000 instead of the old £10,000; but
they preferred the cash. And they will probably be
sorry, because, though the provisions of the Gas Act
as to standard charges and dividend don’t apply to
this co. (unless it has been very lately
added to the schedule to the original mea-
sure), there is no doubt that it would
not at any time have been put on a worse footing
than the cos. named in the Act. That is to say, it
could have been sure of being allowed to pay 10
p.c. on £20,000 if it brought up its capital to that
amount. Now it is probable that if it increases its
capital beyond £lO,OOO, unless it does it very
promptly, it won’t be allowed to draw more than
6 p.c. on the excess.

In 1910 this co. bought out the little local electric
light concern; it paid £6500 for the outfit, and
had to go to the bank for most of the money. Two
years ago the overdraft was £4971 ; but the direc-
tors have kept their nose to the pick, and the debt
has been reduced to £BO4 —a very satisfactory per-
formance indeed. Considerable amounts, it will be
seen, have been written off against depreciation.
This is in addition to money spent on maintenance.
The future of the co. depends, of course, entirely on
the goldfield; and local mining prospects are not
bright. In fact, except in one or two patches, they
are very gloomy. Stock in this co., therefore, isn’t
for a man who wants to sleep on his investments.
* * * *
An unostentatious Sydney “float” is the
Coastal Tugs Co., whose leading spirits appear to
be directors or servants of Allen Taylor and Co.,
the North Coast S.N. Co., and other firms interested
in the Northern River (N.S.W.) trade. This trade
is depending on the services of tugs which are State
subsidised to the extent of £BOOO a year. The
tugs also, of course, earn towage money. In re-
turn for its subsidy the Government does not get 6d.
directly or indirectly. It seems that the timber
people are dissatisfied with the service provided,
and also don’t see why the rich subsidy should go to
outsiders. Hence they have put up £lO,OOO to buy
a few tugs which will cut in for the Government
contracts. Then the people alreadv in the trade will
control the tugs, pocket the subsidies, plus the tow-
ages, and have a still firmer grip on the North
Coast business. It is a good move, and the wonder
is that it was not put in train long ago. Still it is
a greater wonder that the Government doesn’t run
its own tugs instead of heavily subsidising some-
body else’s.
* * * *
The war over the picture shows, in which
a heap of public money is now invested, or specu-
lated, grows keener. The co-operative movement of
the leading city showmen has been naturally fol-
lowed bv a similar action among the suburban
ditto There is a scheme afoot in Melbourne for
the importation of vaudeville artists who will appear
in conjunction with a cinematograph entertainment
at a number of picture theatres—three or four a
night—drawing their salary from the management
of the circuit. This “combined-show” scheme is
onlv in the negotiation stage at present, but the
Co-onerative Film Exchange Co., Dtd., is already
on the market. This co. will cater for showmen
(outside the other combine) in Victoria and South
Aus. Capital £30.000, in 60.000 shares of 10s
each, of which 40,000 are offered .to independent
showmen and the public. Profit available after pay-
ment of a 10 p.c. div. is to go towards allowing
discount up to 10 P.c. to the film-hirers, so the show-
man shareholder will get a bit both wavs if the
business nourishes. And it is for the showmen to
make the business flourish. The prospectus is not
unreasonable in estimating a profit of £2500 a year
for shareholders over and above a discount of £1950
a vear allowed to film-hirers. Nor is the promoter
unreasonable. He wants 10.000 paid shares which he
can deal with as he likes in promoting and procur-
ing shareholders. The other 10.000 will be held in
reserve. „ *

* * # *
Y Water Extended, Emmaville (N.S.W.),
operating on the Graveyard deep lead, has suspended
work at an interesting stage. A shaft sunk in the
middle of the pronerty struck a heavy flow of water
in the drift underlying the basalt. This, though a
favorable indication for good wash values under-
neath, was too much for the small pumping plant.
Funds also ran out, but a £2 call has just been
made. With good management and a reasonable
amount of capital this property still has a very
good sporting chance.
* * *
Grafton Copper Co. (N.S.W.) is jubilant
over a make of ore passed through in “A” level
crosscut. Not enough work has, however, been done
to prove its value to the mine. Some addition to
the reserves is very badly needed, as development
work is very far behind-hand. The new find may
help the co. over a critical period; but at
present its value is extremely problematical, as the
occurrences of ore in the lower level are erratic, and
show a nasty tendency to pinch out unexpectedly.
* * **• *
At the Vegetable Creek tin mine,.Emma-
ville (N.S.W.), the principal feature is the crosscut
from Allen’s shaft. This is just at the moment hung
tip, pending the installation of a more powerful
boiler. Water is making freely in the face of the
crosscut, and a very little more work will show the
value of the gutter at this point. At the Rock
shaft and Chandler’s shaft things are drab. The
former serves to keep the pot boiling, with the aid
of an occasional call. The latter has been most dis-
appointing throughout, extremely rich wash being
struck from time to time, but never an extensive
body. A good hole of wash at Allen’s shaft would
give the co. a lift that it is almost desperately fn~
need of.

The cloud of witnesses in the B. H. Junc-
tion-Junction North Equity case has disnersed, and
now Justice Rich is to have his say. But no matter
what he says it won’t end the cat-fight. In view of
the heavy expense it was at an early stage suggested
that the Junction North should buv its neighbor out;
but the Junction North neoule have a much poorer
opinion of the Junction than the Junction people
have. In fact, their opinion of the show is even
poorer than The Bulletin's.
Chairman Herman Landau was not so
bullish as he was expected to be at
the Associated Northern meeting. With the
air of a man who had missed the market,
he said that some time must elapse before
it would be possible to tell whether the co. had
in the Victorious leases at Ora Banda a really large,
permanent property, and added: “I do not wish you
to run away with the idea that we have discovered
a Bank of England.” A dividend, he said, might
be expected this month or next. The Associated
Northern block at Kalgoorlie was terselv alluded to
as an almost worked-out property which had pro-
duced £1,649,042, paid close upon £BOO,OOO in
dividends, and provided a considerable amount of
cash to acquire and equip new shows and for invest-
ment in gilt-edged securities. As regards the co.’s
Mexican venture, Landau regretted to say that it
was in a state of “suspended animation,” and that
goings-on in its vicinity were still exciting.
Gimlet, at Ora Banda (W.A.). is again in
the market. Oroya Links had it under option for
a time, but after' spending a few thousands in hard
country, that co. came to the conclusion that for
a concern with over a million shares the show was
a bit on the small side, and regretfully withdrew.
Later on the late-lamented Gwalia South came
along, but left without making a deal. Little or
nothing was then heard about the property until a
few weeks ago, when a special report on it by the
State mining engineer was issued. Since then a
likelv buyer from London has been buttonholed, and
in the event of help not coming from this quarter
an eastern flotation will prohahlv be attempted. In
that case the most will no doubt be made of recent
develonments in the mile-awav Victorious leases
worked by the Associated Northern. #

Toora Tin (Vic.) is being introduced to
London by a leaflet, which says little but means a
lot. Capital is £ 75,000 in ss. shares, which are
started on the market at a premium of 25 p.c.
London prying people want to be told why on
earth the biggest sluice-pump in existence is being
put on a mine which does not tell its illustrious
ancestry in the financial puff-rags. Tom Birkbeck
and W. A. Mercer appear to be poling the mine in
Victoria, and, of course, one of the Robbies has a
hand in the float. How isn’t clear, but he is put
down as a vendor to take £4500 cash and £22,000
in shares. Mercer then trips in to get £28,000
cash, but there appears to be some sort of an
arrangement by which two London d'rectors each
take £9333 from Robbie and Mercer. Totting figures
up. the vendors’ cut runs into £32,500 cash and
£22,000 in shares. This is E. and O. E. If with-
out error, the vendors’ cut out of £75,000 is on
the big side. Wads of scrip go thus: 199,200 to W.
A. Mercer, 30,595 to H. F. Lockyer, 18,000 to J. P.
Anderson, 10,000 to A. P. Ash and 6000 to G. C.
Lush. This Gippsland venture is being handled by
the people who made the recent Oxide deal. Hence,
no doubt, Captain Richard's visit.
» * * *
Cain was raised at Bendigo the other day
because one of the brokers got in early and cheaply
over a parcel of Derby United shares. As he had
no competitor to face when tendering, he was allotted
the scrip. Very shortly afterwards the stock rose
100 p.c. on a mine development. Shareholders didn’t
quarrel with the way of things, but the Stock Ex-
change started to piay a hand. There was talk of
knocking the stock off the list for three months, as
if that would stop the public from buying and sell-
ing if it wished to indulge. The end was smoke,
and very thin at that, because the sensible men
wanted to know why any sane man should pick up
shares if he didn’t fancy there was a rise ahead.
Another London group is on the hunt in
Gippsland. This is the Mines Assets Purchase Co.,
which has for its head young mining engineer
Dyason. He has been out after the old Maude and
Homeward Bound mine, Glen Wills, which for so
long was one of the mainstays of Alec Peacock’s
office. Finally it drifted into the liquidator’s hands.
The resurrection is to take the place of a 50,000 share
co. Vendors and the London concern grab 20,000,
leaving 30,000 shares to be issued among shareholders
of the Mines Assets Co. as paid up to 15s. for Is. 6d.
cash —which will provide mighty little capital to go
on with. As to the Maude’s future it may be
remarked that the mine was a “stringer” for years,
but it had a crowd in it that didn’t care much for
calls, preferring to get their bit out of tributors.
Hansel Monday, alias the Great Chaf-
finch, is to be helped by £2500 from the Vic. Mines
Department. The co. (which started so sensationally
at Southern Cross, and then shifted to Bendigo) gets
the money conditionally on its pushing down the
shaft below where it stopped when Rutter Clarke
shut down on his development work on the Carshal-
ton line. The shaft is down 400 ft., so a fair start
has been made with the deep sinking work that Ben-
digo loves. The co. has about £lO,OOO in its pocket
and went to Bendigo because of some sort of promise
that it would get a subsidy. But Peter Mcßride
jibbed at fulfilling the half-promise, because he
didn’t think mining grants were intended for people
with funds. This policy nearly drove the Chaffinch
crowd out of Bendigo, which wanted the side line
tested at someone else’s expense. So a little come-
’ither has been used, and the foreigner gets his bribe
of £2500 to stop. Good for Bendigo—and good,
perhaps, for the Chaffinch. # #
The Cooktown (Q.) people, especially the
old-timers, hang on to their faith in the Maytown
reefs. They have raised £3ooo' as the nucleus of a fund
to re-open and try the Ida and Just in Time line ;
and the Government has promised £4500 towards
the same desirable object. The annual loss of in-
terest on the Cooktown-Laura railway is reckoned
to more than justify this Government outlay. A
co. is to be formed and a prospectus issued shortly.
John Darling, of S. Aus., who bought the
residuum of Stannary Hills, is punching away at the
Dead Finish. The Finish lode is, with the excep-
tion of the Lass o’ Gowrie, the best-defined in the
district. In fact, those two are the only tin lodes
opened, so far, that make any pretence to have
decent walls and length. The Lass also is lifting
her somewhat diminished head, as 60 tons of ore,
valued at 50 p.c., were recently shipped to the
smelters. * » *

In spite of the tin boom and the mine s
reported improvements, the Vulcan (Q.) makes a
poor showing for the half-year to Feb. 10. The
financial result was a loss of £808. For this the
heavy battery charges are largely responsible: 3920
tons went through the Irvinebank Co.’s mill
at a cost of £3791, including £3239 for crushing
alone. The return was only £9936. Nor do the
directors say anything very cheerful about the look of
things underground at any point. Values nearly
everywhere got poorer as the half-year’s work went
on. ' The co. has £4675 of cash in hand, and with
that it will poke about.
In 1912 the Transvaal gold mines pro-
duced 9,124,299 fine oz. valued at £38,757,560, lick-
ing 1911 (the previous best year) by nearly a
million oz. in weight and 3J millions in value.

Sydney, March 10. 1913.
My Dear Arini.—
The Chelmsfords have departed in a flood
of fulsome flattery. Lord Chelmsford has
been described as a kind of super-man, a
being of so great and lofty a .mind that he
must have blushed at the god-like attributes
ascribed to him in sundry after-dinner
speeches—if a Conservative peer can blush
to hear that he is more than mortal.
Mrs. Cocks, Sydney’s Mayoress, gave a
farewell tea-party to Lady Chelmsford. The
latter looked worn, and mildly weary of the
last fortnight’s adulation, but was, other-
wise, in a gentle, lamb-like frame of mind
at Friday’s crush. The Town Hall nest set
aside for the Mayoress and her feminine
mends and acquaintances, and the bun of
peace, was filled to the doormat. Aide
de Moleyns leaned up against the mantel-
piece and suffered the attentions of various
feminine admirers with cheerful resignation,
it was one of his final appearances, and he
beamed on the company over the fence of a
tour-inch collar.

the hurried look of having been hastily
dragged away from moth-balls to meet the
exigencies of a sudden cold snap. Many of
the best frocks were taking their final sum-

tace to its eyelashes.

or the other side. Lady Scott’s calm man-
ner, and the absence of any outward exhibi-
tion of emotion, is the visible sign of a self-
restrained nature burying its bruised feelings

Lady Macgregor, wife of Queensland’s

a trip to Europe.

iu.ij.A, m IN. S. Wales, passed out after a

Ex-Speaker Maurice O’Rorke (M.L.) is
packing up his kit for the Old Land. He
is a fine, hearty old gentleman who has left a
big mark on M.L. affairs, as a Speaker who
spoke With ;-authortty — nr- HU [jiMiuul
perch, and with ease and grace on the hust-
ings. A knighthood is stowed away in his
locker. He goes to England mostly to see
his son, the well-known Teddy O’Rorke,
whose tame as an amateur jockey and cross-
country rider emerged triumphant out of an
occasional eclipse of bandages and sticking-
plaster. Teddy married one of the wealthy
Rhodes girls, of Christchurch, and is now
following the affluent occupation of fox-
hunting in England.
A Sydney girl who has been gadding in the
whirl ol Hobart dissipation, writes an infor-
mative line re the results. Items: stiff
joints from playing tennis most days and
dancing most nights, and an alarming crop
ot freckles on nose and arms.
One of Hobart’s nicest maidens, Miss Jean
(jribson, chipped her shell and came out in a
whirl of white satin during the recent
cyclone of gaiety in Tassy’s Isle. This blithe
young person went to eight dances in 10
?u yS iG playe , d l n a girls ’ cri cket match against

Pianist Laurence Godfrey Smith is just
back after 21 months scamper up and down
the face of the earth. He still wears his hair
short, and shows no signs of having grown
10 minutes older or fatter since he left Syd-
ncy* 1 Q Vienna he raked up reminiscences
with his old teacher, the famous Leschetizky
who i. I s now 85 years young. The
sprightly veteran recently married a
handsome young wife. She’s a Pole, and is
Mrs. Leschetmky !y. 0 r V. Laurence G.
bmith found the Jack London ranch (Countv
Sonoma) so pleasant that he stayed on till
a cruise in the Londons’ yacht finished a
sparkling American incident.
Theßoamer’s sleeping accommodation was
limited. So the skipper and the crew (who
were Jack London and two Japs, and the
bosn and general-utility Mrs. Jack) and
tffe ballast (which was Laurence Godfrey)
packed themselves away like tinned sar-
dines. _ When the ballast had crawled warily
down into its place in the cockpit, one of the
Japs, peered over the edge to see if it was
firmly screwed up in its camp. If it was, then
he carefully dropped the bedclothes on it
Eaurence swears he never slept better in his
life than in that mummy-like niche in the
Roamer, where he had no room to stir a
nnger, and no space for a single snore. In
the morning, the amiable heathen hung over
his recumbent form, and switched off the
blankets. Then the ballast struggled up to
the surface and had its daily shave.

F. S. Kelly, the sleek, black-haired young
Sydney man who can afford to give piano
recitals as a hobby, has a cosy crib on the
Thames shore, where he often shelters wan-
dering Australian music-makers. Last year
his recitals in London happened to coincide
with the Great Strike. In consequence, their
success was not of the crowded-treasury
order. F. Sep., who is one of the wealthy
“Tin” Kelly family, to which little Willie
also belongs, took a serene view of the frost,
and told a Sydney friend that his manager
had said: “Concerts are the first luxury the
public always give up on any reasonable
excuse.” As a musician, he has risen
superior to his handicap of independent
means, and counts as a pianist even in the
City of Gog and Magog.
The municipal claw is still reaching out
for huge rents for the Town Hall on holi-
days. N. J. Gehde pays £ll5 for his luck
in being the successful tenderer for the edi-
fice on Good Friday night. Rent of organ
(nicely furnished with stops), attendance,
artists, etc., are extras. With fine weather
and another streak of luck, the greatly-dar-
ing Gehde ought to clear at least ss. for his
trouble.
Mrs. Mayoress Cocks sat firmly at the
top end of last week’s meeting of the Bush
Book Club. Funds are in a precarious con-
dition, and a dash for £3OO to save the life
of the society will shake up the livers of
those who pledged themselves to pursue that
sum to the uttermost farthing.
Rosina Lion, M.A., who did herself credit
at the Sydney Uni. a while ago, is said to be
■edgaged to an Adelaide man. The learned
Rosina returned to Sydney a few weeks ago.
She’s a relative of one Prof. Bergsohn, whose
lectures on Plato and other heavyweight
ancients once stirred British duchesses to a
brief revolt against the Elinor Glyn school
of literature.
The Ewan Frasers, formerly of this city,
where the late pere Fraser built the family
fortune, are now living in Sussex. Their
palatial nest is said to include everything
necessary to the country-squire business.
The grounds are a landscape gardener’s best
dream, and an ornamental lake laps the edge
of velvet lawns.
Mr. John Marshall, one of Auckland’s
most eligible bachelors, has been winding-up
a tour of the world by climbing all the best-
looking scenery on the Blue Mountains. He
was chaperoned by a married sister, Mrs.
Macdonald, whose daughter was also in the
party. The triplet return to the Dominion
this week.
Politicians of all sizes are fluttering about
like bats. Dr. Buck, a clever Maori half-
caste who represents a native constituency
in M.L. Parliament, is expected in Sydney
some time in the near future. He’s a fine
sample of the educated Maori type.
Mrs. M. Forrest, the Queensland writer,
has been giving her hard-worked pen a rest
in the dull haven of Sick Bay. Brain fag
and other tribulations recently hurried her
off to a private hospital, whence she is creep-
ing slowly back along the road to recovery.
The Hattricks are over from Maoriland.
Their name is indelibly imprinted on the
Wanganui River, where they have a solid
stake in the tourist transport business.
Imperial Trade Commissioner Sir Alfred
Bateman, who has been vigorously prodding
the M.L. lOU Department, must be bulging
with statistics. Ever since he cut his first
whiskers he’s been sitting or jumping on
loss and profit accounts. A few headlines
from the story of his career ought to strike
terror to any witness who tries to dodge the
Bateman note of interrogation: Treasurer
of International Statistical Institute; Sec.
to International Sugar Conferences: Presi-
dent Royal Statistical Society; responsible
for Fiscal Blue Book; late Comptroller-
General for Commerce, Labor and Statistics
at the Board of Trade. There’s about half
a column more to his credit. In his spare
moments, when he isn’t rounding up tariffs,
or looking for a leak in some commercial
intelligence department, he plays golf.
Mr. Alfred Brown, a confirmed Quaker
from Bull’s Island, is hunting for flaws in
Australia’s Compulsory Training Act. The
local Peace Society fell upon the neck of
the non-combative Brown the other day, and
gathered him into its first 1913 meeting.
The man of peace _ expounded the beautiful
theory that Right is Right, and gunpowder
a snare and delusion, with a bad smell to
it. Might is still above ground out Janina
way, but the Hague Conference in May is
going to have another hack at the monster.
Singer Emilie Lambert writes from Lon-
don that she is due in Sydney on April 4
and remarks with a sigh that she hasn’t seen
the sun for weeks. When it did peer
through the gloom it was a dull round disc
like a chemists’ shop-light hung in the fog.
Somehow, a Londoner on his native climate
is reminiscent of “You ought ter’ve been
here last week.” When the Australian
complained of the behavior of last English
summer, the answer was, “Oh, but it’s the
wettest we’ve had for years. You ought to
have been here last June.” The Januarv
togs they dismissed as “the worst for ages.”

Mr. Marcus Clark has acquired the beauti-
ful old home on Mount Wilson that used to
belong (I fancy) to J. D. Cox. It stands in
velvet-grass plots behind a screen of chest-
nut trees. The glory of that redsoil patch
the tall tree-fern, makes a leafy vista of
feathery fronds. The bush horse up that wav
is still trying to diagnose the Marcus Clark
motor, which it doesn’t understand, and
objects to with its hind legs.
The fine racing yacht Thelma, which was
smashed up in Saturday’s wild storm, was
the property of Mr. C. Lloyd Jones. Mr.
W alter Moore, who was swept away when
the Thelma staggered under green seas off
Middle Head, was a well-known and enthusi-
astic sharer in Sydney’s great white-winged
gambols. The owner and the rest of the yacht’s
crew were saved from a trip to Davy Jones’s
locker by the prompt ossistance of the pilot
boat Barundah.
An Australian in London writes a line on
the Wattle epidemic that scattered tufts of
these yellow velvet blooms through wintry
streets, and whiffs of subtle fragrance across
the tables of innumerable restaurants: “Lon-
doners called January 27 Australia’s birth-
day, and many we met were so trimmed up
with sprigs of wattle (they generally call it
mimosa) that we quite expected staid
London policemen would arrest them for dis-
orderly conduct. .. .They eyed us with a
kind of bovine wonder as we crossed Oxford-
street. We were making fairly heavy weather
under armsful of wattle, and might have been
Suffragettes on the war trail (everything a
little out of the way here is suspected of
being a Suffragette conspiracy), with infer-
nal machines stowed away under innocent-
looking yellow blossoms. As there was a
letter-box just ahead, we ostentatiously
avoided it. No London policeman can re-
main calm when he sees a group of women
hovering near a pillar-box these militant
days.”
Fashions shown at the autumn displays
revive a drooping faith in the sairty of our
sex, for the big hat appears to be t< mporarily
dead. Small, sensible shapes that really
bear. some kind of relationship to the human
cranium which they are to cover bloom
mellowly on all the stands. “Mellowly"
means that curry tint. One sees it every-
where. When curry grows cold in the plate
a kind of yellowish green transfigures it.
That is the color I’m trying to get at the
description of. In frocks it is attained by-
placing a robe of yellow satin under a skirt
of green ninon. They do say that purple is
the ruling shade, and, of course, yellow is its
complement. It figures just now mostly in
the shape of yellow roses on white hats.
They are the craze of the month. The new
hats are of felt and velvet and soft fluffy fur.
On a very warm evening last week I saw a
woman with a fur c"n on, and yesterday
(Sunday) brought out many felts. There
was a pretty (though unseasonable) brown
shape turned up one side—they all are —and
finished with a feather duster. It really was
a duster of the sixpenny sort, and just that
golden brown shade. But it looked too sweet
for words.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

Here we have two books for the price of one, and both are equally
good. “The Butterfly Man,’’ indeed, ranks amongst one of the
cleverest things of its kind, and enhanced this author’s reputation

Essentially a book for young men, sisters and mothers, this work
draws such a pen picture of a selfish society butterfly that most people

The young man’s awakening is swift, severe and uncompromising;

one hoped for.
“The Alternative” is a delightful story in MeCutcheon’s best style,
and must be read to be appreciated.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 10, 1913.
My Dear Yarrxe, —
Australian Cup Day struggled through
between splashing thunder drops and fearful
intervals of steam-bath. The Denman lady
had draped herself in a wonderful sheath of
shimmering sea-greenery tone. Unfortu-
nately, one of the nearest and dearest of her
household appeared wrapped in the same
color, and planked herself down beside her
aggrieved Excellency for the rest of the day.
The Fuller lady had borrowed hub’s limp for
the occasion. Perhaps it was just the
weather; anyhow, she moved with a distinct
dot-and-carry-one gait.
The Flemington Autumn rally finished up
on Saturday in furs. The Denman lady was
cosily swathed in the folds of a tail-less
ermine coat. An enormous lancer plume
fluttered from her black hat. The Fuller
dame had a' neat navy tailor-made under her
black sable coat. A strawberry pink hat and
pheasant feather made a top-dressing. The
two house-parties foregathered informally,
and whispered “moral certainties” to one
another. Most of the Helps and both Ex-
cellencies appeared in black boxers ; the sole
exception, Barttelot, was under a straw
boater. He had to hold on to the brim most of
the afternoon. Justice Barton strutted the
lawn with both Excellencies. Alf Dunlop,
tennis champion, looked almost heroic with
one bandaged wrist thrust into the bosom of
his coat in the St. Helena pose. Mrs. Alf,
in sables and mauve shot silk, did her best to
look like Josephine. Oscar Asche wore a
crimson buttonhole and a boxer hat one size
bigger than a thimble. Lily’s incomparable
nose was occasionally visible between the
wings of an immense hat. Lilian Birtles was
supported by Mr. Wiltshire, her fearfully
pale husband. There was an end-of-the-
season gush of cordiality all round, and lots
of people who are hurrying off to Europe this
month were kept busy good-bye.
By Friday the polo tournament had
reached white heat, and the players were so
engrossed that they could not spare time for
the usual chat with the girls, who wasted
their finery on the Antarctic blast and grew
blue-nosed to no purpose. The large number
of competing teams confused most newcom-
ers, and to them at least the day was a
series of blurred impressions of red-silk
jerseys, white sweaters, and the decided
stripes of Colac and Campex-down. An un-
broken ring of motors poked inquisitive
headlamps over the flagged line, and the Very
Best People, cosily snuggled inside amongst
the rugs, gossiped at top speed. Several
girls rode to the tournament. One pretty,
red-haired flapper was on a big chestnut, and
talked down from mid-air to spectacled Dr.
Springthorpe.
The Barttelot lady, in white cloth and
ermine, came out in a small, closed, plum-
colored car with Mrs. Franklyn. The Ful-
lers gave the game besf very early in the
afternoon, and whirled homeward in the
mustard car. A contingent of the children,
with fair hair blowing over their warm, red
coats, stayed behind in the care of Mrs.
Grimwade. The Staughton widow talked
politics earnestly most of the time. She
looks very well again after her illness. Mrs.
Everard Browne sat in a lawn chair as near
to the playing area as possible. A pretty
sister of the new Mrs. Charlie .Wallace came
down from Camperdown in a tricky blue
bonnet-hat. Manifolds, Chirnsides and other
Western District womenkind tucked their
feathers into shelter, while the men sat in
rows on the grass and used their
glasses. Mrs. Jim Purves showed off
Mrs. Rolls. a visiting sister from
Westralia. Captain Nutting, mounted on a
fresh black pony that see-sawed like a rock-
ing horse, acted as Umpire-General. The
motor-cars began to back away in shamefaced
fashion before play ended. The players
were the only ones who had managed to keep
warm; most of the watchers were frozen.
On Monday evening the Denmans gave a
sporting dinner, at which polo talk fairly
raged. The feeding was followed by an im-
promptu dance. At the dinner the
Macgregor lady, wife of the Bananaland
Excellency, who happened to be passing
through Melbourne, was guest of honor. The
big grey house sheltered mother, daughter
and aide Foxton for the night. Next day
the trio continued their journey northward.
Only half the ballroom at Federal Govern-
ment House was used for the dance. It was
a sweltering night, and the little G.-G. wore
a hot flush that went very well with his
gorgeous uniform. Her Ex. looked cool in
an airy lace garment, weighted with ropes of
pearls and a few odd diamonds. The fair
Fuller lady was in shades of wine red, re-
lieved with the family diamonds. The Mac-
gregor dame was unable to be present; but
the Macgregor daughter, a pretty girl in a
white frock, attended, and was in great de-
mand. The Whitburgh widow had her white
hair built up against a black background.
Daughter Sybil wore a rather creased ap-
pearance. Little Mrs. Arden Franklyn,
arrayed in the color of the droopy vioiet,
was surrounded by a mass of Helps, amongst
them Foxton, of Bananaland. The Bartte-
lot lady was inside a black dress that scin-
tillated. As regards chiffons, Ernest Bruce’s
wife bravely held her own, whilst John
Manifold’s wife looked suitably expensive.
Betty Moffatt’s mamma, in violet purple,
startled beholders at first glance; but closer
inspection revealed a barely distinguishable
covering of flesh pink. It is the latest craze,
and we shall get used to it. Dancing ceased
an hour after midnight, and all hands swept
off to a stand-up supper in the dining-room,
managed by the eagle-eyed Nevill, who kept
the cold duck in its place, and overawed the
trifle so that it remained on the plate.
Among the diners at Federal Government
House on Monday night were the Whitburgh
lady and daughter Sybil Bortliwick. They
are on their way to Maoriland. Algernon
Borthwiclc, the present Whitburgh’s brother,
camped with his wife at Mandeville Hall,
Toorak, for two years, and went on to
Maoriland in November last. They will golf
■ again hereabouts during the coming winter.
The Andy Fishers intend to hand sister
Christina to bridegroom-elect -Edwards on
March 19; The Prime Minister’s best front-
room will be used for the ceremony, which
is to be shorn of all fuss and frill. The
Irwin sisters have been inseparables for
years, and Andy’s roof has sheltered both
since Margaret became Mrs. Andy. As Mr.
Edwards’s hobby is sailing his own canoe,
the new home will be pitched as near as pos-
sible to the Albert Park duckpond.
The sun didn’t shine on the wedding of
Stella Macßobertson and Bert Brewer at
Scots’ Church. ,In fact, rain was falling when
the bride approached on the arm of her proud
parent, so the cinematograph picture ordered
for the occasion is likely to be somewhat
blurred. Papa gave the photographer a
chance by pausing for a moment in the
camera’s line of fire, but the elements showed
no consideration for his feelings. Admission
to the show was by ticket only, and a full
house included a number of comely lolly-girls
from the Macßobertson factory. These dam-
sels, in their ehthusiasm, plucked souvenir
blossoms from the bride’s bouquet as she
marched to the altar, and, presumably, took
their trophies home to put under their pil-
lows. The pretty little heroine was in the con-
ventional white trappings, with pink under-
skirt, whilst five bridesmaids in lettuce green
and mauve needed only a few raindrops to
complete the salad effect. Mrs. Macßobert-
son was a conspicuous success in. grey, and
the folks in the foreground made a harmoni-
ous color scheme. After the ceremony, the
party taxied to the Macßobertson home at
Fairfield, in the grounds whereof is the
bride’s future residence —a wedding present
from papa. The breakfast and subsequent
dance provided entertainment for some 300
guests, who mostly felt the blood of their
braw ancestors coursing madly through their
veins when the Scottish pipers arrived at
nine o’clock to serenade the bonnie, bonnie
lassie lately converted into Mrs. Brewer.
Potential heiress Isabel Horsfall has made
a Scotchman named Brian Lippe happy. The
two met in Maoriland, and fell in love at
first sight. The girl hurried home to break
the glad news, and the man sped to Scot-
land to set his affairs in order prior to laying
them before the wool-wealthy father of his
lady. In his feverish haste, he fell off a
horse on to a mountain, or off a mountain
on to a horse. I forget which. At anyrate,
he broke his leg, whereupon Isabel gathered
up some clothes and a chaperone and sped
to Scotland to see that he was being well
looked after. Now they have father’s bless-
ing, and a lovely farm at Macedon. A beau-
tiful house is being dashed together,
and the minute the last lick of paint has
been put on, the pair will be married and
start to live happily ever after. The cere-
mony is booked for April.
Joan Innes-Noad, whose mother was a
Howard Smith, and who is as well known in
Melbourne as in Darling Point-road, has
murmured “Yes” to Billy Williams, a de-
lightful young man and cousin to Surgeon-
General Williams, of our own brown-clad
army.

beside her type-machine.

The Southern Cross Bazaar, to help the
Neglected Children’s Aid Society, has come
and gone at the Athenaeum Hall: but the
raffles —the all-important feature of the con-
cern —will invite ticket-buyers until April 4,
or thereabouts. Prizes range from a' £450
motor to a sewing machine; and a diamond
pendant (£125) and bangle (£45) glitter
among them. Tickets may be purchased for
a “bob” from Mrs. Percy Russell, or Mrs.
Hallenstein, at the addresses stated in the
advts.
Violet Loraine engineered a successful
matinee last week in aid of the Melbourne
stall at the Easter Bazaar. The Gaud
Mayor lent the council chamber for the
frivol, and Violet herself spoke a piece from
the dais which supports the mayoral throne.
She was garbed in white with an ermine hat
decorated with ospreys. Most of the visi-
tors (at 3s. per hat) wore white, and stewed
gently in the earthquaky atmosphere. Three
hundred chairs were ranked across the crim-
son velvet carpet, and each held at least one
woman. Pantomime girls doled out tea and
smiles at half a crown. Some of the most
excruciatingly lovely ones sold programmes
on the stairs and at other strategic
points. Mayoress Hennessy waved won-
derful blue feathers above a very neat
black frock. Fair-haired Mrs. Davey
was under a rose-wreathed hat. Mrs.
Godfrey, who is about to take her two
daughters travelling, said good-bye to various
people. Mrs. Hennessy congratulated the
trio, and murmured plaintively; “J have
every hour crammed with engagements up to
the end of April!” And yet men in gas-
works strike ! By the way, Mrs Hennessy’s
final job at this function was to hand a
boomerang-shaped bouquet to the Loraine
charmer, Now, I ask you. do you know of
any harder employment than handing bou-
quets?
Strong-man Clarence .Weber gave his
muscle-display in the To>vn Hall last Thurs-
day, and people had to be pushed away from
the doors before half-past seven. His
physical-cultured girls were billed to show
themselves —and they did. There were wild
gusts of excitement when Clarence played
Petruchio literally, and walked on the well-
trained shrewlets that lay littered about on the
stage. They “placed their hands beneath his
foot,” only it wasn’t their hands he walked
on. The best Canadian-frocked pupil stood
on the pedestal and posed, and Clarence
played circuses, making all the light-footed
girleens jump over his cane and go through
their tricks. Chief Justice John should have
given out the prizes, but either the doctor
had vetoed the outing, as his letter stated,
or Mrs. John had wisely withheld him from
temptation. He might have wanted to pinch
the lady dux of the school. Dr.
Bryant stepped into the breach, and said the
•nice things the occasion and the girl called
for.

Exchange-officer Major Manser, who bowed
himself off the Kirkpatrick staff because he
objected to something or other, is not to leave
Australia, after all. The Department for
Wounded Pride has offered him a post within
Brother O’Malley’s jurisdiction, and he has
accepted. So the furniture of the Toorak
villa has been unpacked, and the Major and
his Australian wife have burned their P.P.C.
cards.
McKay, of the Sunshine Harvesters —
generally referred to as “Hugh V.” —lately
suffered a painful setback in his Ballarat
electioneering for the House of Reps. An
acetylene motor-lamp exploded in his face,
and, although his eyes escaped permanent in-
jury, he got a few cuts to remind him of his
narrow escape.

A sentimental relic of the '4o’s was a rubv
ring which accompanied Miss Jane Barker’s
“Bless you. my children !” at the wedding of
Dr. Youl Nankivell to Nell Woinarski The
circlet was bought by the late Coroner Youl
of happy memory, and presented to Miss
Barker’s mother on his wedding day, as
token of gratitude to Mrs. Barker for having
introduced him to his bride—Miss Martin
a belle of the period. And now that Coroner
Youl, his wife and Mrs. Barker have all
passed away, the ring has come ' into the
family of the late medico’s grandson to be
converted into an heirloom.
The motor accident that befell the Har
pers’ son Colin in the Macedon district
turned out to be not so verv disastrous after
all. The first newspaper reports placed the
16-year-old Colin at death’s door, and sug-
gested that his young cousin, Margaret Nav
lor, was bruised to a pulp. Also, the prayers
of the congregation were desired, in a casual
way, for a damaged “shover.” The eatas
trophe was greatly exaggerated. Colin
Harper is now as right as the proverbial
ninepence, and the damsel and the “shover”
are merely remembering their upset with a
smile.
One of the big chiefs of the A.N A i s
going to feel very annoyed with himself
when he gazes upon a film showing a recent
vice-regal visit to the Exhibition. First the
big, grey flight of steps that rise to the side
entrance flickers on to the screen Then
several fussy senior Natives issue from the
portal, hurry down the steps, and wait ex
pectantly. The G.-G.’s carriage can almost
be heard approaching, so anxious are their
faces. Just before the equipage slides into
the foreground, the most august person in
the welcoming group furtively wipes his per-
spiring right hand on his ceremonious coat
tails. The slight action of a nervous mo-
ment has been caught by the infernal re-
cording machine and converted into some-
thing like a social catastrophe. •
Perhaps there are more; but Melbourne
certainly holds one very angry woman
Coming in here, she wanted to bring two
dogs with her. Quoth the Customs man
firmly : “Any dog arriving from the United
Kingdom must undergo veterinary inspec-
tion and then be detained in quarantine for
three months. From any Continental port
or Janan or China no dog is allowed to
land.” After a few minutes’ fuming the ladv
settled down to it philosophically, and had
got to think it was quite a good law until
she saw two white Pomeranians brought off
from one of the Jap wafsliips. Inquiring
she found out that it didn’t happen once or
twice, but that the two little beasts had. the
run of Melbourne. Then the explosion
occurred. But it don’t disturb the Customs.
They were too busy being affable, like the
rest of them.

Fruit-growers are mournful. Within the last few
weeks we have had two violent storms, which have
played mischief with vineyards and orchards. Both
currant and grape crops have suffered badly, and the
aggregate loss will mount high.

POLITICAL
POINTS
IN TOPHET.
INTERROGATES : “Who are they that pursued
the wandering Jew?”
REPLYUS : "Only the State Premiers after a
loan.”
The stout heart of Britain has been
deeply moved by recent suffragette proceed-
ings. The cable reports that some men
who caught a woman painting “Votes for
Women” across a seat at ’Ampstead
’Eath pulled her hair down and smothered
her face with green paint. The Lion is fairly
roused. We shall presently hear that a
strong force of “Territorials” has stormed
a suffragette meeting and torn the chair-
woman’s hat to pieces.
The Vie. Labor party won the Warren-
heip seat, and its stock goes up for the next
general election. Certainly there was a
majority of only 37 ; but, then, Warrenheip was
regarded as a safe Liberal seat, and if a “safe”
seat can be stormed, unpleasant things
are likely to happen to quite a crowd of seats
into which the Liberal has hitherto only
managed to claw. The fact is, Victoria
is beginning to grow rebellious against its
dull, foolish House of Fat, and realises at
last that there is no hope of bogus Liberal-
ism shifting the obstruction.
PROTECTION AND THE FUSION.
Mr. Watt spoke sensibly enough, and with his usual force, about the true meaning of Federalism,
but what he said on the fiscal position was not so creditable. Liberals are not bothering about the fiscal
question.... not being quite blind to the relative importance of things, they know that if once Social-
ism gets a firm grip on Australia—as it assuredly will if the anti-Socialists allow themselves to be
divided—it will not matter a rush what kind of a tariff there is.— ARGUS excitedly.
THE ONE UNDERNEATH : “Strikes me I'll be here a long time if I have to wait until
this one comes to his sober senses and says good-bye to his pet jim-jam."
Nielsen brought back one report, anyhow,
which has set a lot of Australians thinking
hard. It was in connection with the open-
ing of the Panama Canal, and its probable
effect on the Australian export of coal to
the Pacific Slope. There was a time when
Australian coal was supreme on the west
coast of America, and the trade is still very
large. But Nielsen points out that when
the great canal is opened, Alabama (U.S.A.)
coal will be available at 13s. 6d. per ton,
with an average haul of 3370 miles, as
against Australian coal at 11s. per ton, with
an average haul of 7200 miles. So the
chances of Newcastle holding out against
Alabama look blue; and the thousands of
people who are interested in N. S. Wales
coal mines, and have barracked for foreign
trade instead of helping to build up coal-
consuming industries at home, are begin-
ning to wonder what sort of fools they have
been.
In 1910 Australia exported
£456,000 worth of hides, and
its own tanners had to bid
heavily against the foreign
demand to get a bit of tbe
ox’s overcoat to make Aus-
tralian boots with. In one
recent year Australia ex-
ported £1,007,000 worth of
hides to supply Bull’s growing
population, and as the local
supply hadn’t increased its
own tanners had to offer sky-
high prices for the cow’s vest
in order to get boot material
for their customers. Natur-
ally, the price of boots is going
up. You can’t both sell your
hide and have it, and nothing
save a heavy export duty on
hides is likely to alter the sit-
uation. If this country con-
tinues to sacrifice everything
on the altar of commerce it
had better wear clogs. Or it
might make silver or zinc boots at a push,
after the fashion of the brass cothurn of the
old Carthaginian soldier.
One Herford, of Rhodesia, dropped into
the Sydney papers the other day with a hint.
The Northern Territory of to-day is much as
Rhodesia was 20 years ago, he says. But —
and this is where the hint comes in—Rhodes
didn’t reckon Rhodesia was of no account
because the climate of Beira, the port, wasn’t
of much account; and he didn’t set to work
to make Beira the capital or boost it into
importance simply because it had saltwater
on its whiskers. The Portuguese had the
Australian habit of squatting down on the
coast and trying to make the tadpole grow
its head there. Rhodes, when he came,
aimed at the heart of the country, and re-
fused to regard the coast-place as anything
more than a forwarding depSt, and that
policy has done more for the country in two
decades than the other did in two centuries
—or could have done in two aeons.
The Scaddan Government of Westralia is
still busy knocking holes through the old gibe
that the Labor party doesn’t worry about
anything except a bob a day for Bill. At
latest, it was considering the farmer and his
handicaps; and when it had satisfied itself
that one of his stillest handicaps is the price
he has to pay for some of his implements, it
took off its coat, and is now proceeding to
build State works for the manufacture of
the goods at Fremantle.
THE TRAIN-WRECHER.
Trouble threatened last week in connec-
tion with the Sydney Ferries, whose em-
ployees were exasperated because a case of
theirs had been five months before a Wages
Board and was still unsettled. Five months
is a long time for a case to drag through a
court, and the Labor Government should
find out what causes such gradualness ; but
before they throw over arbitration on that
score, Laborites had better think over a
matter or two. When employers know that
strikes are to be the weapon, they prepare,
too; and then the strike isn’t a matter of
days or weeks—it is a matter of months.
And even if the actual strike is a matter of
a few weeks only, the preparation is a
matter of months or years. That fine old
patient patriarch, W. G. Spence, who better
deserves the title of Father of the mine and
bush workers’ unions of Australia than any
other man, could tell tales of how he and
his executive sat down and prepared for a
solid four years for a strike. Arbitration
is slow and ought to be expedited ; but it is
a streak of lightning compared with the
other method.
