Brother Barlow, who represents the Den-
ham Government in Bananaland’s Legisla-
tive Council, has been telling the guileless
Maorilanders how Federation has ruined
Australia, particularly since Labor sat in
the seats of the rulers. The state" that
Barluw was in is suspected from the follow-
ing outburst: —
If I went down to your station here to go to
Auckland, and I saw a chimpanzee driving the engine
a gorilla doing the firing, and a blue-tailed monkey
in the guard’s van, I would get off that train and
come back to this hotel.
A hotel isn’t exactly the place The
Bulletin would recommend in the cir-
cumstances ; still when a person finds his
train crowded with things like that, even
a pub would be safer, especially if one could
get the housemaid to dust the blue snakes
.if the bed posts and shoo the piebald spiders
up the chimney.
The Sydney Telegraph seems deter-
minded to speak the truth and shame its
policy about Yassberra. Three indepen-
dent departments of the paper have des-
patched reporters to the place where K.
O’Malley is going to pour lakes upon the
landscape and import some river for the
Cotter dust-bed, and each of them has ex-
nosed the swindle that has been worked.
The first exposure came from the fishing
expert, the second was handed out the week
before last by the political writer, and the
third appeared on Feb. 5 from the pen of
the military reporter on the spot. He wrote
from the Federal site camp:—
The brigade was completed this afternoon by the
arrival of the western and southern troops of Light
Horse, who detrained at Yass Junction, early yester-
day morning, and marched across country... .At
Joir, where the bivouac was formed for the night,
Mr. Johnston placed the large woolshed at the dis-
posal of the party, accommodated the horses in the
sheep and stock yards, and provided man and beast
with water, which is no inconsiderable trifle in these
days of drought.
Presumably the Governor-General will fill
a kerosene-can with the necessary dampness
and take it along with him on Christening
Day in case the friendly Mr. Johnston has
none left.
T. E. Way, chief sur-
veyor in the Northern Ter-
ritory, drops in with his
opinion of the empty land
after 12 months’ residence
in it. “A white man’s
country with a . good
future,” he says, and takes
his wife and family back to
share it with him. Of
course, these certificates or
testimonials are very grate-
ful and comforting; but
there isn’t much practical
value to them. In fact, to
yell the virtues of a land
we are not making serious
efforts to occupy is a good
deal like attracting possi-
ble rivals’ attention to a
girl we want to marry by
sounding her praises before
definitely attaching her.
Quite a number of scraps
of information have been
supplied to this country as
the result of the American
mission which got that in-
convenient Labor politician
and ex-Minister, Nielsen, of
N.S.W., out of the road
for over a year at £4 a
day for “expenses,” and his
full Parliamentary _ salary.
One just published is about
how the San Francisco
private - enterprise ferry
boat runs into a sort of
dock, drops a gangway
equal to the whole width of
the boat, and discharges its
passengers with amazing
velocity. The San Francisco ferry boats have
been doing that for 30 years or so, to the
knowledge of everybody who has happened
that way, though to a raw, untravelled sort
of person the business might seem wildly
new and worthy of an excited and jcostly
report. If Nielsen had been sent to Egypt
at £4 a day 7 he might, in like fashion, have
discovered the pvramids and reported in
great alarm about them.
THE UPRISING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How much more will Australia be expected to
calmly endure? In N. S. Wales last year we had an
act of rank disloyalty in the eviction of the Gover-
nor-General. .. .Lately we have felt ashamed every
time we have sent a letter outside the Common-
wealth at the hideous stamp we have had to use. .. .
The latest insult. ... etc., etc. — A rabid long-sufferer
in the Argus, 27/2/’l3.
How long, good losh! how long shall we sit calmly
and endure
Before we rise, and for Australia’sh ills arrange a
cure?
How long from padded chairs shall we be satisfied
to snort
“Dishloyalishts!” and “Traitors!” or some out-
burst of the sort?
A kangaroo upon our stamps, inshtead of George
[hie] King?
Oh, this is ’orrible! Le’sh [hie] arise, ’n’ shtop
the thing!
When we, awake-arising, come to reckon up our
wrongsh,
We know to whom the chief [hie] shame, the final
blame, belongsh.
Who threw the Guv’nor’s pots ’n’ pans out on the
Noo South mat?
Who gave us holy men of cash our beashly name
of Fat?
Who took the House o’ Reps, ’n’ filled it up with
anarchists ?
Traitorsh ’n’ rebelsh did it, boys, ’n’ blank dishloy-
alists 1
Yet what can we expect from those who curse Aush-
tralia thus
When there is vim in them, and little vigor left
in us;
While in [hie —beg y’r pard’n!] seats, we nation-
pillars sit
And curse, inshtead of gettin’ up to throw things
round a bit?
Up, then! Awake! Rise up! The time hic-deed
is ripe, I shink,
To rise and shplash around anuzzer blanky pint of
ink !
It is a truly shplendid thought, if not entirely new,
That, right or wrong, these knaves are wrong, what-
ever they may do.
So let us then rise up and do our duty without
fear !
Come, get the flag out, lads, and wave [hie] —wave
’er round ’n’ cheer!
Hooray ! Hoor —oh, le’sh rise up, men—le’sh ’wake-
rise, ’n’ be
Loyal some more—’n’ down with kangaroosh ’n’
anarchy!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
WHAT MOTHER ASKS OF US.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To exclude all but the locally born from the six
State Governorships would not be generous. Aus-
tralians were a generous people, and understood more
and more every day the material advantages of be-
longing to the great British Empire, but surely they
did not wish to enjoy all the advantages in a one-
sided manner.— State Governor Strickland.
The great advantages we gain
Just by belonging to
The Empire, must to ev’ry sane
And loyal citizen be plain ;
And we will nothing do
That’s calculated to thereby
Cut, snap or strain the Crimson Tie.'
And in return for all her care,
What does our Parent ask?
A grievous burden, hard to bear,
She begs of us that we should share.
It is our humble task,
Lest kinship’s thread should be destroyed,
To help Ma with her unemployed!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The victory of Almissa in the Australian
Oup race raises a question as to what is meant by
the word “interference” when it is used to fore-
shadow a probable “reversal of forrt)” by a horse
that has run very badly., Almissa, the original
favorite for the Australian Cup, started for the
Essendon Stakes three days before the big event, and
the running of the English horse was supposed to- be
a i test of his unprqye-n capacity to stay a longer
He ran prominently for a while, and then
dropped back, finishing' seventli in a field of 10.
This poor performance seemed to put Almissa out of
reasonable calculation for the Australian Cup,
although he was going to meet his conquerors—•
Piastre, Cadonia and others —on better terms in the
long distance race. The daily papers published
vfigue statements of “interference,” without attempt-
ing to explain why a little accidental jostling or
cunning jockeyship could cause a horse that was
always running second or third for the first mile to
drop back and remain in the background for the last
half-mile. The interference couldn’t have gone on
for, say, 50secs., and if it was a very serious sus-
tained injustice to Almissa, how came it that the
public was not fully informed of the business?
Beyond a brief statement that one of the riders in
the Essendon Stakes had been suspended by the
stewards for three months, the public saw no reason
to suspect that it was not a fairlv run race. As for
the “interference” story, evidently the bookmakers
didn’t see how Almissa could have been so com-
pletely knocked out of the running without being
actually injured. Thev lengthened their odds from
3 or 4 to 1 against Almissa for the Australian Cup
before his Essendon Stakes performance, to 10 to 1
on the day of the race. But when the race came to
pass, Almissa reversed his previous form in a most
remarkable way. Instead of running prominently
at first, and dropping back when “interfered with,”
he ran in the rear for If miles, and then rushed to
the front, and stayed there. And the stipendiary
stewards, seemingly, took it as a matter of course.
* * * *
Strange that the stable which had brought
off the Almissa coup should have missed the chance
to make a big win on Aleconner in the Bourke
Handicap, an hour or two later. Aleconner, like
Almissa, belongs to the Baillieu brethren, and the
stable companions were backed in doubles for the
Newmarket and Australian Cun. The 3-vear-old son
of Malster performed ignominiously in the Futurity
Stakes, and the stable must have accepted the run-
ning - as a public trial. For the Bourke Handicap
an apprentice jockey was mounted on Aleconner, and
starting at 20 to 1 the colt won from the legitimate
favorites, Volsloane and Kirn, even as Almissa had
won from Piastre and Cadonia.
Tittle Almissa, coming away from the field at the
business end of a long Cun race, reminded one of
the recently-expressed expert opinion that “EnoHsh
horses are superior over all distances to Australian
horses” —hist at present. Hallowmas, a horse of no
class in his own country, was treated as a duffer bv
Australian handicanpers. vet he was nearly good
enough to win the Melbourne Cun under a light
weight. Almissa, sold in England as a poor little
thing, touched in the wind, has already won three
good races un to miles, after running second in
an important mile sprint. On the o+her hand,
Eudorus, the Fnturitv Stakes winner, has hardlv
run un to his English form, but he is now a 7-year-
old stallion with a temper. The imported horses
nre certpinlv nervous, uncertain beasts in many
oases, and not one
longdistance race under a weigrh f .
* * * *
When Piastre had duly won the Champion
face, punters asked themselves how it happened
that this consistently good performer had seemingly
been fairlv beaten by Cadonia, at a difference of
4lb. only, in the 2-} miles Australian Cup. True,
there was a difference of 51b. the other way—or
9lb. in all—when they met in the three miles , test
race, but this time Piastre was lengths ahead of
Cadonia from start to finish. The latter’s reputation
as a “great stayer” is apparently founded on the
fact that he’s a very backward beginner if the pace
is good from the jump. In the champion journey
Cadonia made a remarkably poor show of speed
until he was asked to give a start of, say, 10
lengths to Piastre in the last mile, with the result
that he never got near enough to prove whether the
Melbourne Cun winner was all out, or otherwise.
- The time (Srnin. 26sec.) has been beaten only thrice
in 38 years, and the going, although not soft, was
not favorable to record-breaking.
* * * *
The backers of Booligal had a couple of
sad experiences at the V.R.C. meeting. In both the
two-mile and the three-mile steeplechases the tear-
away son of Carbinier went out a warm favorite,
rushed to the front 'at an early stage of the argu-
ment, and then settled his hash by running off.
The horse that goes astrav when he is in the lead
is always expected to win next time, and maybe
Booligal will run up to expectation—if he doesn’t
run amok—in the Onkaparinga Steeple.
Kirn was a eonsoicuously unsatisfactory
performer at Flemington. The mare, coming with
a late rush, finished third for the Newmarket (six
furlongs), and was third again for the Bourke Han-
dicap (seven furlongs), after getting badly away.
On this form she looked likely to make amends
in the Farewell Handicap (one mile), but this time
she failed even to get a place behind Gillamatong,
whose victory, bv the way, was strikinglv incon-
sistent with his failure to see out the finish of the
Bourke Handicap four days earlier.
“F.C.”: When, a year or two ago, the
V.R.C. handed over Footscrav Hill, across the Salt-
water Riyer and overlooking Flenr'neton racecourse,
as a public park and nicnic ground, - most people
suspected something behind the niece of generosity.
The something has now materialised into a grant,
by the Watt-Weary Government, of the right _to
charge for admission to the Flat. The non-naving
“sports” will be pushed across the river, and the
club will acquire considerable revenue—for which it
does not give, and can never, in the nature of things,
give, much return—from the bob-a-head Flatites.
But it is just possible the parties to the deal have
bitten off more than they can chew. Footscrav
council, sundry Labor leagues and the A.N.A. have
voiced strong protests. Both Caulfield and Fleming-
ton courses were originallv set aside for public use
ns “sporting and recreation” grounds. The “flat”
on the former is still reserved for public traffic, etc.,
except on race days, while Flemington Flat was re-
served for public use on anv and every day. Then,
a few years back, the V.R.C. grabbed the excuse of
danger from horses in training to close it except on
race days. Now it reaches out a<ra ; n and practically
steals a block of public land worth many thousands.
With rain drifting in waves across Syd-
ney on the wings of the wild south-easter on Satur-
day, cricket and every other sport were Quite im-
possible. Certainly, a yacht race was attempted,
but it ended in disaster. It was a dreadful after-
noon and a still more dreadful night. Ferry traffic
was paralysed; tramlines were washed out; a cab-
man was drowned at Petersham while attempting to
drive along a street with Bft. of water in it; at
Cook’s River people had to be taken off the house
tops in boats. And in one suburb three reporters
were blown clean through the door of a public
house into the bar.
To come back to cricket. As play was
impossible the 10th round had to be decided on the
figures of the previous Saturday, and some queer
results eventuated. Chief among them was the
miraculous escape of North Sydney from a bad
hiding, and the retention of its place at the head of
the competition on a draw. Redfern beat Central
Cumberland, Gordon did the same to University,
and Burwood found itself definitely on top of Peter-
sham. North Svdney-Glebe, Waverley-Paddington,
and Balmain-Middle Harbor games were counted
drawn. So North Sydney leads with 17 points to
Redfern’s 16, University has 12, Burwood seven,
Gordon four, and Central Cumberland two. Below
that are seven clubs with less than nothing.
The wintry week-end in Melbourne de-
stroyed the chances of those cricket teams whom the
loss of the toss had relegated to the second day’s
play. At Victoria Park ’Wood promptly closed at
214 for six wickets, x - an ’Varsity in and out again
for 77 (Pitcher, five for 18, Ryder four for 28), and
then set about demolishing it anew, in the attempt
to gain a three-point victory. Curiously enough
Uni. again vanished for 77, so ’Wood won by an
innings and 60 runs. South, with 335 runs for two
wickets, naturally closed, and told ’Cote to get busy.
’Cote did its best, but that only amounted to 132
runs for the first innings, and there was no time for
a second. North made a valiant effort to match
Carlton’s moderate 227. but only raked up 193 for
eight wickets. St. Kilda virtually dropped all its
nennant chances when it lost the toss to ’Pran. The
latter had piled up 313 for two wickets, and then
left the Saints a sodden wicket and about two hours
of decent weather to beat the figures. St. Ivilda
could only scrape up 118, and so retired definitely
from the turmoil.
The N.S.W. XI which drifted over to
Tasmania walloped the apple-landers by an innings
and 289 runs. Colt H. L. Collins made 250, scored
for Ma. Other inter-State smiters of more than
250 include Murdoch (321), H. Moses (297 n. 0.),
and Trumper 292. N.S.W. called on only three
bowlers; Massie took six wickets for 76, Scott six for
84, and Hordern seven for 124.
Corner men—the comic relief of the noble art.
Roy Kenny (defeated by Clarke) being
titivated by the gentlemen-in-waiting.
Analysis of the Sheffield Shield averages
throws no limelight on the young bloods. The only
batsmen to scramble through with figures above 40
per innings were Macartney, Trumper, Barbour, Bards-
ley (N.S.W.) ; Mayne, Crawford and Hill (S.A.) ; and
Matthews and Armstrong (Vic.). All of these, bar-
ring Barbour, are of the old school. In bowling
Whitt.y (25) and Crawford (21) gathered 46 wickets
out of 72 for S.A. ; Massie (N.S.W.) took 22 wickets,
and the only other Ma State bowler to take more
than five was Minnett, who got away with six ; while
Ryder’s 22 wickets for Vic. overshadowed the rest
of the trundlers of that State.
* * * *
After all, S.A. has not grabbed the Shef-
field Shield out of its turn. Since 1892-3 the Shield
has hung in its gilded halls but three times: ’93-4,
’O9-10 and ’l2-13. The rest of the time it oscillated
between Ma and Vic. However, there is a lot of
promising young talent in S.A. just now, and the
Land of the Crow is going to water that young talent
assiduously.
* * * *
Another remarkable bowling performance
was put up in the Brisbane A Grade senior competi-
tion match between Toowong and Nundah on a
recent Saturday, R. K. Oxenham taking six of Too-
wong’s wickets for eight runs. This is a perform-
ance to be placed alongside G. Duffin’s six for seven.
Rain took a hand in the finish of the last
Sheffield match of the season, played in Adelaide.
Victoria, battling to make an unlikely 381, stopped
at just ovex 200. The honors were with the old
crowd, though Hill and Gehrs failed. Matthews,
Armstrong and Ransford did most of the work on one
side, Crawford, Whitty and Mayne (now one of
Australia’s best batsmen) on the other. Ryder was
away, and Moves and Baring did little. S.A. takes
the Shield with three wins, to N.S.W.’s two, and
Victoria’s one.
Harry Trott has been lecturing the Maori-
landers on their lack of cricketing style. He slings
no mud at Maoriland cricket; in fact, he hands
out bouquets, remarking that he could even now
pick a Maoriland team which would make a good
fight with an Australian State XI. But it would
have to be on Maoriland pitches, he adds, dryly.
He says the Maorilanders adopt the English method
of protecting their stumps with their pads, and on
the fast Australian wickets the ball would be upon
them before they had time to get the bat in action,
and the whole team would be out l.b.w. Some-
body retorted that it was W. Carlton, an Australian,
who introduced the practice; to which Trott imme-
diately replied that Carlton never learned it in Aus-
tralia, where he was busy unlearning it as a member
of North Melbourne. Trott also pointed out that in
a match in which Carlton played against a team
with which Trott was associated, he was given out
l.b.w. in both innings, the pace having utterly
beaten him.
The race in which the deep-keeled old-time
yacht Thelma came to grief on Saturday in
Port Jackson should never have been run.
Neither should the 18-footers who threw spume
on the beard of Providence have been allowed
to bullock over the mountains of water to
the Sow and Pigs. The water police launcn
Argus practically put in the afternoon rescuing
capsized and swamped crews. There’s no fun in
this sort of thing, and people will realise it one of
these days when a whole company vanishes for ever.
The wreck of the Thelma was a very sad business,
the only bright gleam in it was the heroic effort
made by the others on the yacht to save the man
who had been washed overboard. The sea was
pouring in through the Heads in gigantic waves,
topped with blinding rain. The Thelma and &>ayo-
nara had crossed from Watson s Bay to round the
Manly buoy, and had started to thresh back against
the howling sou’-easter. The Sayonara § lacing was
carried away, and she ran into North Harbor for
shelter. Thelma held on magnificently, and ap-
parently would have come through all right but
that a giant sea swept W. Moore, the mam-sheet
hand, overboard. Mr. Lloyd-Jones never hesitated
a second ; one of his crew was overboard, and if it
was humanly possible he was going to save that
man. Realising the danger, he ordered his crew to
throw off their oilskins, and then gibed. The people
on the steamer saw the yacht rise on her beam-
ends and vanish. They waited breathlessly to see
the white hull reappear. But her mast had carried
away, thousands of tons of booming waves poured
down on her, and remorselessly drove her towards
the rocks. Moore, the lost man, drifted out
of sight across the Manly bombora. It looked as
though the Thelma would be swept there also, and
she would have been had not the pilot steamer
Barunda succeeded in reaching her. After she had
been dragged to an anchorage off Dobroyde, the
crew was transferred to a Watson’s Bay steamer
and brought on to Sydney, mighty lucky to be alive.
No trace of the missing man could be seen ; but at
least his skipper and mates did all that could be
done, and more than many would have risked, in
the attempt to save him. All the same, if they
had not been there, there would have been no need
to try to save him.
The Sydney Yacht Squadron held a race
for the eight-metre class, and of five entrants three
faced the starter in spite of the wild weather. Petrel,
perfectly rigged for the day, was the obvious winner,
although Magic made an excellent showing under the
difficulties. The skipper of the third boat, Sunbeam,
showed more sagacity than the other two rolled into
one, for when he got a v'ew of the Heads and the
sea that was pouring through them, he promptly
remarked, “No good to Gundy!’’ and retired. Petrel
won with over 40 minutes to spare.
The result of the 18-foot open-boat race on
Port Jackson on Saturday afternoon proves the wis-
dom of the ancient adage, “An old sea dog for a
hard blow.” Only seven were mad enough to start,
and of these but one got through, the ancient Don-
nelly, skippered by the ancient George Holmes, now
somewhere about 70. Eunice lost her mast on the
way to the line; Pastime’s gib blew out as she got
away; Quibree’s mast went by the board off Brad-
ley’s ; Sunny South lost the mast at the Sow and
Pigs : Mona, like the light of other days, vanished
utterly; and Scot was swamped. Donnelly alone
remained afloat, triumphant and soaked.
The N.S.W. eight-oar crew for Adelaide
is a verv queer basket of fish in one respect. Not
one of the men who visited England and Stockholm
has a seat —probably they’ve used up all the leave

Still it is good to see the young bloods have a show.

The W.A. rowing rumpus has subsided. At
a special meeting of the association a motion was
adopted that, in the event of the controlling body
being unable to secure a representative crew, the
offer of the W.A. Rowing Club to despatch an eight
be accepted. So, whichever way it goes, W.A. is
likelv to have a finger in the pie at Adelaide in
April. The squabble has stirred up the old-time
votaries of the sport, including the Bishop of Perth,
Frank Parker (Crown Prosecutor) and Gawler,
M.L.C.. who did a good deal towards clearing the
atmosphere.
In the U.S.A. the rule of the road is quite
the opposite to our own, and the vehicular traffic
keens always to the right. The result is that when
ordering a Yankee-made car one has to be particu-
lar to specify which side of the machine he desires
his steering and other gear. Incidentally, there is
something to be said in favor of the left-hand seat.
The time the motor driver has to exercise caution
is when overtaking and passing another \ c '+ fle,
which mav decline to make sufficient way. and for
this the left-hand seat is ideal. It has this great
drawback, however: the controlling levers must be
either worked bv the left hand or placed between
the passenger and the driver in a place where they
are liable to accidental manipulation when 18« t.
squashes in beside the driver. But in spite
of this obvious disadvantage, it is said that fullv 35
per cent, of American-made cars have the driver’s
seat placed on the left hand.
Rananaland will control the Australian
amateur cvcling championships in 1914, N.S.W. in
1915, and Vic. in 1916. If any of the other cycling
bodies can be fished to the surface in the other
States at the end of three years, they will be granted
a share in the draw for 1917. The cycling confer-
ence, which decided these things in Sydney last
week, evidently reckons it is here for some time.
N.S.W. cyclist Stanley Burtenshaw added
another gem to his already over-jewelled d : adem by
wmning the One Mile Australian Amateur champion-
ship at Svdney Sports Ground last week. The other
championship, Five Miles, drifted to the plate-chest
of A. Beck, a vastlv improved whirler who will be
found in the front of manv future races, if the gods
do not snatch him to themselves. The riders from
Queensland and Vic. had a cheerless time, and nary
a crumb or the price of a drink fell their way. It’s
a cold world.
D. Campbell and Bops Soden go on the
warpath at an earlv date against G. G. White’s Svd-
motor-car record of 19hr. 47min.
Their proposed schedule is 18hr. 29min., and
arrangements are being carefullv organised to arrange
for a clear run through. The opening of railway
gates is a big factor in a record run between Svd-
ney and Melbourne, and if that can be arranged for
so as to avoid delav, and the roads are no worse
than usual, White’s figures will probably be smashed.

“Condamine” : Re The Bulletin’s re-
marks on Jerome after his fight with Balzac Tii
speak of Jerry in the same breath as the “smokes’'
from the other side is an insult to the genial abo
from Warra. His style may not be orthodox but
what can be expected, seeing that he learned the ar
through sparring with casual backblock “shrewdies’’
who knew as much of boxing as Jerry does If
algebra ? Then, in his favor, look at his career as an
athlete, foot-runner, rough-rider and steer-thrower
and the fact that he can put up such scraps at ail
age when most pugs are down and out shows that he
has at least led a clean life. A more unassuming
fellow, or one more averse, as a general thing to
hurting his fellow man never put up his hands. ’
Roy Kenny and Jack Clarke, who met at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday night, were unlucky
The terrific downpour ruined the gate, and the hous'
was probably insufficient to pay their training e .x
penses. Still, there was one incentive to fight—the
winner could look forward to a couple or three good
mills in the near future. So they fought. But what
sort of a fight was it! An entirely new brand of
scrap, in which there was much loud stamping of
feet, the combatants smiting alternately, and both
standing stiffly to take the wallop. Occasionally
there were rushes, alarums, and weird happenings, out
of which one bruiser would emerge with the other
lying across his shoulder, or with his nose buried in
the other’s back hair. It was wildly grotesque, but
whether the burlesque was accidental or subtly
intentional, Heaven only knows. At the end the
referee declared Clarke the winner—as good a verdict
as any other he could have given, if not better.
N.S.W. Rugby Union has received a batch
of alterations in rules from the English Union, and
the N.S.W. body, being a baby in the cradle, will
swallow ’em all without question. Only one really
matters, and, by some miracle, it is for the better.
It provides a penalty for wilfully heaving the ball
into touch, or kicking out of bounds. Of course,
that makes for faster and more spectacular play, and
was one of the reasons of the success of the Rugby
League in Sydney. When the N.S.W.U. proposed to
introduce this reform some years ago, the “Home”
authorities lumped on the proposal with both feet.
But evidently things have been moving in England
of late. And yet the use of the touchline was not
altogether bad. Against a heavy crowd it gave a
light, skilful team with a few good kickers a chance
that would be impossible under the new arrangement.
The eight entries for the Davis Cup—in-
cluding the “British Isles,’’ the holders —will neces-
sitate a big batch of preliminary rounds. The first
draw is: America v. Australasia, Germany r.
France, S’Africa v. Canada, and Belgium a bye.
England, being in possession of the pannikin, sits
quiet till the semi-final is played, and then meets
the winner, who by that time will have played
three distinct matches—that is, provided no one
drops out. Wilding, A. B. Jones and S. N. Doust
will look after Australia’s interests, and the first
round, against America, will be played in England.
Airmen have to go up, safe or otherwise,
when it suits the interests of the firm, according
to evidence given at the inquest on Geoffrey Eng-
land, one of the pilots of the Bristol School on
Salisbury Plain (Eng.). His firm had made four
aeroplanes to the order of the Roumanian Govern-
ment, which presumably thought they might come
in handy against Bulgaria if that truculent princi-
pality kicked too hard over Silistria. Prince Can-
tacuzens, the Roumanian agent, was anxious to see
the . machines fly, and the firm .sent word the
machines must be tested by a certain date, “good,
txid, or indifferent weather.” On the Monday England
wrote to his mother, “They don’t care if our necks
are broken, so long as the prince is satisfied.”
Nevertheless, he obeyed orders, went up on Thurs-
day, turned turtle at an altitude of 600 ft., and was
dashed to death. Now there’s going to be a row.

“Oh. yes,” said the man from Mont
Blong, “we have plenty of fine athletes in Switzer-
land. The reason the outside world knows, so little
about them is that we have no professionalism. We
have, of course, nominal prizes, which are highly
valued, but there is rygyer any money in the game,
and if a Swiss atlieWe takes part in a money-com-
petition abroad, he is barred ever after from home
competitions. You see, we are. so far. as free from
the man who makes a trade of athletics as we are
from the bookmaker, and all the other parasites of
sport, and we intend to remain so.”

WELLINGTON and CHRISTCHURCH.
The gall (or something) of these Women s
Fashionable League orators is paralysing.
Mrs. Robert Morton assured a meeting in
Melbourne, the other day, that “Mr. Cook,
the leader of the Opposition, was respon-
sible for the initiation of the present de-
fence scheme.” Cook, the man who con-
demned compulsory military training as “the
worst form of continental conscription,
and denounced the idea of an Australian
navy which, he alleged, would one day be
turned against our poor old Mother, to
whom he wanted to present “scores of
Dreadnoughts!” Has Mrs. Robert Morton
the remotest idea of what she talks about,
or is she just a plain, unvarnished
“Liberal”?
Having long disregarded the demands
from various parts of the State for adequate
school accommodation, Victoria’s Education
Department has at last bumped against the
machinery of the law. Recently a Woome-
lang parent was summoned for not sending
his youngster to school. He pleaded that
he was justified in declining to endanger
his child’s health, the school having floor
space for only 40 scholars, whereas 90 were
in attendance. The Bench —a P.M. presid-
ing—agreed that his plea was valid, and dis-
missed the case. So the Weary State must
now either provide adequate school accommo-
dation or let its children grow up ignorant.

THE ASSASSIN’S DREAM.
A stern and silent warder
His moody watch did keep
O’er a grim and death-doomed convict
Who muttered in his sleep.
The slumberer from a dream awoke
With a loud and hideous yell.
I’ve been haunted in my sleep, he gasped,
By the horrid imps of Hell.
My victim’s shrouded form arose from
The deep and awful grave.
O God! ’Twas a fearful Dream
The wretched man did rave.
Condemned by judge and jury
For the murder of my Wife
For which I’ll pay the penalty
Upon the gallows with my life.

“Temple Court” considers his stomach :
Having practised vagrancy in many lands, this
glutton and bibber wishes to complain that we Aus-
tralians don’t value our local food and drink at any-
thing like their true value. So far, no start has been
made to create an Australian school of cookery. No-
body has invented a national dish, not even a national
sauce. We copy our daily meals from cold countries,
where bacon and eggs have displaced blubber and
crude oils as a breakfast delicacy. We use only one
bread made of wheat, and know nothing of maize,
rye or oats. Living in a country covering 30deg. of
latitude and 40deg. of longitude, we look upon fruit
as a luxury, and prefer that which comes from old
and cold climes before our own luscious tropical and
sub-tropical products. You could as easily prize out
of an Australian his great-grandfather’s fondness for
carrots and cabbage (ideal cow food) as you could
pull out of a Presbyterian his love for his Shorter
Catechism. Verily is an Australian’s diet mostly
made up of beef and mutton and onions and cabbage.
And he knows nought of the hundred salad plants and
vegetables of the Latin peoples living in a region like
his own. In the way of green stuff he
has little else but lettuce, and likes it
better for being stale and limp and not over-clean.
Australia imports more fish than it catches, and
what local fish is caught is cooked in one way in a
frying-pan. If the limited choice of badly-cooked
food upsets my patriotism, it nearly vanishes when
contemplating the drink question. We make as
good beer as is made anywhere on the planet, and
most of us prefer to buy imported muck because it is
imported. Once on an N.D.L. steamer I had been
drinking the ship’s draught lager beer copiously for
breakfast, luncheon .and dinner, also for supper, anc
before getting up. and just before lying down, and
every time the forecastle bell was struck. I told th<
chief steward I was deadly sorry 1 could not gei
such good German lager in Melbourne. “Ach nein!
he replied. “It is of Sydney!” Then I started to
investigate the beer question, and _ found we brew
here just as good beer as they do in Milwaukee, or
Pilsen or Munich, and often better. I had a South
Australian burgundv at dinner last night fully equal
to any Romanes Conti, at half the price of the
foreign wine; and habitually I drink a N.S.W. hock
as fine as the best Johannesburger, at a quarter the
cost.
“F.C.”: It seems to me there is something
wrong about these alleged 00-, 60- and 80-
miles an hour records by motorists. I was
perambulating South Yarra (Melb.) at an un-
civilised hour the other morning when a large
motor came down Toorak road at a smart
pace. In it or about it somewhere were four
men, while a fifth bestraddled the bonnet
and explained to a bottle in his hand that
he “wash jolly good f’low.” His prominent
position seemed to harass those behind, and
while endeavoring to persuade him into the
car someone grabbed the steering wheel, and
the machine ran smash into a telegraph post.
The post was well and truly sunk and of
good blue gum, and stood the shock well,
better than the imported car, which was—
well, it just wasn’t anything, only a sugges-
tion of something which had once been. It
took four of the occupants half an hour to
realise the details of the earthquake, while
the fifth only took up the burden of mun-
dane matters again some hours later in a
hospital. (He wasn’t the bonnet-rider, by
the way.) But what bothers me is that
each and every one of these four maintains
that the car was only going a “moderate.”
between* 15 aad 20 aides an hour.

.itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-

quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound, prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

The Inky Way
“Senior”: Blundell, M.L.A., discussing (8.6/2/’lS < )
“Wus’s” castigation of the Adelaide Labor Herald’s
treatment of its staff, meanly says that the Herald
had a hard row to hoe because “rejects” were un-
loaded on to it. Also, he says that the majority
of those who were with the’paper at the start are
with it still. Let me tear Blundell to pieces, and,
incidentally, show how this paper has broken the
hearts of the enthusiasts who wanted to make it a
success. The Daily Herald, when it started, had a
literary staff consisting of Wedd (editor), Burgoyne
(associate), Whitehead (chief), Chamberlain (sub.),
Kneebone, Watson, Martindale and Young. These men
had to do the whole of the work for an eight-page
daily, with a 12-page issue on Saturdays. Blundell de-
scribes them as a fine lot of “rejects.” The facts
are that Wedd was induced to take his position after
Blundell, and other members of the board of manage-
ment had chased him up for at least two months,
despite repeated refusals of the job. He was editor
of the Adelaide Chronicle, and before that had been
in the chair of the weekly Herald, so the Board
knew his work. He is the only member of the orig-
inal team left with the paper. Burgoyne had been
editor of the weekly Herald for two years. Blun-
dell, M.L.A., must have known all about his work.
He left the Herald' to become leader writer on the
West Australian, and is still there. Whitehead,
persuaded by the editor to leave the Advertiser,
stepped from the Herald to the Argus. Chamber-
lain got so sick of press work that he left it altoge-
ther. Kneebone was appointed publicity expert in
London for the Commonwealth Government. Wat-
son, who was brought down from the Barrier Miner,
was taken by the Melbourne Herald. Martindale
went to the Advertiser, and was presently promoted,
Young was sacrificed by the Board because two or
three officials of the United Laborers’ Union threat-
ened to boycott the paper unless he was discharged.
This action was taken against the advice of the
editor, and it nearly caused a strike of the staff. By
the way, the paper devoted many columns last year
to castigating the union officials it had previously
grovelled to. So much for the first team. Surely
not a bad record for a crowd of “rejects”! But
there is any amount more. Daly, chief of staff on
Hobart Daily Post, found Herald conditions so
uncongenial that he remained there only a few months.
Clarke, Shanahan, Maxted, Letcher—these and many
others have quitted the Herald, and their subse-
quent appointments show that they are reckoned
good men. And I would sooner accept the judg-
ment of Schuler, Cunningham, Davidson, Hackett,
Sowden and Bonython in regard to pressmen than that
of Blundell. If the Board will make some inquiries
from impartial journalists it can easily find out
where the weakness lies. They will tell him it is
in the board of management. When the daily was
established the Board consisted of Blundell, M.L.A.;
Styles, M.L.C.; Coneybeer, M.L.A.; Thompson
Green, M.L.A. ; Macgillivray, M.L.A.; Senator
Guthrie and Memmler —a tobacco-twister, a shop-
assistant, a boilermaker, a wharf-laborer, a sailor,
a collar-maker, and a laborer; all excellent men at
their jobs, no doubt, but with not much knowledge
of newspapers. This board, including as it did six
legislators, made all the capital it could for these
politicians. A speech delivered in the House by one
of them, and published fully in one of the other
morning papers on Wednesday or Thursday, has been
re-published in the Saturday issue of the Herald.
This was the board’s method of serving readers
with red-hot news. Interference with the inner
working of the office caused more friction than I have
ever seen in any other office. So bad did this be-
come that a meeting of the staff was held at the
Aurora Hotel, in 1910, and an ultimatum was issued
that there would be a general cessation of work un-
less the practice was discontinued. Whether it was
discontinued or not I don’t know. I left soon after-
wards. But judging bv the number of others who
have left since, I shouldn’t say that things had im-
proved much.
“Jack Hardgraft”: More politicians have gradu-
ated from the Kalgoorile Sun than from any other
paper of its age in Australia. Vosper, Hugh Mahon
and Wallace Nelson, successive editors, won the
Parliamentary haven. Then Tommy Walker, a one-
time Ma State member, beat Labor-leader Hastie at
the selection ballot, and left the chair to represent
Kanowna in the Perth State House. About the only
Sun editors who never got into politics were Jack
Drayton, who did a month in Perth gaol for alleged
contempt of Parliament; and Andrew Hayward,
whose vices are poetry and cricket.
* * * *
From H. C. Coghlan, president of the Australian
Writers and Artists’ Union (N.S.W.) : “Nightowl”
(B. 13/2/’l3) says it’s about time country press-
men got a Wages Board. I don’t know what State
“Nightowl” writes from, and a Wages Board is a
State tribunal, but I can tell him that the Writers
and Artists’ Union (N.S.W.) has already taken pre-
liminary action in regard to country pressmen in that
State, and intends to follow it up as soon as pos-
sible, the union’s Wages Board to be invoked if
considered necessary. At present, the union’s Wages
Board, after sitting once a week from October 14 to
December 4, and hearing evidence upon our first
batch of claims, is resting or something; but it will
have to re-sit and issue an award. Other city
writers and artists and country writers and artists
will be covered by the union’s next batches of claims,
and the union will unhesitatingly submit all these
to the Wages Board rather than accept those “amic-
able (?) agreements” and “honorable” (?) “under-
standings,” which generally prove so unsatisfactory.
* * * *
“Vulcan”: Astonishing the penchant of the ancient
and ponderous London Times for startlingly youth-
ful editors. The recently-retired G. E. Buckle was
only 29 when appointed. He held the job for 28
years. His predecessor, the “great Delane,” took
the reins at the still earlier age of 23, and continued
his grip till he was 60. Two other pre-Delane
guides, Barnes and Ohenery, died in harness at a
comparatively youthful 66 and 68 respectively. Now
Buckle’s successor, Geoffrey Robinson, wtat. 37, car-
ries the infantile tradition a. further stage.
* * * •
“H. 0.”: Re “Nightowl’s” remarks (B. 16/2/’l3),
anent roundabout sentences and stencilled phrases, I
will be pleased when somebody informs the great
dailies that a gun doesn’t explode, whatever the
charge inside it may do. Furthermore, explosions
don’t happen gradually; yet the newspaper scribe is
continually writing of a “sudden” explosion.
MY INKY MAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear girl of my dreams, who has never existed—-
Except in my dreams, or,’ in verse, as a theme;
(Whereas you have often most ably assisted
In earning a crust, as the price of a dream).
The verse is the guinea stamp—nothing can bar that,
1 Though poets seek vainly for gold in the bar—
You, you are the gold, girl, for a’ that and a’ that —
A queen for a sovereign—you’re worth it, you are.
Dear versatile girl, of all sizes and ages,
You serve many turns, and you play many parts;
We serve you up daily, in shrines or on stages,
As mistress of hearts or as Master of Arts.
It goes without words that I love and adore you ;
And yours is the luck that you never have been,
For, sure as I live, I’d neglect you or bore you,
And make you my drudge, while I called you my
queen.
Ah, Queen of Variety—Angel of Purity—l
, Nymph of wegk tea, or of Pommerv Sec,
You are along bbtli the cash and security, '
A stimulant draught, or a sobering check.
Dear girl, that need nought but to live in my ink
pot,
And twinkle at will in a verse or a blot,
Do I want you, for keeps, by my side? Well, I
think not.
I trust you, my inkling, you’re safe in the pot.
MAJORCA.

IN BANANA LAND
The Brisbane Standard (Labor), fling-
ing a brick at Joe Cook, points out that
Hughes has appointed three Conservatives
to the High Court. This may successfully
demonstrate that Cook is a man of no ac-
count ; but it’s a very back-handed compli-
ment for Hughes.
A strong disposition towards cannibalism
has been evinced of late in Brisbane. Re-
cently a holiday-maker accused a fellow
rioter of having bitten off his ear in a rail-
way carriage, and attempted to devour his
"blanky nose.” Ear produced (Exhibit
A). Two days later a lady charged her
husband with having chewed off one lobe,
with earring attached. Said fragment of
ear also produced, but in so smellful a condi-
tion that the magistrate was compelled to
order immediate application of boracie. But
this husband was a humorous soul: he had
merely attempted to prove, so he said, that
‘‘she was good enough to eat.”
‘‘Kuscobin” on the dairy farmer and co-
operation :
The Government employs experts to grade butter,
and give scientific instruction in modern dairying
methods; but it supplies nobody with a license to
kill the dirty dairyman. The Queensland Health
Act applies to dairies in rural districts; but while
prosecutions lie, and are very properly taken, against
the retailer who waters his milk, has anv convic-
tion ever been recorded against the slovenly farmer
who mixes microbes with it by the million? Some
day some M.P. will call for returns showing how
many prosecutions of this kind have been taken and
how many have been suppressed by political influence
* c Prosecution has been advised by the inspectors
of the Department. If that information is given
truthfully, it ought to make somebody blush. In
Denmark, where the co-operative principle is thor-
oughly applied, the conditions are different. There
are creameries there every five miles, and the most
distant supplier has to carry his milk no more than
half that distance. Here, on the other hand, home
separation is the rule, and the cream is taken in
bi-weekly, or tri-weekly, rarely daily, to the fac-
tories. Under any proper system of regulation the
home separator would 'be thoroughlv cleansed twice
a day. More often now it is only cleansed once
a week. And the factory manager has no redress.
Many of him start out with good intentions; but
the enthusiast soon loses his zeal—it seldom lasts
oyer a twelvemonth—and finds that letting things
slide is the course to ease and affluence. And
things do ■ slide! That there are quite a large
number of co-operative factories grading their
cream is admitted, but the managers of these con-
cerns themselves admit that the standard is too low,
and it is this fact that accounts for the altogether
disproportionate quantity of second-grade butter" com-
market, particularly in summer.
“W.C.M.”: The Queensland Labor
party is good Australian all right.
Even in the disputed matter of im-
migration it declared that it wants
liberalised land laws so as to attract
newcomers; what it objects to is the dump-
ing of men in Brisbane, where admittedly
most of the Denham immigrants remain.
Then, as to other things, at the recent Labor
Conference the following plank was incorpor-
ated in the general platform: “State land
bank, co-operating with Commonwealth Sav-
ings Bank, with a view to extending and
liberalising the principle to persons engaged
in primary and other industries.” What
other Labor party has even suggested
that the State Savings Bank should be
turned down in favor of the Commonwealth
Bank ! I urther, it was unanimously decided
to support the Referenda proposals, and for
some weeks past State Labor members have
been taking the platform in support of the
Commonwealth Government. Nor did any
conference have to coerce the members, as
was found necessary in N.S.W. The State
h rights microbe simply can’t exist in the
Bananaland Labor party.
THE TOWN TRAVELLER.
MILK: “'Edo, Fred. Goiter job?"
BOTTIES: "Yes, blime. I'm town traveller ter a marine store."
The wraith of Mcllwraith still flutters
over Brisbane occasionally. For many years
an intermittent “fund” has been hawked
round, the object being to provide a memorial
of some kind to the deceased Scot. The
committee met again last week, and found
that the fund, with interest, stood at £872
10s., so one of the squatter Collinses put up
£127 10s. to round the figures off to £lOOO.
Next, the committee considered the Father
of the Boom and the manner of man he was,
and decided that the most appropriate
memorial would be an Engineering scholar-
ship in the baby Brisbane Uni.
“Politicus” : So Andy Fisher asserts that
Australia has no time for a third party in
politics. How the Radical of to-day betrays
the cloven hoof of the Tory of yesterday—
and to-morrow ! Some 20 years ago Queens-
land had two great parties—Liberals and
Conservatives —and the Liberal and Tory
journals bellowed from the housetops tha\
the new Labor party was an absurdity, as
Australia had no time for a third party in
politics.” To which Fisher and Co. retorted
valiantly that Queensland had a right to a
dozen parties if the people chose. But note
politicians who entered public life to inaug-
urate a new era say the country has no time
for any more new eras, and therefore bid the
sun stand still to suit their convenience.
According to A. H. Barlow, “the Labor
party is disloyal at heart to the Throne and
the British flag, and wishes to cut the
painter.” Then the journeyman Acting-
Premier went on to say that he was “out
for separation from the Commonwealth
as soon as separation was practic-
able.” If a man wants to separate
from Britain he is a disloyalist. But if he
wants to cut off from the Australian Com-
monwealth, and precipitate a civil war, he
is a loyal citizen of the uttermost respecta-
bility. However, when “separation is prac-
ticable,” Barlow (an octogenarian! will no
longe ; r be practicable : so it doesn't matter.
Rockhampton has an aldermanic aspirant
with a record that gazes over ike shoulders
of most others. At a recent council election
the ratepayers rubbed his name out so per-
sistently that he occupied the tail-end of the
list —a throne he has graced no less than
seven times. Still he threatens “to come
again,” and no doubt will unless somebody
throws a steam roller on him. Nothing
short of a steam roller has a hope of stop-
ping that sort of candidate.

the committee is made to test usefulness of the
rafts and get aboard them and afloat in a nice
rouch sea.
Northward Ho!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Phenomenally Severe Weather in Melbourne and
Sydney. —Newspaper headline.
Now the sun has lost its sting and the
South has had its fling,
So they’re packing up their boxes to
return
To their kindly northern State, ere for sea-
trips ’tis too late,
Where the sunshine still has warmth
enough to burn.
Though the Autumn gale will pass, still the
early morning grass
Bears the footprints of the Winter done
in frost.
So it’s pack your bags and go, for the cry is
Northward Ho!
And there’s little time remaining to be
lost.
By the Manly cove and cliff you hear pack-
ing strangers sniff,
And Sorrento waves good-bye to ev’ry
boat.
When the black Antarctic screams, they see
Brisbane in their dreams,
And amid the river’s water-lilies float.
Up from Hobart town they speed for the
sunshine that they need,
While the vicious gusts pursue across the
Strait.
To escape the coming wrath, all the truants
stumble North
Where the long-deserted sunny home-
steads wait.
Oh, by steamer, train and car, caked with
mud from near and far,
They are coming—they are coming home
again,
Muffled thickly to their throats in new
wraps and overcoats,
And the chilly South may call to them in
vain !
For the Winter they will rest in the State
they love the best,
Yes, and bank the dizzy cheques for hides
or wool,
Till the Southern waters’ lap draws them
swiftly down the map
•Tust to keep hotels and boarding-houses
full.
BANANAbASD,
BARRIE REEF.

Our new Mayoress made her first -mihli»
appearance at the Oriental ball commiti-C
meeting the other day. Unlike most of X
females present, she neither whispered w
grew excited. As a rule, a women’s meeting
is a cross between a secret society murder
ballot and a Carrie Nation axe-hurling cele
bration. Brisbane’s Mayoress seems inclined
to set a better example.
May Lahey, a Queensland girl who is
carving her way to a legal career at th!
Southern California (U.S.A.) College
Law, is keeping up the reputation of Aus-
tralian women. She beat all the male per-
sons at the last exam., and topped the score
with 100 per cent, of marks in one subject.
Two of the members of the firm of T
Borthwick and Sons floated into Brisbane
per Otranto this week. One seems to be a
peer, and the other is an honorable, which
is only l-50th of a peer. The whole peer is
Lord Whitburgh; the l-56th is Algernon
Borthwick.
Another visitor is Methodist minister
Bromilow. The rev. tourist missioned for
some time in Papua, and is an authority on
the unpleasant ways of Australia’s colored
subject thereabouts. The Governor’s hospit-
able roof covered the holy pilgrim during his
stay here. Macgregor and Bromilow were on
more than speaking terms in Papua many
years ago.
It was quite in keeping with the fitness of
things that the lady swimmers’ contingent
from N. S. Wales should have arrived m a
wet week. Your Fanny (it ought to be
Finny) Durack is the star attraction, and
her crawl stroke is the marine rage. Last
night Mrs. Morry Baldwin and the members
of the Excelsior Club gave a theatre party
in the visitors’ honor, and to-night, at the
Cafe Eschenhagen, the festive scone and
sandwich are being mangled at the expense
of the Commercial Club.
The proprietor of a city cafe is searching
madly for a mislaid wedding party. The
usual three-tier indigestibility, fluids and
edibles were ordered for 3.30 p.m., but the
sun had been put to bed for quite a while
when I saw the agitated purveyor of vic-
tuals, and the wedding party had not then
materialised. No explanation has been made
public; but, then, so many things are not
made public, my dear, that one more or less
need not make our curiosity dow’n-hearted.
Railway-Commissioner Evans was about
to leave Brisbane the other day to inspect
the Northern and North-western sections,
when, to his carefully-concealed delight, a
determined female aopeared in his strongly-
entrenched office and coyly pressed into his
hands a subpoena to appear and provide a
legal tribunal with some information to help
her win an action she has brought against
his department. Aloud, he said: “Thank
you, madam; very well.”
Lylia Fegan was the star performer at a
recital in the Pianola Hall on Thursday
night, when old Omar was roused out of his
tent and wound up to some purpose. Tessie
Mulcahy and Leonard Francis tunefully filled
in a too-short gap. Francis is an artist in
every sense of the word, and Tessie Mul-
cahy’s rich, creamy contralto would be hard
to buy at an auction sale.
Little Mrs. Bob Bell, the winner of several
soprano championships, is round at the Em-
pire. singing with Dan Llewellyn. She has
a delightful voice, and in these times of the
midnight dog and the daybreak bottle-oh a
delightful voice.is something to be appreci-
ated.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Bananaland’s Liquor laws are in a beauti-
ful state of uncertainty. The other day
Le Yaux, a Cairns solicitor, was charged
with Sunday drinking in a pub. The magis-
trate quashed the case on the grounds that,
although Le Vaux had a glass of spirits in
his hand, it had not been shown that he had
swallowed any. An analysis of the Sun-
day drinker’s stomach will be a necessary
part of police prosecutions if this sort of
thing goes on.
The Liquor Act, which comes into force
on April 1, throws the old honorary magis-
trates clean overboard, and a fine old bag of
corruption goes with them. The Stipendiary
Magistrate alone will now adjudicate on
licensing matters, and the old spectacle of
the country J.P. (who was often intimately
connected in business with the local Bung)
sitting on the Bench in cases wherein
that same publican was concerned will no
longer be seen. Neither will Spoofkins,
J.P., any longer give 24 hours without the
option to a poor devil who was only half
as drunk as himself the nisrht before.

Nothing like this Steel
Barrow for Goal, Coke, Hot
Ashes and Rough Work!
You can verify this in scores of Australasian Mines, Foundries,
Coal Yards, and in hundreds of Steafirn Plants, where Tubular Steel
Barrows stand the strain of stoking and general rough work.
In ''Gas Works, for removing red hot coke just from the
retorts, the Steel Barrow simply ca?ft be done without.
The trays are pressed from a
solid plate of steel, without
or rivet, and have much greats,
strength, and are more durable,
than riveted iron trays of same
thickness. The flange of tray is
turned over a five-sixteenth steel
rod, which passes entirely -aroufid
tray, giving a smooth finish to the
edge of the bowl, preventing break-
ing, and stiffening and strengthen-
ing it. The wheels revolve on- a
fixed shaft or axle bolt, similar to
a buggy wheel, and run ' .true and
evenly, and the axle shaft serves as
a brace to the handles. The tubu-
lar iron handles are not pierced at
any point by bolt or rivet. All
other tubular barrows have bolt or
rivet holes through handles, and
are thereby materially weakened.
Note how the tubular handles pass
round in front and protect the
wheel when “dumping.” •

Kalgoorlie to Port Augusta Railway.
•teel Hopper Ballast Waggons.
TENDERS will be received until 11 a.m.
on TUESDAY. APRIL 8, 1913, for
the manufacture, supply and delivery of
Ninety (99) Steel Hopper Ballast Waggons
and spare parts.
Forms of Tender, Specification, Condition*
of Contract and Drawings can be seen after
MARCH 17, 1913, at the offices of the Com-
monwealth Railways, 84 William Street,
Melbourne ; Works Director, Customs House,
Sydney; Commonwealth Works Registrars.
Brisbane, Adelaide, Hobart; and Common-
wealth Public Service Inspector, Perth.
The charge for each copy of Specification
will be £1 Is. and ss. a copy for each plan,
which must be paid before the documents
can be handed over.
■Tenders, accompanied by the necessary
deposit, should be endorsed “Tender for Steel
Hopper Ballast Waggons,” and addressed to
The Secretary, Department of Home Affairs.
Melbourne.
(Signed) KING O’MALLEY,
Minister of State for Home Affairs.

Melbourne, 27th February, 1913.
APPOINTMENT OF EDITOR—“COM-
MONWEALTH MILITARY JOURNAL.”
Applications will be received by
the Secretary, Department of Defence,
Victoria Barracks. Melbourne, up to and
inclusive of Ist APRIL, 1913, from persons
desirous of appointment to the position of
“Editor of the Commonwealth Military
Journal.”
Salary—£4oo per annum, including all
allowances except travelling allowances.
Forms of application and particulars of
appointment may be obtained on Application
to the Secretary, Department of Defence,
Melbourne; or to the Military Command-
ant, Military District, Headquarters, Syd-
ney, Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, and Hobart.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE,
Secretary.

COUNTERFEIT COINAGE PLANT.
£lOO Reward.
WHEREAS certain imitations of silver
coins have recently been passed into
circulation at Sydney, in the State of New
bouth Wales : Notice is hereby that a
reward of One hundred pounds will be paid
by the Government of the Commonwealth
for information leading to the apprehension
of the coiners and the discovery of the plant
used in the manufacture of the coins referred
to. The Commonwealth Government will
request the State Government to extend a
full pardon to any accomplice Avho will give
the desired information. This offer will
remain open for thre months from this
date.
ANDREW FISHER,
Treasurer.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense. Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can

PRAISES
DR. SHELDON’S NEW DISCOVERY.
I wish to tell you how much benefit I have received from the use of Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery. In my work my throat often becomes irritated from
strain and theatre dust, and often it would be impossible for me to go through
my part if it were not for the benefit I get from a dose or two of Dr. Sheldon’s
New Discovery. Also, on two occasions I have been so unfortunate as to con-
tract a heavy cold, and on each of these occasions Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery
helped me at once, and soon rid me entirely of the trouble.
I think there is nothing like it, and always have a bottle at home, for dur-
ing the coming winter I look to it as my safeguard against the throat chills con-
tracted so easily when leaving the heated dressing-room, and going into the
chilly night air.
I assure you I will always recommend it to all my friends.
Yours sincerely, (Signed) ELWYN HAR\ EY.

Spring Song of a Bloke.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ri'lirEJ world ’as got me snouted jist a
’ I 1 treat;
Crool Forchin’s dirty left ’as smote
me soul;
An’ all them joys o’ life I ’eld so sweet
Is up the pole.
For, as the poit ses, me ’eart ’as got
The pip wif yearnin’ fer—l dunno wot.
I’m crook ; me name is Mud ; I’ve done me
dash;
Me flamin’ spirit’s got the flamin’ ’ump!
I’m longin’ to let loose on somethin’ rash. . .
Aw, I’m a chump!
I know it; but this blimed ole Springtime
craze
Fair outs me, on these dilly, silly days.
The young, green leaves is shootin’ on the
trees,
The air is like a long, cool swig o’ beer,
The bonder smell o’ flow’rs is on the breeze,
An’, ’ere’s me, ’ere,
.Tist mooehin’ ’round like some poor, barmy
coot.
Of ’ope, an’ joy, an’ forchin’ destichoot.
I've lorst me former joy in gettin’ shiek,
Or ’eadin’ browns; I ’aven’t got the ’eart
To word a tom; an’, square ’n’ all, I’m sick
Of that cheap tart
Oo chucks ’er carkis at a feller’s ’ead
An’ mauls ’im....Ar! I wisht that I wus
dead!
Ther's little breezes stirrin’ in the leaves,
An’ sparrers chimin’ ’igh the ’ole day
long;
An’ on the air a sad, sweet music breaves
A bonzer song—
A mournful sorter choon thet gits a bloke
Fair in the brisket ’ere, an’ makes ’im
choke....
Wot is the matter wif me?. .. .1 dunno.
I got a sorter yearnin’ ’ere inside,
A dead-crook sorter thing that won’t let go
Or be denied —
A feelin’ like I want to do a break,
An’ stoush creation fer some woman’s sake.
The little birds is chirpin’ in the nest,
The parks an’ gardings is a bosker sight
Where smilin’ tarts walks up an’ down, all
dressed
In clobber white.
An’, as their snowy forms goes steppin’ by, •
It seems I’m seekin’ somethin’ on the sly.
Somethin’ or someone —I don’t rightly know;
But, seems to me, I’m kind er lookin’ for
A tart I knoo a ’undred years ago,
Or, maybe, more.
Wot’s this I’ve ’eard them call that thing?...
Geewhiz!
Me ideel bit o’ skirt! That’s wot it is!
My ideel tart!. . . .An’, blime, look at me !
.Tist take a squiz at this, an’ tell me can
Some square an’ lionist tom take this to be
’Er own true man?
Aw, Gawd 1 I’d be as true to ’er, I would —
As straight an’ stiddy as....Ar, wot’s the

Managin’ Women.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE chops were so cold they stuck in
their own frozen fat, and the subdued
sobbing of a woman came from the
sleep-room. The husband sat with his chin
in his palms, while the lodger played with
the burnt end of a cigarette and looked
SU “T.knew you two wuz raisin’ trouble,” he
said. . , ,
“’Ow?” asked the dejected one.

striking a match on his trousers S-
did not light up, however The husband
made no reply, but resumed his dejected at
titude. For a full minute only the sound ot
the sobbing woman w r as heard. ~
“You ironumkers are all the same, said
“Whir do you know about women?”
rapped out the ironmonger.
“I’ve not worked in a boot factory for
nothin’,” replied the lodger.
“You want to be married to know’,” said
the husband.
“P’raps I have bin married,” spoke the
lodger, bitterly, striking another match and
lighting his cigarette.
“I never ’eard of it,” said the one who
was married.
“Nobody ’eard of it. I didn’t mention it.
I wasn’t married long.”
“Who d’ you marry?”
“Little piece worked at the pointers.”
“How d’ you come to marry her?”
“Oh, she got me by the wool, somehow.”
“Couldn’t you agree, Tom?”
Tom again lit his cigarette, which had
gone out.
“Things was a bit slack at the works, en
the whisper w r ent round that a few of us was
due for the bullet. I went ’ome a bit wor-
ried-like. Seemed ’ard on Daphne to marry
a joker just when he was gettin’ chucked
from his job.”
He threw his cigarette end away and relit
another, which he let go out before he spoke
again.
“Don’t want to make a long tale of it.
When I got ’ome she chucked the teapot
over me ’ead.”
“Not because you was goin’ to get the
sack?”
“No. I didn’t tell her me suspicions.
S’pose I wasn’t as cheerful as usual.”
“So you separated?”
Tom nodded and lit up again, and after j
another two puffs he said: “When I’d
cleaned the tea leaves out of me ’air I went 1
out for a walk, and at the corner of Park
an’ Castlereagh I said : ‘Strike me if ever I
go back again.’ En I didn’t.”
“What became of her?”
“Back at the pointers.”
“Seems a bit ’ard on you, Tom.”
“It all comes be way of experience. It’s
let me know a bit about wimmen, my oath!
This is the first time I’ve spoke about it.
Daphne en me made it up to say nothin’
about it, so’s not to spoil her prospects if
she gets another chance. So you needn’t
make a song about it, Dick. I wouldn’t ’ave
mentioned it only you’re so cock-sure that
this is the first barney a joker’s ever ’ad
with his missus. Wimmin takes upder-
standin’. They’ve got yer beat. every time.
“Rose ’as me beat,” said Dick, despond-
ently.
“It’s yer own fault.”
“ ’Ow?”
“Well, I wouldn’t ’ave promised to take
’er to the plumbers’ picnic, and then come
’ome en stalled her off by saying you’re goin’
to see Glebe down the Darlingtons.”
“Why not?”
“Well, yer don’t think she’s howlin be-
cause you chipped her about the chops, do
yer?”
“Well, that’s wot set her goin .
Tom waved his arm oracularly.
“Wimmen don’t always raise a row about
the real thing ‘wot’s at the back of their
heads. They always make the most row
on a side-track. You en me would come
straight, but with wimmen you don’t want
to listen to what they say to know what
they means. For the matter of that, it
wasn’t the disappointment about the picnic
either, though no doubt she’s bought a pair
of open-work stockincs en a new blouse to
go in. Fact is. she thinks you’re givin her
a bit of the push in yer affections.”
Dick suddenly stood up.
“If I thought that I'd go in and square
things.” „ ,
“Go in en make a d —d fool of yerself, yer
mean!”
“Well, what am I to do?
“If yer feels called to go in, go in en give
her one on the sidc« of her lug, en then come
out again.”
“I’m not that sort of bloke, Tom.
“Well, put on your cady en let us go en
see Glebe come out on top. en forget yer
troubles in a bit of barrackin’.’
“I can’t go and leave Rose like that, said
Dick, pointing to the bedroom.
Tom lit up his cigarette and puffed a few
times. _ , ~
“You know Snowy Rogers? he said.
“But yer don’t. He was one of us when I
used to knock about with the old Gipp-
streeters. I useter lodge with him when
he first married. She was a bit of skirt who
follered the tailorin’. I was just thinkin’ of
his first row (something like you en. Rose),
en he was all on for doin himself in same
as you are. but I got him out. en we went
to see a cricket match. Snowy was fair
miserable. All the time he thought his own
little lot would be throwin’ herself into the
’arbor, or cuttin’ her throat with a pam of
scissors. It was all dark when we got ome,
en when Snowv saw it he was afraid to go
in, en I had to drag him. We struck
matches all over the place, en lit no, but
no sign of Liz till we come .to the bedroom,
en there she was, souattin’ in the middle of
the bed, ’uggin’ her knees. ‘Oh, Snowy.’ she
says, ‘I thought something dreadful .’ad ap-
pealed to ver.’ They’ve got on snide ever
since.' What would .’ave ’anpened if Snowy
had tried to make it up?”
“What?”
“Well, one evenin’ Snowy would aye come
’ome and found a bloke with oily ’air en in
his Sunday duds, named Jones, avin tea
with them," en after tea Liz would say, Mr.
.Tones has kindlv promised to take me to a
dance to-night. Snowy. If you’re ome first
don’t wait up. I’ll let meself .in.
“T’d break his jaw.” said Dick.
“O’ course you would. But take, my ad-
vice en there won’t be any oily- air jokers m
the case. Come along,” he said. Lets go
to the football match.”
Dick let himself be led outside, but sud-
denly shook himself free. “It’s no use, he
said! “I can’t leave Rose breaking her
heart like that.” . ~ , ,
He went inside and straight to the bed-
room. Tom sat down and smoked five cigar-
ettes. Then the bedroom door opened and
Rose and Dick came out.
“Tom ” said Rose, “do you mind eating
the cold chops, or would you prefer just

the Plumbers’ Picnic at Clifton
