A Grimy Industry,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Sydney man, wkilat walking; along a lonely road,
was recently accosted by two ramshackle men, and
implored to buy a £ 1-note for 18s. or 16s. He
noticed that the two capitalists had a bag stuffed
full of paper money, and refused. The police think
it has been manufactured somewhere in the lonely
Middle Harbor hills.
FROM the back window I look o’er the
hills—
The lonely hills that shimmer in the
sun:
They hold a manufactory of bills:
In some dark cave the villainy is done.
I picture dossers with a printing press
That clanks and shudders o’er each bogus
note:
They take it tenderly like priests that bless.
And o’er its new perfections softly gloat.
But when the printing’s done, the work be-
gins
Of making it like all the filthy rags
That fill the honest banker’s wooden bins.
And draw red gold from out his money-
bags.
They clean their boots with notes. Their
abject dog
With eager teeth to edges then attends :
Then afterwards they’re left upon a log
To fade and soak in rain the good Lord
sends.
A week or so of that, and then they’re
brought
To be anointed with old mutton fat.
(The banker, if he gives the thing a thought,
Says honest human sweat accounts for
that. )
Lastly, a day or so beneath a weight
(lives them the pocket creases that they
bear;
Then go two dossers and a dog in state
From the dull world some hard-earned
cash to tear.
Last week, our old and honest Mary Ann
Rose from her scrubbing with a floor-
cloth rank,
And, on a message to the teller man,
Invaded the adjacent Savings Bank.
She laid her floorcloth on the counter wide;
Ere she could speak, he grabbed it up.
“Yes, Miss !”
(The inky thing he indolently eyed.)
“Will you take gold or silver now for
this?"
N.S.W.
KODAK.
A Discolored Melodrama.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T TE entente cor diale between Society
and Henry Thompson was ended.
Thompson had been obliterated.
A wise man would have shaken hands
with himself. The drop of discolored blood
in Thompson impelled him to melodrama.
It is hard to feel like Julius Knight when
you’re “scutching” at a flaxmill for a living.
And the scenery was altogether wrong.
Though Thompson cursed the picture post-
cards which had lured him to the kainga
and the simple life he wouldn’t quit. Ro-
mance died, but Thompson thrived. In
time he acquired a taste for dried shark and
mussels. As the crack euchre-player of the
kainga his status was second only to that of
Mita Pouro, the, decrepit and mendacious
chief. His beauty gained him the not-
always-desirable admiration of the Maori
ladies. When Romance seemed perma-
nently dead these things counted. Thompson
had become a Pakeha-Maori.
Occasionally Thompson’s aunts wrote em-
Bjoring him to return to grace and the
*-M.G * 1 Then .Thompson would curse the
Y.M.C.A., likewise grace, and go over to
Taare s whare, where bad whisky was re-
tailed at an astounding profit to Big
Charley. But most of all would he curse
the boss of the A.S.C.
The Amalgamated Shipping Company
came into existence on the Boss’s 21st birth-
day, and with him _it grew to maturity.
From its inception it was a slave-driving
concern. Amongst the generals of Private
Enterprise it was tacitly agreed that the
A.S.C. owned the waters which surround
the three islands. When obtuse persons, be-
lieving that competition was a healthful
stimulant, started in opposition they were
suppressed. It was either a case of elimina-
tion or assimilation—and assimilation led
by a short head. Thus the A.S.C. grew.
The Boss’s repertoire was extensive. In
the public eye he appeared as a benign poli-
tician whose kindly desire it was to deflect
the misguided lumper from such sordid aims
as a living wage. With the Best People he
was a genial soul. To the underlings at the
office he was a coarse-minded martinet with
a low faculty for the recognition of incom-
petence. As a sideline the Boss was a
family man. _ Numerically the family was
small, but aristically she was flawless. Her
name was Rosamond. Until Henry Thomp-
son propelled himself within her vision
Rosamond’s mental activities were restricted
to clothes, confectionery and her own
beauty. Thompson made good on sight. To
Rosamond’s excited fancy he seemed to be a
cross between the Bully Hayes of fiction
and the Apollo Belvedere of old Rome. She
thought he might be an earl, faintly dis-
guised. Between their first dance and 2 a.m.
Rosamond capitulated. It was during the
latter function that the Boss found them.
He was sustained by ten whiskies and the
recently acquired knowledge that a promis-
ing _ liaison had gone wrong. Next day
Society had lost an ornament and the A.S.C.
its assistant freight clerk.
At the back of Te Haupiri was a long
line of man-eating savages. They had done
credit to their diet in the sanguinary tribal
wars and Te Haupiri’s mana was that of a
very great chief. From Gisborne to East
Cape he was the boss dago. The mission-
aries, recognising his prospective value, had
captured him when young. In England he
acquired knowledge and some plain vices.
On the European continent he gained more
knowledge and many fancy vices. Then he
returned to the hapu and chased the mis-
sionaries away with curses in three lan-
guages.
It was ,no slouch’s job to vermilionise
the coast in the pristine days of the Bays
district, when methylated spirit was con-
sidered a lifueur and kerosene tested a man’s
drinking capability. When the joy god
entered into Te Haupiri he collected his
forces and swept down the coast leaving in
his wake a bottle-oh’s harvest and the rem-
nants of disrupted law’n’order. Those who
were side-tracked during the jaunt were
promptly struck off the pay-roll. Thus Dar-
win was vindicated. The Bays took off its
hat to Te Haupiri.
At Arahura Bay Te Haupiri had built a
castle. It was the product of a brainy man
and a ton of gold. Nature had supplied the
necessary setting with a lavish hand.
Naturally, Te Haupiri paid heavy tribute
to the A.S.C. The freight rates to the Bays
were framed on a sumptuous scale. Be-
tween refreshments Te Haupiri brooded on
this serious loss of beer money.
One day the snub-nosed steamer swung
into Arahura Bay and landed on the beach
a young man and a battered portmanteau.
The stranger climbed cautiously to the
grassed slope and headed for Te Haupiri’s
bush-embowered castle. That night in the
great dining-hall which boasted neither
table nor other appurtenance of pakeha
dwellings he saw Te Haupiri’s girl-sister,
Arini, whose beauty was like that of no
brown girl whom he had seen. Then Ro-
mance lived again in the heart of Henry
Thompson.
Next day Thompson, who. was now To-
moana, talked with Te Haupiri. Theii
korero was of business, but Thompson’*
thoughts were ever with the beauteous Arini
Since hastiness is not an attribute of Maori
character the korero extended over many
days, and Tomoana, who had come with one
motive, found that he had two. When Arini
smiled upon him he almost forgave the Boss
of the A.S.C.
Some time later the little steamer picked
up its former passenger and carried him
northward. As Thompson watched the re-
ceding bay he thought complacently of his
pact with Te Haupiri. That he would suc-
ceed he felt confident. And success meant
the winning of a prize on which he had set
his heart. The prize was Arini.
There was consternation at the head
office of the A.S.C. when the Gisborne mana-
ger’s report came to hand. Rumor had pre-
ceded the report. It appeared that Te Hau-
piri’s chartered steamer had annexed the
trade of the Bays to the detriment of the
purse and prestige of the A.S.C. In the
past the co. had contemned the supplication
of the white settlers for a better service.
The worm had revolved.
Thompson toiled strenuously with a light
heart. Each week-end was made glorious
by a letter from Arini. In his leisure
moments he prepared the nest. Arini had
expressed a wish to live near the city where
she had been educated, and her lover had
found an ideally-situated cottage overlook-
ing the waters of the harbor. For a space
Henry Thompson was the happiest man in
Auckland.
Came a letter, blotched by haste and
marked with fear. It told a tale of Maori
treachery. Unheralded the Boss had arrivec
from Wellington. Though ostensibly on a
shooting expedition, he had spent long hours
in conference with Te Haupiri. For the
delectation of the great pakeha there had
been hakas and poi dances; and, since the
Maori eats when he is glad or woeful, there
had been much feasting. Te Haupiri had
drunk deeply of the waipiro. Then he had
spoken to his assembled people. He told
them that an enemy of his great-grand-
mother, Titoki, had sent an evil spirit called
Henry Thompson to lead him astray. But
the pakeha ran-gitira, returning good for
evil, had hastened to his assistance, and the
taipo had been cast out. Now he, Te Hau-
piri, would make reparation. The people,
being filled with food, had assented joyously.
There was worse to tell. Te Haupiri looked
with approval upon the nakeha’s admiration
for Arini. Arini was much afraid.
Te Haupiri’s steamer was at the wharf.
In the teeth of a savage gale Henry Thomp-
son, manager .of the new company, drove
her south. When her crew protested he
cursed them. When they talked of mutiny
he backed his curses with a badly-handled
fire shovel.
The sun, just risen, contended with a
heavy bank of clouds as the steamer dropped
her anchor in Arahura Bay. Thompson
stood at the rail and looked shoreward,
whence came a sound that filled him with
uneasiness. It was the tangi wail.
He was the first into the surf-boat, and
the first on to the beach. Here he was re-
ceived by a solemn-faced deputation. With
many verbal embellishments and appro-
priate gestures they recounted the tragedy
of the day before. The pakeha rangitira
—otherwise the Boss of the A.S.C.—had
gone, unaccompanied, to shoot wild pigeons.
When he did not return search had been
made, and his body found. A double-bar-
relled gun makes a mess of a man’s features.
Thompson heard them in silence. Then he
went to have a reckoning with Te Haupiri.
Te Haupiri received his partner effusively.
Thompson accepted the greetings without
prejudice. The potentate, having set out
the glasses and waved his retainers away,
began to talk. His explanation was entirely
satisfactory. When its accustomed quiet
had settled down on Arahura Bay Thomp-
son again went northward. He was escorted
to the beach by Te Haupiri and the peerless
Arini. Thompson had made good. The
wedding had been fixed for a month later
Thompson neglected to ask who had fired
the gun that ended the Boss’s career.
He received indirect information that
Rosamond’s affections might still be his for
the asking, and that he might thus be him-
self Boss of the A.S.C., but he heeded not.
Incidentally, he consulted a doctor about
some obscure symptoms. The doctor seemed
deeply interested, and diplomaticallv drew
from him much information about the life
he had led—about the Maori ladies, and the
flax mill, and the kainga , and the dried
shark and, the joyous life in the castle of
±e llaupin. He was a non-committal doc-
tor, but he must have said something in
haste to some one in authority, for thimrs
began to happen in haste. 8
Henry Thompson fought the men who
tried to arrest him, and put up a magnifi-
cent scrap on general principles. When he
learned that his destination was the leper
station he wished that he had made the
scrap even more heroic than it was
